xiv<br>
<b>Preface</b><br>
This textbook, Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools, is written specifically<br>
for Form Five students in the United Republic of Tanzania. It is written in accordance<br>
with the 2010 Accountancy Syllabus for Advanced Secondary Education Form V -<br>
VI, issued by the Ministry of Education, Science and Technology.<br>
The book consists of ten chapters, namely, nature and context of accountancy,<br>
books of prime entry, corrections of accounting errors, accounting for reserves and<br>
provisions, depreciation and disposal of non–current assets, recognition of revenue<br>
and expenses, preparation of financial statements, financial statements analysis and<br>
interpretations, investment accounts, and accounting for branches. Each chapter<br>
contains text, examples and illustrations, activities, exercises and end of chapter<br>
revision exercises. You are encouraged to do all the activities and exercises as well<br>
as other assignments provided by your teacher. Doing so will enable you to develop<br>
the intended competencies.<br>
<b>Tanzania Institute of Education</b><br>

<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
1<br>
<b>The nature</b><br>
and context of<br>
accountancy<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>One</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter, you will learn about the concept of accountancy, its origin, branches<br>
and purpose. You will also learn the nature of accounting information, its users,<br>
fundamental principles of accounting and accounting system. The competencies<br>
developed in this chapter will enable you to describe well accountancy as a<br>
field of study, nature of accounting information, the characteristics that makes<br>
it useful and its users as well as fundamental principles of accounting used for<br>
processing and generating accounting information.<br>
<b>Accountancy as a concept</b><br>
<b>At one point of time, we are interacting</b><br>
with traders in retail shops, grocery<br>
stores, pharmacy and mobile money<br>
agents. You might have seen those traders<br>
maintaining some financial records of<br>
their transactions. Furthermore, you<br>
might have seen in some newspapers,<br>
financial reports of business entities like<br>
commercial banks showing how they<br>
have perfomed in a particular accounting<br>
period. Have you ever wondered why<br>
these practices are taking place? To<br>
whom are the published financial reports<br>
targeted? How do they make use of<br>
reported information and for which<br>
purpose or objectives? The answers<br>
to these and other related questions<br>
define what accountancy is all about.<br>
<b>Accountancy is defined by its relevance,</b><br>
that is, what it can offer for those<br>
interested to learn about it and what it<br>
does in terms of supporting business<br>
management and decision making.<br>
<b>As a concept, accountancy is the</b><br>
systematic field of knowledge pertaining<br>
to accounting, including the rules and<br>
principles that govern actual accounting<br>
procedures. Thus, accounting is a<br>
subset of accountancy that involves the<br>
practical application of accountancy<br>
principles to execute the profession’s<br>
core duties. In other words, accounting<br>
tries to explain the nature of the work<br>
of the accountants (professionals) and<br>
accountancy refers to the profession<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
2<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
these people adopt. According to<br>
<b>American Institute of Certified Public</b><br>
<b>Accountants (AICPA), accounting is</b><br>
the art of recording, classifying, and<br>
summarizing in a significant manner<br>
and in terms of money, transactions and<br>
events which are, in part at least, of a<br>
financial character, and interpreting the<br>
results thereof. Four important activities<br>
are captured within this definition<br>
including the following:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Recording of transactions and</b><br>
events in books of original entry.<br>
(ii)	 Classifying transactions through<br>
the process of posting from<br>
the books of original entry to<br>
different accounts in the ledger.<br>
(iii)	 Summarizing<br>
the<br>
results<br>
periodically in financial reports.<br>
(iv)	 Interpreting the results of the<br>
business operations through the<br>
analysis of financial statements.<br>
<b>Differences between accounting and</b><br>
bookkeeping<br>
<b>Better understanding of accounting</b><br>
can also be attained by comparing<br>
with bookkeeping learnt at lower-<br>
level secondary education. Accounting<br>
is much broader; it includes all<br>
the basic elements of bookkeeping<br>
as well as advanced concepts and<br>
practices. Besides the preparation of<br>
financial statements, accountants are<br>
also involved in financial statements<br>
analysis<br>
and<br>
their<br>
interpretation.<br>
<b>Individual with relevant accounting</b><br>
skills and knowledge, and can undertake<br>
accounting jobs professionally is known<br>
as accountant. Besides the recording of<br>
business transactions – the usual activity<br>
of bookkeeper – they are also engaged<br>
in summarizing and further processing<br>
of financial data to provide meaningful<br>
financial information. Due to their high<br>
level of expertise, accountants are also<br>
responsible for designing accounting<br>
systems of different organizations. In<br>
order to carry out these activities in an<br>
effective manner, accountants must be<br>
highly trained than book keepers.<br>
<b>Historical development of accounting</b><br>
<b>Accounting is perhaps one of the oldest</b><br>
and structured discipline, which has<br>
evolved in response to the changing<br>
social and economic development of the<br>
society. It is known to have existed in<br>
one form or another since at least 3,500<br>
<b>BC. Considerable evidence shows that,</b><br>
accounting was being practised in ancient<br>
times in several places e.g., Egypt,<br>
<b>China, Greece, and Rome. One of the</b><br>
oldest textbooks relating to accounting is<br>
the one written by an Italian Franciscan<br>
monk and mathematics Professor, Luca<br>
<b>Pacioli in year 1494. His book titled,</b><br>
<b>Summa de Arithmetica, Geometria,</b><br>
<b>Proportione et Proportionalita, translated</b><br>
as, summary of arithmetic, geometry,<br>
and algebra is seen by many as providing<br>
comprehensive elaborations on the use<br>
of double entry bookkeeping in ancient<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
3<br>
times. The book provides a detailed<br>
description on the rules of debits and<br>
credits in both, journals and ledgers that<br>
is still used today as basis of accounting.<br>
<b>Due to this, he is referred by many as the</b><br>
father of accounting.<br>
<b>The popularity of accounting increased</b><br>
due to its information usefulness to<br>
assist in decision making and control of<br>
businesses. People needed accounting<br>
to record business transactions, know if<br>
they were being ﬁnancially successful,<br>
and know how much they owned and<br>
how much they owed. For example,<br>
during the period of pre-industrial<br>
revolution, accounting was extensively<br>
used by feudal societies that had wealthy<br>
landlords who owned vast properties and<br>
wealth. These properties were scattered<br>
at many distant places thus making it<br>
difficult to maintain and control activities<br>
that were taking place in those properties.<br>
<b>In this kind of situation, management</b><br>
were expected to prepare financial<br>
reports for the landlords to know what is<br>
happening with their businesses as well<br>
as how they were performing. By using<br>
these reports and physical evidence e.g.,<br>
increased properties and resources like<br>
cash, management could be rewarded,<br>
demoted or replaced based on their<br>
performance.<br>
<b>During the industrial revolution and the</b><br>
development of Multinational  Companies<br>
(MNCs), the world has experienced<br>
the emergence of large corporations,<br>
extending their activities beyond the<br>
countries of their incorporation. This<br>
requires the use of advanced accounting<br>
systems to provide highly sophisticated<br>
accounting information reports between<br>
individuals located in far distantly places.<br>
<b>The role of accounting to act as medium</b><br>
of communication between management<br>
and external stakeholders has thus<br>
remained the same. Owners and creditors<br>
who provide necessary resources for the<br>
development of businesses consider<br>
accounting as important for understanding<br>
the performance and financial position<br>
of the businesses. This is important in<br>
supporting the achievement of different<br>
business decisions and control.<br>
<b>Exercise 1.1</b><br>
1.<br>
<b>What do you understand by the</b><br>
term accounting?<br>
2.<br>
<b>In what ways does accounting</b><br>
differ from bookkeeping?<br>
<b>There are several branches of accounting,</b><br>
which have emerged due to changes in<br>
economic and business development. In<br>
other words, the demands of accounting<br>
information evolved to suit the needs<br>
and objectives of different classes of<br>
persons. Conventionally, there are three<br>
branches of accounting as presented in<br>
figure 1.1.<br>
<b>Branches of accounting and their</b><br>
general objectives<br>

<b>Branches of</b><br>

<b>Management</b><br>
<b>Accounting</b><br>
<b>Figure 1. 1: Conventional classification of accounting</b><br>
<b>As observed in Figure 1.1, conventionally,</b><br>
the three branches of accounting include<br>
financial accounting, cost accounting<br>
and management accounting. Financial<br>
accounting is concerned with recording<br>
of financial transactions and eventually<br>
reporting of financial statements that<br>
show the performance (profit or loss)<br>
over a span of time/period and financial<br>
position of the business (assets, liabilities<br>
and capital) as on a particular date. This<br>
is usually done at the end of accounting<br>
period. One of the distinctive features of<br>
financial accounting is that, accounting<br>
records and financial reporting are closely<br>
guided by well-established accounting<br>
standards, known as the International<br>
<b>Financial Reporting Standards (IFRS),</b><br>
which were adopted in Tanzania by July,<br>
2004. The information produced under<br>
financial accounting, primarily aim at<br>
meeting the information needs of external<br>
stakeholders of a business.<br>
<b>As for cost accounting, its early</b><br>
development was shaped more by its<br>
application in manufacturing firms.<br>
<b>Primarily, it was used to record and</b><br>
analyse manufacturing costs with the<br>
major objective of determining the cost<br>
of products. Nowadays cost accounting<br>
is widely used in different organizational<br>
settings including, service providing<br>
firms. Contrary to financial accounting,<br>
cost accounting is not strictly guided by<br>
accounting standards (e.g., IFRS) but<br>
a variety of cost accounting principles.<br>
<b>As for users, it is mainly used by</b><br>
management of the organisation to<br>
achieve the objectives of control and<br>
reduction of costs in the production of<br>
goods and services. In other words, cost<br>
accounting information is generally<br>
meant for management consumption i.e.,<br>
internal use of accounting information.<br>
<b>Comparably, Management accounting is</b><br>
closely related to cost accounting as it<br>
utilises all the principles and techniques<br>
of cost accounting in data processing.<br>
<b>However, it is relatively much broader</b><br>
in terms of nature of data to be processed<br>
and usage. Besides financial information<br>
it also takes into account different<br>
types of qualitative factors e.g., issues<br>
from areas of marketing and human<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
5<br>
resources. Nevertheless, as observed<br>
in<br>
cost<br>
accounting,<br>
management<br>
accounting also is not strictly guided<br>
by accounting standards. Management<br>
accounting reports such as budgets and<br>
other types of operational reports, they<br>
are normally prepared at the discretion of<br>
the management using different methods<br>
and formats provided they yield desired<br>
quality information. This is different<br>
from financial accounting; the contents<br>
and format of financial reports are given/<br>
specified in different types of accounting<br>
standards.<br>
<b>As of recent, other branches/types of</b><br>
accounting have emerged besides the<br>
three identified ones. Examples on<br>
this include, tax accounting, forensic<br>
accounting and social responsibility<br>
accounting. For instance, Tax accounting<br>
is concerned with tax related matters<br>
including, the determination of tax<br>
liability and preparation of tax returns<br>
for individuals, companies and other<br>
entities. Tax accounting is governed by<br>
tax laws and regulations applicable in a<br>
particular country. For example, in the<br>
context of Tanzania, it is the Income<br>
<b>Tax Act of 2004 that provide the legal</b><br>
framework for persons to fulfil their tax<br>
obligations. Professionals in this area<br>
namely, Tax accountants usually provide<br>
tax related services to individuals and<br>
business community. This includes,<br>
the use of legal ways to minimize<br>
tax liabilities for their clients. As for<br>
forensic accounting, this deals with the<br>
application of accounting, auditing and<br>
investigative skills in analysing financial<br>
information, which can appropriately<br>
be used as evidence in the Court of<br>
law. Experts in this area, are usually<br>
engaged in investigating financial fraud<br>
and embezzlement of funds. In contrast,<br>
social<br>
responsibility<br>
accounting<br>
is<br>
the process of communicating the<br>
social and environmental effects of<br>
an organization’s economic actions to<br>
particular interest groups within society<br>
and to society at large. In other words,<br>
social responsibility accounting focuses<br>
on financial reporting on what the<br>
business is giving back to the society in<br>
return for the benefits it enjoys from it.<br>
<b>Arguably, the business economic actions</b><br>
have direct or indirect impact to different<br>
stakeholders<br>
such<br>
as<br>
employees,<br>
customers, the community and the<br>
environment in general. Due to this,<br>
the role that business plays besides its<br>
ordinary business undertakings must be<br>
of great interest to different stakeholders<br>
in the society.<br>
<b>To</b><br>
regulate<br>
something<br>
means<br>
to<br>
exercise control over it by way of<br>
directing or governing according to<br>
a given set of rules. The regulation<br>
of the accountancy profession covers<br>
several issues. These include, entry<br>
and licensing requirements (including<br>
education<br>
requirements;<br>
monitoring<br>
of the behaviour and performance<br>
of<br>
professional<br>
accountants;<br>
the<br>
accounting standards (including ethical<br>
<b>Regulatory</b><br>
framework<br>
governing<br>
financial accounting practices<br>

<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
standards) that professional accountants<br>
must meet, disciplinary systems and<br>
procedures for those who fail to meet<br>
the requirements. For implementation<br>
and enforcement, there are different<br>
sources of rules and regulations and<br>
professional bodies, which oversee the<br>
practice and development of accounting.<br>
<b>For example, sources of rules and</b><br>
regulations include the use of company<br>
laws and accounting standards. Different<br>
countries have their own company laws,<br>
which provide different conditions<br>
and rules for the establishment and<br>
operation of the companies. In the<br>
context of Tanzania, Companies Act<br>
2002 requires that, every company<br>
registered in Tanzania to prepare a set<br>
of financial statements including, the<br>
statement of financial position (the<br>
balance sheet), statement of income<br>
(profit and loss account) and cash flow<br>
statement. Therefore, companies and<br>
their accountants are expected to comply<br>
well with the requirements specified in<br>
the regulations; otherwise, they may<br>
be penalised or punished. Examples on<br>
this include, fees or penalty for the late<br>
filing and deregistration for serious and<br>
extended defaults.<br>
<b>As for accounting standards, they</b><br>
provide different types of accounting<br>
rules to guide the preparation of<br>
financial statements. They provide the<br>
basis for recognition, measurement,<br>
presentation and disclosure of different<br>
transactions and events reported in<br>
the financial statements. For example,<br>
companies are required to report their<br>
performance (profit/loss) and financial<br>
position by using the same accounting<br>
rules (consistency) over time. This is<br>
considered as important in minimizing<br>
fraudulent manipulations and enhancing<br>
uniformity in financial reporting. It also<br>
simplifies the evaluation and comparison<br>
of performance and financial position of<br>
business entities operating in the same<br>
industry.<br>
<b>A country can opt to have its own</b><br>
locally established standards or adopt<br>
the international accounting standards.<br>
<b>For the latter, the global International</b><br>
<b>Accounting Standards Board (IASB) is</b><br>
the one responsible for the development<br>
and<br>
establishment<br>
of<br>
accounting<br>
standards<br>
known<br>
as<br>
<b>International</b><br>
<b>Financial</b><br>
<b>Reporting</b><br>
<b>Standards</b><br>
(IFRS). Regionally, some of the East<br>
<b>African countries have adopted IFRS</b><br>
at different times. Each country has<br>
its own professional body to oversee<br>
the implementation and enforcement<br>
of accounting standards and other<br>
regulations. For example, the Institute of<br>
<b>Certified Public Accountants of Kenya</b><br>
(ICPAK), the Institute of Certified Public<br>
<b>Accountants of Uganda (ICPAU) and the</b><br>
<b>Institute of Certified Public Accountants</b><br>
of Rwanda (ICPAR). In Tanzania, we<br>
have the National Board of Accountants<br>
and Auditors (NBAA).<br>
<b>At the moment, NBAA is responsible for</b><br>
a number of tasks including providing<br>
quality<br>
assurance<br>
review<br>
system,<br>

investigating accounting malpractices<br>

and preparation of financial statements.<br>
<b>Their clients, in many cases, are the</b><br>

<b>To qualify as professional accountant,</b><br>
one is required to attend several<br>

involved in authorization and approval<br>
of professional accountants to include,<br>
maintaining and publishing register of<br>
authorized and approved professional<br>
accountants in the country.<br>

<b>Accounting information and its users</b><br>
<b>Nature of accounting information</b><br>
<b>Accounting information as presented</b><br>
in different types of financial report<br>
provides the means through which<br>
different users of accounting information<br>
can understand the different activities<br>
taking place within the business. It does<br>
so in a unique way by reflecting the<br>
monetary values of different activities<br>
undertaken by the business. When<br>
recording in books of account, business<br>
activities are commonly referred to<br>
as business transactions i.e., business<br>
events that have monetary impact on<br>
an entity’s financial statements and<br>

<b>Examples of business activities or</b><br>
transactions include, paying a supplier<br>
for services rendered or goods delivered,<br>
buying office equipment by cash, and<br>
selling goods on credit.<br>
<b>Types of business activities reflected</b><br>
in accounting information<br>
<b>Categorically, there are three major</b><br>
types of business activities reflected in<br>
accounting information. They include,<br>
operating activities, financing activities<br>
and investing activities. Operating<br>
activities are the main activities that a<br>
business does to bring its products and<br>
services to the market on an ongoing<br>
basis. They depend on the nature<br>
of the business. For example, for<br>
manufacturing company these include<br>
manufacturing, selling and marketing of<br>
the products. Non-operating activities<br>
will not occur so frequently – they<br>
include one-time events that may affect<br>
revenues, expenses or cash flow but<br>
fall outside the company’s routine and<br>
core business activities. In contrast,<br>
<b>Financing activities affect the long–</b><br>
term liabilities and equity/capital of the<br>
owners as a result of the flow of cash<br>
(inward and outward between a business<br>
and its owners on the one hand, and with<br>
creditors on the other hand). Examples<br>
on these include, selling of shares,<br>
adding loans, payment of dividends and<br>
interest. In contrast, investing activities<br>
include, purchases of long-term assets<br>
such as property, plant, and equipment.<br>
<b>They also include acquisitions of other</b><br>
businesses and investments in financial<br>
securities such as stocks and bonds<br>
within a specified period.<br>
<b>Accounting information can also be</b><br>
understood well by considering its key<br>
attributes or characteristics that makes it<br>
useful in decision making, as described<br>
in the following section.<br>
<b>Qualitative characteristics of useful</b><br>
accounting information<br>
<b>Qualitative characteristics of useful</b><br>
accounting<br>
information<br>
are<br>
well<br>
described in the conceptual framework<br>
of financial reporting, as revised in<br>
2018. This provides important theory<br>
with details about the basic reasoning<br>
underlying the preparation of financial<br>
statements and reporting. Qualitative<br>
characteristics can be divided into two<br>
levels, the fundamental and enhancing<br>
qualitative characteristics. Fundamental<br>
qualitative characteristics are the basic<br>
or primary characteristics while the<br>
enhancing attributes are the additional<br>
features that add more value or they<br>
enhance more the decision usefulness of<br>
financial information.<br>
<b>Further details regarding the major</b><br>
categories<br>
and<br>
specific<br>
types<br>
of<br>
qualitative characteristics of useful<br>
accounting information are given using<br>
Table 1.1.<br>

<b>As indicated in table1.1, to be relevant,</b><br>
accounting information should be able<br>
to make impact or difference in decision<br>
making. Decision making is all about<br>
making selection between or among the<br>
given alternatives, which may include<br>
investments decisions. For example, one<br>
may have to decide whether to invest in<br>
project A or project B by considering the<br>
potential of each project in generating<br>
income. The relevance of accounting<br>
information will be evaluated by<br>
considering its ability to show the<br>
attractiveness of each project under<br>
consideration such as the differences<br>
in revenues to be generated and cost<br>
savings.<br>
<b>As for faithful representation, major</b><br>
focus is on three attributes as shown in<br>
table 1.1, completeness, neutrality and<br>
free from material errors. Completeness<br>
underlines the basic idea that, accounting<br>

<b>Relevance</b><br>
<b>Faithful representation</b><br>
 Information is relevant if it is<br>
capable of making a difference to<br>
the economic decisions made by<br>
users.<br>
	 Information<br>
must<br>
faithfully<br>
represent the substance of what it<br>

	 Financial information is capable of<br>
making a difference in economic<br>

or confirmatory value<br>
	 A faithful representation means<br>
information should be to the<br>
maximum extent possible, complete,<br>
neutral and free from material error.<br>
	 A faithful representation is affected<br>
by level of measurement uncertainty.<br>

information should be considered as<br>
faithfully represented if all pertinent<br>
information has been presented. When<br>
this is done as expected, then it will be<br>
possible for one to make a complete<br>
and accurate assessment of business<br>
performance and its financial position.<br>
<b>On the other hand, any omissions</b><br>
of relevant information, would lead<br>
to a false or misleading depiction of<br>
accounting activities and phenomena<br>
thus providing incorrect picture of<br>
business performance as well as its<br>
financial position. As a result, this will<br>
render information useless to whomever<br>
use/need<br>
it<br>
to<br>
support<br>
business<br>
management or any kind of decisions.<br>
<b>As for Neutrality, this underscore the</b><br>
basic idea that, accounting information<br>
should be neutral, or free from any bias.<br>
<b>To achieve this, it is strongly forbidden to</b><br>
alter or present accounting information<br>
in any way that is meant to influence a<br>
decision to be made with a predetermined<br>
result in mind. The logic and justification<br>
for this; it will do good for society and<br>
accounting profession when the overall<br>
societal goals and accounting guidelines<br>
are adhered to, rather than the desires<br>
of any one person or group of people,<br>
with their own agendas. On the other<br>
hand, free from material error stresses<br>
the need or desire to keep accounting<br>
information free from error. This can be<br>
achieved by ensuring that, there are no<br>
errors and inaccuracies in the description<br>
of the phenomenon and no errors are<br>
made during the different processes of<br>
preparing financial statements. Notably,<br>
due to human nature, mistakes do<br>
occur – it is something that cannot be<br>
completely eradicated. As professional,<br>
it is advised to be careful and making<br>
sure that, one abides by the rules of<br>
conduct as well as checking or examining<br>
the data and figures used in process of<br>
preparing financial statements. This will<br>
ensure that, accounting information is<br>
accurately portrayed and reported.<br>
<b>It is also important to understand</b><br>
the key differences among the four<br>
enhancing qualitative characteristics.<br>
<b>For instance, comparability is the</b><br>
quality of accounting information to<br>
enable its users to identify similarities<br>
and differences between two sets of<br>
business phenomena. It is important<br>
that, similar situations should be<br>
presented in the same manner while<br>
different situations should be presented<br>
differently. Moreover, there is a need to<br>
observe consistency, which includes, the<br>
use of the same accounting policies and<br>
procedures from one accounting period<br>
to another – within the businesses and the<br>
industry. This will facilitate the ability<br>
of users of accounting information to<br>
compare the financial statements of the<br>
business over time as well as with other<br>
similar businesses within the industry.<br>
<b>Verifiability, underscores the basic idea</b><br>
that, for accounting information to be<br>
considered as true or correct, it should<br>
be possible for one to prove/confirm<br>
whether it is true/correct. For example,<br>

during the audit assignment, auditor may<br>
cross check with the bank to see whether<br>
the bank account balance of the business<br>
being audited is the same as that shown<br>
in its balance sheet (i.e., statement of<br>
financial position). To enhance the value<br>
of verifiability, confirmation may be<br>
sought from different knowledgeable<br>
and independent parties to see whether<br>
they reach the same consensus. However,<br>
it is not necessary to have complete<br>
agreement that a particular depiction is<br>
a faithful representation. Verifiability<br>
helps users of accounting information<br>
to<br>
be<br>
assured<br>
that,<br>
information<br>
presented faithfully reflects the business<br>
transactions or events being examined.<br>
<b>In contrast, timeliness captures the</b><br>
general idea that, the sooner information<br>
is available, the more useful it is. Delays<br>
in financial reporting more than expected<br>
may hinder timely access to information<br>
by decision makers, and when available<br>
it may no longer be useful to solve their<br>
decision problem.<br>
<b>As for understandability, this underline</b><br>
the basic idea that, information should<br>
be presented in a way that is readily<br>
understandable by users who have<br>
reasonable knowledge of a business and<br>
its economic activities. Understandability<br>
of accounting information is more likely<br>
to be enhanced when information is<br>
classified, characterized and presented<br>
clearly and concisely. The use of tabular<br>
or graphic formats may improve the<br>
understanding of presented accounting<br>
information. Moreover, clarifying some<br>
issues e.g., the ways in which certain<br>
financial figures have been derived and<br>
relationships between them may also<br>
enhance the clarity and understanding of<br>
key issues presented in financial reports.<br>
<b>Additionally, it is also important to</b><br>
note that, ability of users to understand<br>
accounting information as presented<br>
in financial reports depend partly, on<br>
their capabilities or competencies to<br>
understand basic financial matters and<br>
the way they are displayed in financial<br>
reports.<br>
<b>Table 1.1 also emphasizes the importance</b><br>
of cost constraint, in which one is<br>
required to consider the cost and benefit<br>
of reporting certain items in the financial<br>
statements. The benefits of reporting<br>
financial information should justify and<br>
be greater than the costs imposed on<br>
supplying such information. When it is<br>
excessively expensive to report certain<br>
information in financial statements, the<br>
accounting framework allows a reporting<br>
entity (i.e., a business entity in respect of<br>
which financial reports are prepared) to<br>
avoid reporting such information.<br>
<b>Users of accounting information</b><br>
<b>Users of accounting information are the</b><br>
various stakeholders (parties or persons<br>
interested and/or concerned with the<br>
affairs of the business) who need<br>
financial information to make informed<br>
decisions. It is for this reason that,<br>
accounting is commonly referred to as<br>
the “language of business” since it acts<br>
as medium of communication between<br>

various parties involved/interested in a<br>

Table 1. 2: Users and their objectives for demanding accounting information<br>
<b>Users</b><br>
<b>Objectives and examples of decisions</b><br>
1. Internal users<br>
<b>Closely related to the company’s published financial</b><br>
statements<br>
(i)	 Management<br>
<b>Accounting information provide the basis for examining</b><br>
the performance of their business e.g., whether, they<br>
have been able to make profit or not. Thus, accounting<br>
information helps management to analyse their<br>
organization’s performance and financial position to be<br>
able to take appropriate measures, which will improve<br>
the business performance in case of poor performance.<br>
<b>In short, accounting information is important to achieve</b><br>
effective planning, control and decision making by<br>
management.<br>
(ii)	 Employees<br>
<b>In a market driven economy, demand for wage rise, bonus</b><br>
and better working conditions depends more on the<br>
financial performance of a business. Thus, accounting<br>
information serves as a useful tool for employees to<br>
assess the company’s profitability, which might have<br>
some consequence on their future remuneration and job<br>
security.<br>
(iii)	Owners<br>
<b>These provide funds or capital for the organization</b><br>
thus are curious to know whether the business is being<br>
conducted soundly including proper use of capital or<br>
economic resources of their business. Accounting<br>
information as presented in financial reports provides<br>
the basis to analyse the viability and profitability of their<br>
investment. Based on this, shareholders may decide<br>
whether to sell/hold shares and how to vote important<br>
matters related to their investments/business.<br>

2. External users<br>
<b>Distantly related to the company which has published</b><br>
its financial statements<br>
(i)	 Creditors/lenders<br>
<b>Creditors are the persons who supply goods and</b><br>
services on credit. Lenders on the other hand include<br>
banks or lenders of money to the business. The two<br>
are interested to know the financial soundness of the<br>
business before granting credit/loans. This information<br>
is very important to decide whether to extend goods and<br>
services or loans to the business. The information also<br>
is useful in setting the terms of credits.<br>
(ii)	 Tax authorities<br>
<b>Tax authorities need to know the earnings made by the</b><br>
businesses for the purpose of taxation or to determine<br>
the credibility of the tax returns filed on behalf of the<br>
business or to assess taxable income.<br>
(iii)	Investors<br>
<b>Prospective investors wish to examine the performance</b><br>
and financial position of the business to evaluate<br>
the potential for getting good or reasonable returns<br>
and prospects for viable and sustainable business<br>
undertaking. This information is important for their<br>
investment decisions.<br>
(iv)	Customers<br>
<b>Customers are interested in getting goods and services</b><br>
at reduced price. Informed customers may wish to know<br>
whether the business has in place a proper accounting<br>
control, which in turn will reduce the cost of production<br>
and eventually less price to be paid for supplied<br>
goods and services. For example, utility companies<br>
like TANESCO – when negotiating for new price of<br>
electricity they will normally be required to show the<br>
prevailing performance to justify their claims.<br>

<b>Fundamental principles of accounting</b><br>
<b>Fundamental principles of accounting</b><br>
are the basic rules/regulations and<br>
customs that provide guidelines for the<br>
preparation of accounting records and<br>
financial statements. Since there are<br>
many users of accounting information<br>
and their interests are not similar, it is<br>
important that, information provided<br>
is uniform and contains figures, which<br>
all can generally agree on. As these<br>
users look at information from different<br>
businesses and over different periods<br>
of time, they need some assurance that,<br>
information provided is relevant and<br>
faithfully represented. To achieve this,<br>
financial statements need to be prepared<br>
using<br>
similar<br>
approaches<br>
across<br>
businesses and over time. In short, by<br>
observing the same basic principles,<br>
preparers of financial statements achieve<br>
both uniformity and consistency in<br>
the structure and quality of presented<br>
financial statements. These are critically<br>
important in making comparison on the<br>
performance and financial position of<br>
the business across time and with other<br>
similar businesses in the industry.<br>

<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
17<br>
<b>Fundamental principles of accounting</b><br>
can be divided into two classes,<br>
accounting concepts and conventions.<br>
<b>Accounting concepts are the general</b><br>
rules and assumptions established by<br>
accounting bodies to guide the recording<br>
of business transactions and preparation<br>
of financial statements. They include the<br>
following: business entity, accrual basis<br>
of accounting, going concern, money<br>
measurement, accounting cost, dual<br>
aspect of accounting, accounting period,<br>
realisation and matching concept. On<br>
the other hand, accounting conventions<br>
refer to the common practices which are<br>
universally followed in recording and<br>
presenting accounting information of<br>
the business entity. One of the important<br>
differences with accounting concepts<br>
is that, accounting conventions are<br>
followed like customs and traditions in a<br>
society. They have been developed over<br>
the years, and adopted either by general<br>
agreement or common consent among<br>
accountants. In other words, accounting<br>
conventions have been evolved through<br>
regular and consistent practice over the<br>
years to achieve wider acceptance and<br>
influences the recording of books of<br>
accounts.<br>
<b>Four important accounting conventions,</b><br>
which have been used over time are;<br>
consistency, full disclosure, materiality<br>
and conservatism. The following sections<br>
describe further the different types of<br>
fundamental principles of accounting<br>
and how they affect the preparation of<br>
books of accounts.<br>
<b>Business entity</b><br>
<b>A</b><br>
company<br>
is<br>
considered<br>
as<br>
a<br>
separate living entity or person from<br>
its owners. One can observe this by<br>
looking at different aspects of business<br>
development. For example, a company<br>
has its own name, a birth-date which is<br>
the date of incorporation (legal process<br>
of forming a company) and place of<br>
incorporation. These details are usually<br>
maintained by the institution responsible<br>
for registering companies, business<br>
names and intellectual property rights. In<br>
<b>Tanzania, the institution responsible for</b><br>
this is called, the Business Registrations<br>
and Licensing Agency (BRELA). Other<br>
features of business entity include,<br>
clearly defined set of activities, regular<br>
reporting of financial affairs to the<br>
public, payment of taxes and ability to<br>
file lawsuits.<br>
<b>From accounting point of view, whatever</b><br>
the legal position or form, any business,<br>
whether a company or sole trade,<br>
qualifies to be called business entity.<br>
<b>Therefore, the books of accounts of any</b><br>
type of business are expected to reflect<br>
this viewpoint i.e., as accountant you<br>
should refrain from mixing the affairs<br>
of business with those of its owners.<br>
<b>When recording business transactions,</b><br>
one should not incorporate personal<br>
transactions and activities of the owner.<br>
<b>For example, if the owner buys a new</b><br>
pair of khanga, trousers or shoes for<br>
personal use, it is incorrect to record<br>
this in the business’ financial records<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
18<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
as business expenses. The importance<br>
of business entity includes, ability to<br>
measure properly the performance and<br>
financial position of the business as well<br>
as determining taxable income of the<br>
business. Without observing the business<br>
entity principle, the business transactions<br>
and owners personal revenue/expenses<br>
may be mixed up thus making it difficult<br>
to achieve the stated objectives.<br>
<b>Accrual basis of accounting</b><br>
<b>Accrual basis of accounting is the</b><br>
most important concept in accounting<br>
that governs the timing in recording of<br>
revenue and expenses. According to this<br>
concept, revenue should be recognised/<br>
recorded in financial records and<br>
matched against expenses to determine<br>
profit in the financial statements, when<br>
earned rather than when cash is collected.<br>
<b>Revenue is considered as being earned</b><br>
when the goods or services have been<br>
delivered to the clients. Expenses are<br>
supposed to be recognised (incurred)<br>
when goods and services are consumed<br>
in generating income rather than when<br>
actual cash is paid to suppliers of goods<br>
and services. Matching and realisation<br>
concepts are closely related with accrual<br>
basis of accounting, as explained in the<br>
following sections.<br>
<b>Realisation concept</b><br>
<b>The term realisation means the creation</b><br>
of legal right to receive money. This<br>
concept is derived from the principle<br>
of recognizing revenue. According to<br>
this principle, revenue from the sale of<br>
goods/services can only be recognized<br>
when such goods/services are delivered/<br>
rendered to the customer. It is at this<br>
point that, revenue can be recorded in<br>
the financial records of a business.<br>
<b>Matching concept</b><br>
<b>According to the matching concept, the</b><br>
revenue and the expenses incurred to earn<br>
the revenues must belong to the same<br>
accounting period. So once the revenue<br>
is realised and expenses incurred, the<br>
next step is to allocate and match up the<br>
two to the relevant accounting period.<br>
<b>This can be done with the help of accrual</b><br>
concept already explained.<br>
<b>Going concern</b><br>
<b>Financial statements are prepared by</b><br>
assuming that, the business will continue<br>
to operate for the foreseeable future<br>
unless there is evidence which indicate<br>
otherwise. In practice, the reasonable<br>
period of time, which is 12 months<br>
beyond the date when the financial<br>
statements are issued, is used to assess the<br>
going concern assumption. For example,<br>
suppose the business has difficulty in<br>
paying its debts or significant outstanding<br>
liabilities or has scaled down significant<br>
parts of operations or shut down one of<br>
its key branches. This might cast doubt<br>
on the ability of the business to continue<br>
operating as going concern and would<br>
need to be reflected in the financial<br>
statements. If there are no issues that<br>
indicate the existence of going concern<br>
problem, the business will be required<br>
to continue valuing all of its fixed assets<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
19<br>
(non–current assets) at original cost.<br>
<b>This is because, it is not foreseen or</b><br>
expected that, such assets will be sold in<br>
a near future. The valuation of assets at<br>
current value as reflected in market can<br>
only be done when the business is going<br>
to be sold or released to different person.<br>
<b>The going concern is very important in</b><br>
business world because it gives investors<br>
and creditors an indicator of how long<br>
a business will be around. The concept<br>
is used to indicate the business future<br>
stability.<br>
<b>Money measurement</b><br>
<b>Money measurement is also known as unit</b><br>
of measure concept. This is concerned<br>
with how different transactions are<br>
usually captured in financial records<br>
and financial statements. Generally,<br>
only business transactions and events<br>
that are capable of being measured in<br>
monetary terms are recognised in the<br>
financial statements. Money is therefore<br>
used as the unit of measurement in<br>
different accounting records and related<br>
financial reports. Examples of unit of<br>
measurement include, but not limited<br>
to, Tanzanian shillings (TZS), Kenyan<br>
shillings (KES), Uganda shillings (UGX)<br>
and the United States Dollar (USD or $).<br>
<b>Cost concept</b><br>
<b>Cost concept is also known as historical</b><br>
cost concept. Assets of the business<br>
are usually recorded at their original<br>
cost and no adjustment is made for the<br>
changes in the market value. Cost is<br>
usually determined through business<br>
transactions in which two or more<br>
unrelated and unaffiliated parties agree<br>
to do business, acting independently and<br>
in their self-interest. Therefore, this is<br>
more likely to be objective figure to use<br>
as long as the going concern is sustained.<br>
<b>Dual aspect of accounting</b><br>
<b>Dual aspect of accounting is also known</b><br>
as double entry system. This underscores<br>
the nature of business transactions i.e.,<br>
every business transaction has a dual<br>
or two aspects that must be recognized<br>
within assets, liabilities or equity. It is<br>
important to recognise both, the party<br>
giving the benefits and the one that<br>
receives the benefits. This is closely<br>
related to the basics of double entry<br>
principles i.e.; each business transaction<br>
must be recorded twice; every debit<br>
entry must have its corresponding credit<br>
entry.<br>
<b>Accounting period</b><br>
<b>Financial statements are usually prepared</b><br>
and presented to its users in periodical<br>
basis referred as accounting period.<br>
<b>However, the normal accounting period</b><br>
which is also expected legally is the<br>
twelve months period. In other words,<br>
on annual basis, financial statements of<br>
a particular business will be prepared to<br>
determine its performance and financial<br>
position.<br>
<b>Consistency</b><br>
<b>This concept holds that, when a business</b><br>
selects a particular method to account<br>
for specific items, then it should<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
20<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
continue to use that method even in<br>
subsequent accounting periods unless<br>
condition warrants a change. If, for some<br>
unavoidable reasons the method has to<br>
be changed, there should be a distinct<br>
note in the financial statements of the<br>
business giving relevant explanations<br>
so that users are informed of the reasons<br>
for such changes. This is important to<br>
enhance comparability of accounting<br>
figures over time in a meaningful manner.<br>
<b>Materiality</b><br>
<b>Information is considered to be material</b><br>
if its omission or misstatement could<br>
influence the economic decisions of<br>
users taken on the basis of the financial<br>
statements. Materiality therefore, relates<br>
to the significance of transactions,<br>
balances and errors contained in the<br>
financial statements. Materiality defines<br>
the threshold or cut-off point after which<br>
financial information becomes relevant<br>
to the decision-making needs of the<br>
users. Materiality is a relative concept<br>
depending with the size and particular<br>
circumstances of individual companies<br>
under consideration.<br>
<b>Full disclosure</b><br>
<b>Convention of full disclosure requires</b><br>
that, all material and relevant facts<br>
concerning financial statements should<br>
be fully disclosed. Full disclosure<br>
means that, there should be full, fair<br>
and adequate disclosure of accounting<br>
information. Adequate means sufficient<br>
set of information to be disclosed.<br>
<b>Fair indicates an equitable treatment</b><br>
of users. Full refers to complete and<br>
detailed presentation of information.<br>
<b>Thus, full disclosure suggests that,</b><br>
every financial statement should fully<br>
disclose all relevant information for all<br>
interested parties like investors, lenders,<br>
creditors and shareholders to see. This is<br>
important because, shareholders would<br>
like to know profitability of the firm<br>
while the creditors would like to know<br>
the solvency of the business. In the same<br>
way, other parties would be interested<br>
in the financial information according<br>
to their requirements. This is possible if<br>
financial statements disclose all relevant<br>
information in full, fair and adequate<br>
manner.<br>
<b>Conservatism</b><br>
<b>Conservatism or prudence convention</b><br>
is based on the principle that, one<br>
should anticipate no profit, but provide<br>
for all possible losses. When recording<br>
transactions in the books of accounts,<br>
assets and income are not to be overstated<br>
and provision/allowance should be<br>
made for all known expenses and losses<br>
whether the amount is known for certain<br>
or just an estimation. By doing so, all<br>
business expenses and liabilities will not<br>
be understated in the books of accounts.<br>
<b>It is based on the policy of playing safe in</b><br>
regard to showing profit – never overstate<br>
profit. Doing so, may lead to distribution<br>
of dividend out of capital, which is not a<br>
fair policy as it will lead to the reduction<br>
in the capital of the business. Based<br>
on this convention, profit should not<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
21<br>
be recorded until it is realised and any<br>
loss that is anticipated in the near future<br>
should be provided for. A good example<br>
on this includes, valuation of inventory<br>
whereby closing stock is supposed to<br>
be valued at cost or net realizable value,<br>
whichever is lower. Similarly, it is<br>
important to create provision/allowance<br>
for doubtful debts, discount on debtors,<br>
writing<br>
off<br>
intangible<br>
assets<br>
like<br>
goodwill and patents. The convention<br>
of conservatism is a very useful tool in<br>
situation of uncertainty and doubts.<br>

<b>Accounting information system</b><br>
<b>Generally, a system refers to a grouping</b><br>
of things, which are connected or<br>
interdependent forming a complex<br>
unity. A system usually has inputs,<br>
processing capability or activities and<br>
the output. Accounting information<br>
system (AIS) reflect well this definition<br>
as it is composed of different interrelated<br>
components and activities. They include,<br>
inputs (e.g., the source documents),<br>
processing (activities of transforming<br>
data from source documents into ledger<br>
accounts and preparation of financial<br>
statements)<br>
and<br>
output<br>
(generated<br>
financial statements). Technically, an<br>
<b>AIS can be defined as a system or</b><br>
set of processes for collecting data<br>
about business transactions; recording,<br>
organizing, and summarizing the data,<br>
which culminates into preparation of<br>
financial statements and other reports for<br>
internal and external users.<br>
<b>Types/categories of accounting system</b><br>
<b>By considering the way in which</b><br>
information is being captured, processed<br>
and generated, AIS can be classified into<br>
manual and computerised accounting<br>
system. A manual accounting system is<br>
dominated by paperwork i.e., the use of<br>
physical records, pads of paper and books,<br>
onto which the transactions are entered<br>
by hand. In contrast, in computerized<br>
accounting system, most of the work<br>
are done using information technology<br>
(IT). In other words, computers and<br>
application programs are used to process<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
22<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
business<br>
transactions.<br>
<b>Nowadays,</b><br>
most of the businesses make use of<br>
computerized accounting system except<br>
for small scale businesses. The reasons<br>
for this include, availability of advanced<br>
technology at relatively affordable<br>
cost. In other words, computers and<br>
related application programs that help<br>
in processing and generating of financial<br>
reports are relatively inexpensive.<br>
<b>Components/parts and contents of</b><br>
<b>AIS</b><br>
<b>Accounting information is composed of</b><br>
at least six basic components or parts.<br>
<b>These include people, procedures and</b><br>
instructions, data, software, information<br>
technology infrastructure and internal<br>
controls. People refers to individuals who<br>
use the system, including accountants,<br>
managers and business analysts or<br>
staff. Procedure and instructions are<br>
the ways and methods used to collect,<br>
store, retrieve and process data. Data<br>
include, all the details extracted from<br>
source<br>
documents<br>
while<br>
software<br>
consists of computer programs used for<br>
processing data. Information technology<br>
infrastructure includes all the hardware<br>
(e.g., computers) used to operate the AIS<br>
while internal controls are the security<br>
measures used to protect data (e.g.,<br>
passwords).<br>
<b>The contents of accounting system</b><br>
usually reflect the nature of business<br>
activities being undertaken by the<br>
company. For example, purchase of<br>
goods and services, sales of goods and<br>
services, payments to employees for<br>
wages earned, and financing activities<br>
(e.g., obtaining debt, selling shares, and<br>
paying interest to lenders). Specifically,<br>
the contents of AIS include, accounts<br>
payable, billings and accounts receivable,<br>
fixed assets, inventory and payroll.<br>
<b>Depending on the size of the company</b><br>
and volume of transactions, specialized<br>
accounting staff may be employed to<br>
facilitate information processing and<br>
generation of financial statements of a<br>
business.<br>

<b>This chapter intended to equip you with</b><br>
important knowledge about the nature<br>
and context of accounting. Different<br>
issues have been considered including,<br>
the meaning of accounting as a<br>
concept, brief history of the profession<br>
and branches of accounting. In order to<br>
understand the context that govern the<br>
accounting practices, the chapter has<br>
introduced the concept of regulatory<br>
framework of accounting. Different<br>
components of regulatory framework<br>
have been considered including, the<br>
<b>International</b><br>
<b>Financial</b><br>
<b>Reporting</b><br>
<b>Standards</b><br>
<b>(IFRS),</b><br>
<b>Accounting</b><br>
professional bodies (e.g., NBAA) and<br>
company law (Tanzania Company<br>
<b>Act, 2002). The chapter has also</b><br>
introduced the concept of conceptual<br>
framework of financial reporting. This<br>
has been discussed, as the primary<br>
source of qualitative characteristics<br>
of accounting information and other<br>
financial reporting matters.<br>
<b>Two</b><br>
fundamental<br>
qualitative<br>
characteristics of useful accounting<br>
information have also been identified<br>
including, faithful representation and<br>
relevance. Besides, there are four<br>
enhancing qualitative characteristics<br>
namely comparability, verifiability,<br>
timeliness<br>
and<br>
understandability.<br>
<b>Other important issues such as users</b><br>
of<br>
accounting<br>
information<br>
from<br>
within and outside the reporting entity<br>
have been considered. Examples on<br>
this include, managers and employees<br>
(internal users) and lenders, creditors<br>
and scholars (external users). The main<br>
reasons for each user groups to demand<br>
accounting<br>
information<br>
include,<br>
the decision-making usefulness of<br>
accounting information. This chapter<br>
has also provided the basic principles<br>
of accounting such as business entity,<br>
going concern and accrual basis of<br>
accounting. Moreover, the chapter<br>
provides basis for understanding<br>
how different accounting principles<br>
(concepts and conventions) guide the<br>
preparation of financial records and<br>
financial reports. The chapter has also<br>
introduced the concept of accounting<br>
system, which may be adopted by a<br>
business to facilitate the preparation of<br>
financial statements. Noted is the fact<br>
that, accounting system may either be<br>
computerised or manual depending<br>
on the level at which information<br>
technology is used to facilitate<br>
different processes involved in the<br>
preparation of financial statements.<br>
<b>The chapter provides a number of</b><br>
activities and exercises to enhance<br>
student’s learning about the nature<br>
and context of accountancy.<br>

<b>Business transaction</b><br>
<b>A business transaction is an economic</b><br>
event, which involves the exchange<br>
of goods or services from one person<br>
to another with money or money<br>
equivalent. The business transactions<br>
should have monetary value and tangible<br>
economic value to the economy of the<br>
entity as well as impact on the financial<br>
position of the entity. For example, when<br>
you go to the shop and buy clothes, food<br>
or other groceries for home use, you pay<br>
money and receive an item equivalent to<br>
the value of money paid.<br>
<b>Cash</b><br>
transactions<br>
versus<br>
credit<br>
transactions<br>
<b>By considering the timing of cash</b><br>
payment in exchange for goods and<br>
services, business transactions can be<br>
classified into two categories, cash<br>
transactions and credit transactions.<br>
<b>Cash transactions is the exchange of</b><br>
goods and services whereby cash is paid<br>
or received immediately. In other words,<br>
it is a transaction that involves cash<br>
receipt or payment. In contrast, credit<br>
transactions involve the exchange of<br>
goods and services but whose payment<br>
have been postponed until future time.<br>
<b>Therefore, for a business transaction to be</b><br>
considered as cash or credit transaction,<br>
this will depend on the nature of the<br>
agreement between the parties involved<br>
in the exchange. Different transactions,<br>
which may be involved in cash or credit<br>
transactions include, the purchases or<br>
sales of goods and services; the returns<br>
of goods previously bought or sold and<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Recording business</b><br>
transactions in<br>
books of accounts<br>
<b>Two</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter you will learn the concepts of business transactions, accounting<br>
records, principles of double entry system and books of prime entry. Also, you<br>
will learn how to prepare books of prime entry, ledger accounts and trial balance.<br>
The competencies developed in this chapter will enable you to describe books of<br>
prime entry, analyse different business transactions and how to record them into<br>
books of prime entry. You will also be able to post items contained into books of<br>
prime entry into ledger accounts and trial balance.<br>

payments to suppliers of goods and services. Others include, payments of expenses<br>
and receipts of cash from sale of goods as well as sale or acquisition of properties.<br>
<b>Accounting process</b><br>
Accounting process is also known as accounting cycle to reflect a series of steps<br>
that are regularly repeated in the same order from identifying and recording of<br>
business transactions to the time of preparing financial statements. The six basic<br>
steps involved in the accounting cycle are presented using figure 2.1:<br>
1. Indentify<br>
transactions<br>
through their<br>
source<br>
document<br>
2. Recording<br>
transactions<br>
in a journal<br>
3. Posting to<br>
ledger<br>
account<br>
6. Preparing<br>
financial<br>
statement<br>
5. Making<br>
year-end<br>
adjustements<br>
4. Preparing<br>
trial balance<br>
Figure 2. 1: Accounting process for preparation of financial statements<br>
As shown in figure 2.1, there are six major accounting steps associated with preparation<br>
of financial statements. The arrows in the figure (from one circle to another) indicate<br>
the flows of activities from the first step (number 1) to the last step (number 6). An<br>
arrow pointing to the first step from the last one i.e., prepare financial statements<br>
 identify business transactions through their source document, indicates that, after<br>
completing the cycle, usually at the end of an accounting period, another round or<br>
cycle will start again. Therefore, “accounting cycle” involves the cyclic sequence in<br>
which business transactions are recorded and processed until they become part of the<br>
financial statements at the end of the accounting period. Therefore, the preparation<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>

27<br>
of financial statements, will be repeated<br>
by using the same procedures in the new<br>
accounting period.<br>
<b>In this chapter, the major focus will be</b><br>
on four early processes, which include,<br>
identifying<br>
business<br>
transactions<br>
through their source documents, listing<br>
source documents in their respective day<br>
books, posting to ledger accounts and<br>
preparation of trial balance. The other<br>
processes are covered in subsequent<br>
chapters.<br>
<b>Books of prime/original entry</b><br>
<b>Books of prime entry or journals are</b><br>
the diaries of business activities where<br>
the transactions made by a business are<br>
recorded for the first time before posting<br>
into separate ledger accounts. The<br>
contents of books of prime entry appear<br>
in a descriptive and chronological (diary-<br>
like) record of day-to-day financial<br>
transactions. Other names for books of<br>
prime entry that reflect their use include,<br>
books of original entry and subsidiary<br>
day books or simply day books. Different<br>
types of business transactions each<br>
evidenced by a specific source document<br>
will be recorded in books of prime entry.<br>
<b>Due to this, combining them in one</b><br>
book of original entry, especially for<br>
large business entities, may be difficult<br>
to provide trails or follow-through<br>
in the accounting process. However,<br>
this might not be the case with a small<br>
business, it can be possible to record all<br>
its transactions in one book, the general<br>
journal, in which every transaction will<br>
be differentiated from each other with its<br>
specific explanations called narrations.<br>
<b>Therefore, for large businesses, it is a</b><br>
common practice to find specialised<br>
day books being used in recording the<br>
transactions of similar nature instead<br>
of recording all of them in one book<br>
(general journal). Used in this context,<br>
books of prime entry are also known as<br>
specialized journals. Whether general<br>
or specialized, books of prime entry<br>
serve the same purpose, act as sources<br>
of information for the preparation of<br>
various ledgers and provide trail of the<br>
transactions posted in the ledgers. This<br>
is important especially for auditors and<br>
accountants to find easily the chain of<br>
references that makes it possible to trace<br>
information, locate errors, and prevent<br>
fraud.<br>
<b>Structurally, books of prime entry</b><br>
are designed to capture the following<br>
information:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Date which indicates when the</b><br>
transactions occurred.<br>
(ii)	 The details column providing ex­<br>
planation related to the transac­<br>
tions,  usually reflecting the titles<br>
of accounts.<br>
(iii)	 The folio/reference column for<br>
cross-referencing back to the<br>
original source document such as<br>
the invoice or where the ledger<br>
accounts will be posted.<br>
(iv)	 The amount column, which show<br>
the monetary value of the trans­<br>
actions under consideration.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>

<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>The number and types of books of</b><br>
accounts to be prepared by the business<br>
entity will differ depending on the nature<br>
of activities being undertaken. A firm<br>
can either be a merchandising firm, a<br>
manufacturing firm, a service-providing<br>
entity or a non-profit organisation. This<br>
chapter will discuss only the books<br>
prepared by a merchandising firm. By<br>
definition, merchandising firm is a<br>
business that is engaged in buying and<br>
selling of finished products. The most<br>
common books of prime entry prepared<br>
in this context are, sales daybook or<br>
sales journal, purchases day book or<br>
purchases journal, sales returns daybook<br>
or returns inward journal, and purchases<br>
returns daybook or returns outward<br>
journal, cash book, and general journal.<br>

<b>Activity 2.1</b><br>
<b>In a group of five students, visit any</b><br>
two business entities operating within<br>
your locality. The visited business<br>
entities should be trading on various<br>
scales such as retailers, wholesalers,<br>
manufacturing (small or large), and<br>
service providers. During your visit,<br>
you should collect information to<br>
address the following questions:<br>
(a)	What kind of transaction are there<br>
in each visited entity?<br>
(b)	How is recording done and in<br>
which books?<br>
(c)	What documents are issued to<br>
evidence transactions?<br>
<b>The format and recording in special</b><br>
journals<br>
<b>To understand how business transactions</b><br>
are captured within the books of<br>
original entries, it is important that, you<br>
familiarise well with the format and<br>
contents to be entered into each category<br>
of special journal.<br>
<b>Sales day book or sales journal</b><br>
<b>The word sale means, the transacting</b><br>
or the exchange of goods in which a<br>
particular business transfer the ownership<br>
of goods to another/other persons. The<br>
goods under consideration must be those<br>
in which that business deals with in its<br>
ordinary under takings. The word ‘sales’<br>
must never be given to the disposal of<br>
other items, such as vans or buildings<br>
that were purchased to be used and not<br>
to be sold. Sales can be made either in<br>
cash or on credit.<br>
<b>A sales journal is used to record</b><br>
separately, the sale transactions made on<br>
credit, especially for a business that has<br>
a lot of transactions. The key source of<br>
information (source document) for sales<br>
journal recording is sales invoice. All<br>
details associated with the transaction<br>
are recorded in the journal in different<br>
columns. These columns will record<br>
the date, customer name and invoice<br>
number and folio, which is also known<br>
as posting reference column used to<br>

<b>Purchases day book or purchases journal</b><br>
The term purchases in accounting is used to mean those goods which the business<br>
buys with the prime intention of selling. Sometimes the goods are altered, added to,<br>
or used in the manufacture of something else, but it is the element of resale that is<br>
important. Purchases can be made either in cash or on credit. A purchases journal<br>
is a book of prime entries that is used to record credit purchases of goods. The cash<br>
purchases are not recorded in this journal; they are recorded in the cash book. The<br>
source of information for recording into purchases journal are invoices and bills<br>
received from suppliers. The net amount shown in the bills and invoices is the ones<br>
recorded in the journal.<br>
The purchase journal contains the following information: date, details or supplier’s<br>
name, invoice number, folio and amount payable, as follows.<br>
<b>Purchase journal format</b><br>
<b>Date</b><br>
<b>Supplier’s Name</b><br>
Invoice No.<br>
<b>Folio</b><br>
<b>Amount payable</b><br>
<b>(TZS)</b><br>

<b>Sales returns day book or returns inward journal</b><br>
Sales returns occur when a customer does not accept such goods and returns them<br>
to the seller for a full refund or credit. The goods returned are recorded in a journal<br>
known as sales return journal or return inwards day book. In response to the returned<br>
goods, the firm prepares a credit note for acknowledging the receipt of the goods.<br>
The credit note contains all key information associated with the goods returned,<br>
including details of the goods (quality, quantity, price), customer name, invoice<br>
number of goods being returned, and the date. Depending on the reasons associated<br>
with the return, such goods are usually received at the store for appropriate action.<br>
<b>Sales return day book format</b><br>
<b>Date</b><br>
<b>Credit memo No. Customer’s name</b><br>
<b>Folio</b><br>
<b>VAT</b><br>
<b>Sales return</b><br>
amount (TZS)<br>

<b>Purchases returns day book or returns outwards journal</b><br>
Purchase returns occur when a firm does not accept such goods and returns them<br>
to the supplier for a full refund or credit. The goods returned are recorded in a<br>
journal known as purchase return journal or returns outward book. A firm can return<br>
purchased items to the supplier either due to a mismatch in the quality of the item<br>
versus those required by the firm or for some other genuine reasons. Upon receipt<br>
of the returned goods, the supplier may acknowledge through a credit note. Hence,<br>
a credit note serves as a source of information for recording the goods returned in a<br>
purchases return journal.<br>

<b>Cash book</b><br>
A cash book is used to record all transactions involving cash payments and cash<br>
receipts. The book also serves as a ledger and it usually starts with the opening<br>
balance at hand or bank. A cash book of an organisation can have more than one<br>
column on both sides; on the receipt side and the payment side. A single-column<br>
cash book is used if it does not have a bank account, otherwise two columns or<br>
three columns cash book will be used. In two columns cash books, one column is<br>
for recording transactions made through cash on hand and the other is for recording<br>
transactions made through the bank. In a three columns cash books the third column<br>
is used to record discounts allowed to customers and discounts received from<br>
suppliers. In a company with a huge number of transactions, a separate petty cash<br>
book may be maintained to record smaller payments made by the business entity.<br>
The cashbook has two sides: the debit side which records an increase in cash or cash<br>
receipts and the credit side which records a decrease in cash or cash payments. The<br>
book will also record the cash withdrawn from the bank by the firm as well as cash<br>
deposited in the bank account by either the firm or customers.<br>

37<br>
<b>General journal</b><br>
<b>In a business organisation that keeps a</b><br>
specialised journal, the general journal<br>
is used to record transactions that are<br>
general and does not qualify to be<br>
recorded in any other books of prime<br>
entries. Such entries include, but are<br>
not limited to recording depreciation,<br>
purchase, and disposal of an asset on<br>
credit, writing off bad debts, bad debts<br>
recovered, interest income, interest<br>
expense, correction of errors in the<br>
ledger accounts, and other final account<br>
adjustments. The preparation of a general<br>
journal follows a double-entry system<br>
under which two accounts that are<br>
affected by the transaction are identified.<br>
<b>One account is debited and the other is</b><br>
credited together with a narration that<br>
summarizes what happened.<br>
<b>Examination type questions, normally</b><br>
use the term journalizing to describe<br>
the process of recording of business<br>
transaction into journal. For general<br>
journal, this involves the following steps.<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identifying the transaction from</b><br>
the source documents such<br>
as, bank slip, salary slip and<br>
invoices.<br>
(ii)	 Identifying the affected accounts<br>
by the transaction along with<br>
identifying the type of the<br>
accounts affected, whether it<br>
is an asset, liability or owners’<br>
equity account.<br>
(iii)	 Determining the effect of the<br>
transaction on the account i.e.,<br>
increase or decrease.<br>
(iv)	 Using the rules of the double<br>
entry system, determine whether<br>
to debit or credit the account.<br>
(v)	 Entering the transaction in the<br>
journal.<br>
(vi)	 Prepare the journal entries for<br>
the accounts affected and give a<br>
short but clear explanation about<br>
the nature of the transaction (the<br>
narration).<br>
<b>Format of general journal</b><br>
<b>Date</b><br>
<b>Account titles and explanations</b><br>
<b>Folio</b><br>
<b>Debit</b><br>
<b>Credit</b><br>
26/11/2022<br>
<b>Account to be debited</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Account to be credited</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Narration to describe the transaction</b><br>
Based on double entry accounting principle, assets and expenses are debited when<br>
there is an increase and credited whenever there is a decrease. Liabilities and revenues<br>
are credited when there is an increase and debited whenever there is a decrease.<br>
Using example 2.5, the general journal for some of the transactions will be as follows.<br>

<b>Required</b><br>
As accountant trainee in Kazingumu Enterprises Ltd., prepare general journal for<br>
the month of January, 2019 to record the capital in books of accounts.<br>
<b>The principles of double entry and ledger accounts</b><br>
Double entry principle requires that every business transaction must be recorded<br>
twice, a debit entry must have its corresponding credit entry, and vice versa. In other<br>
words, every financial transaction undertaken by the firm will have an equal but<br>
opposite impact on at least two accounts. This means that, the amounts entered as<br>
debits will be equal to the amounts entered as credits. In this respect, double-entry<br>
implies that, the accounting equation (Assets=Liabilities + Owner’s equity) will<br>
always remain in balance. The accounting equation can also be expanded to include<br>
drawings made by the owners as well as profits made by the firm in normal business<br>
transactions. The equation can be rewritten as follows:<br>
<b>Assets =Liabilities +Capital +Profits-Drawings or</b><br>
<b>Assets= Liabilities +Capital +Revenues-Expenses-Drawings</b><br>
<b>Assets +Expenses +Drawings=Liabilities +Capital +Revenues</b><br>
The assets, expenses, and drawings accounts are debited when there is an increase<br>
in balances and credited whenever there is a decrease in balances. The accounts for<br>
capital, revenue, and liabilities are credited for any increase in balances and will be<br>
debited whenever there is a decrease in balances.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>

<b>Ledger accounts</b><br>
After recording transactions in the books of original entry and the general journal,<br>
individual accounts are prepared and the amount is posted either on the debit side<br>
or credit side of the ledger account. An account is a page or a section within the<br>
ledger that records a particular transaction of either asset, liability, capital, income or<br>
expense. The format of the account indicating key issues to be recorded is presented<br>
as follows.<br>
Dr.<br>
<b>Name of account</b><br>
Cr.<br>

<b>Date</b><br>
<b>Particular</b><br>
<b>Folio</b><br>
<b>Amount</b><br>
Alternatively, account can be presented in “T format”; hence the name “T Account”,<br>
which depict the two major sides of the account, i.e., debit and credit side. It is<br>
called as such (T Account) to reflect its visual representation as it resembles the<br>
shape of the alphabet “T”, as presented in the following diagram.<br>
