<b>T account is widely used by accountants</b><br>
and<br>
students<br>
for<br>
practices<br>
and<br>
examinations due to its simplicity in<br>
recording business transactions as all the<br>
additions and subtractions to the account<br>
can be tracked and represented easily.<br>
<b>For good presentation and maintenance,</b><br>
similar types of accounts will be grouped<br>
together depending on the nature or<br>
class of the transactions forming what is<br>
known as ledger. There are three major<br>
categories of ledgers including, sales<br>
ledger in which the customers’ accounts<br>
are kept; purchases ledger, which<br>
keeps suppliers’ accounts and general<br>
ledger which keeps the remaining other<br>
accounts.<br>
<b>The general ledger is also known as</b><br>
nominal ledger while purchases and sales<br>
ledgers are collectively called personal<br>
ledgers. The cash book sometimes is<br>
also regarded as ledger as it has the two<br>
common accounts for cash and bank<br>
transactions. Remember, the accounting<br>
process aims at preparing financial<br>
statements of the business entity.<br>
<b>Therefore, the general ledger accounts</b><br>
are the ones that will be processed<br>
throughout the accounting process. The<br>
personal or subsidiary ledger accounts<br>
will be represented by two “total<br>
accounts” called “trade receivables<br>
account” and “trade payables account”<br>
in the general ledger.<br>
<b>Sales ledger</b><br>
<b>This ledger book contains all the accounts</b><br>
of individual credit customers, which are<br>
the personal accounts. Thus, it is used to<br>
records all transactions made between<br>
the business and credit customers for<br>
goods or services. Periodically the sales<br>
day book transactions are posted to the<br>
general ledger accounts and to the sales<br>
ledger accounts. In addition, transactions<br>
listed in the sales returns day book do<br>
also affect various customers’ accounts<br>
in the sales ledger and their totals should<br>
be summarised in the general ledger<br>
accounts. The double entries postings<br>
from sales day book and sales returns<br>
day book are as follows:<br>

<b>Purchases ledger</b><br>
Purchases ledger book contains all the accounts of individual credit suppliers –<br>
the personal accounts used to record credit transactions between business entities<br>
and their suppliers of goods and services. The purchases ledger thus provides the<br>
detailed transactions of each individual credit suppliers of goods. Periodically, the<br>
purchases day book transactions are posted to the general ledger accounts and to the<br>
purchase’s ledger accounts. Furthermore, transactions listed in the purchases returns<br>
day book do also affect various suppliers’ accounts in the purchases ledger and<br>
their totals should be summarised in the general ledger accounts. The double entry<br>
postings from purchases day book and purchases returns day book are as follows:<br>
<b>In the general ledger:</b><br>
Dr.<br>
<b>Purchases account</b><br>
xx<br>
Cr.<br>
<b>Trade payables account</b><br>
xx<br>
(With the periodical total of the purchases day book)<br>
Dr.<br>
<b>Trade payables account</b><br>
xx<br>
Cr.<br>
<b>Returns outwards account/purchases returns account</b><br>
xx<br>
(With the periodical total purchases returns of the purchases returns day<br>
book)<br>
<b>In the purchases ledger:</b><br>
Dr.<br>
<b>Individual suppliers’ accounts</b><br>
xx<br>
(With their respective value of goods returned to credit suppliers)<br>
<b>Cr.	 Individual Suppliers’ accounts</b><br>
xx<br>
(With the respective value of goods purchased from them on credit)<br>

<b>General ledger</b><br>
General ledger is the main ledger book of the business, which contains all the accounts<br>
of the business recorded in double entry system. The list of accounts presented in the<br>
general ledger include the following; assets, liabilities, capital/equity, revenue and<br>
expenses. Therefore, general ledger provides all the accounts used in the preparation<br>
of financial statements.<br>
In relation to the books of original entries, the general ledger is the book of accounts<br>
into which periodical totals from the books of prime entries are posted to. Information<br>
presented earlier as example 2.5 is reproduced here for quick reference to illustrate<br>
the preparation of general ledger.<br>

<b>Balancing off accounts</b><br>
In the previous section, you have learned how to prepare accounts using a double-<br>
entry system. At the end of each accounting period, figures in the account are<br>
examined to summarize the situation they present. This is done to all accounts of<br>
customers and suppliers of various services and goods. The purpose is to determine<br>
how much customers owe the business for the goods that have been sold to them<br>
as well as how much the company is owed by its suppliers as a result of services/<br>
goods delivered by them. The remaining balances will either be transferred to the<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
49<br>
next financial year or closed and the total<br>
amount transferred to another related<br>
account.<br>
<b>The following steps should be followed</b><br>
when balancing off various accounts:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Add up both sides to find</b><br>
out their totals – do not write<br>
anything in the account at this<br>
stage.<br>
(ii)	 Deduct the smaller total from the<br>
larger total to find the balance<br>
(the balancing figure).<br>
(iii)	 Enter the balance on the side<br>
with the smallest total. This<br>
now means that the totals in<br>
both sides will be equal – this<br>
balancing figure that has been<br>
inserted is known as balance<br>
carried down (balance c/d)<br>
(iv)	 Enter the total amounts for both<br>
the debit and credit side.<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Enter</b><br>
the<br>
balance<br>
amount<br>
(labelled earlier as c/d) on the<br>
line below the totals on the<br>
opposite side to the balance<br>
shown above the totals. This is<br>
known as balance brought down<br>
(balance b/d).<br>
(vi)	 Against<br>
the<br>
balance<br>
c/d,<br>
complete the date column by<br>
entering the last day of that<br>
period and show the first day<br>
of the next period against the<br>
balance b/d.<br>
<b>Note that, the balance brought down</b><br>
can appear in any side of the account<br>
(debit or credit) depending on the nature<br>
of transactions. However, normally all<br>
assets, expenses and losses accounts<br>
have debit balances while incomes/<br>
revenue; capital and liabilities, credit<br>
balances.<br>
<b>During balancing off accounts, one</b><br>
needs to take note of whether the<br>
account is nominal, personal or a<br>
real account. Nominal accounts are<br>
temporary accounts that are maintained<br>
within one accounting period. In the end,<br>
their balances are transferred to income<br>
statement.<br>
<b>All</b><br>
income<br>
statements<br>
accounts<br>
(losses,<br>
gains,<br>
expenses,<br>
revenues) are nominal accounts. On the<br>
other hand, real accounts are permanent<br>
accounts representing tangibles assets<br>
and their balances are carried to the<br>
beginning of another accounting period.<br>
<b>Hence, they appear in the balance sheet/</b><br>
statement of financial position. Examples<br>
on this include, furniture, motor vans and<br>
buildings. As for personal accounts,<br>
these can either be assets or liabilities.<br>
<b>They too have to appear in the balance</b><br>
sheet/statement of financial position.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
50<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
Examples of personal accounts are accounts receivables and payables, drawings and<br>
capital accounts.<br>

Trial balance and its use in preparation of financial records<br>
A trial balance is a report or a worksheet generated at the end of the accounting<br>
period containing the list of all the ledger accounts and their corresponding balances.<br>
This worksheet is designed such that it has about three columns. The first column<br>
contains the details or particular of the ledger accounts (name of the account) while<br>
the second and third columns are usually labelled as debit and credit, respectively.<br>
If an account has a debit balance, then this figure will be recorded on the debit side<br>
while for the credit balance on the credit side. In other words, trial balance reflects<br>
the application of doubled entry system as used in recording ledger accounts. If<br>
the summation in the two columns appears to be the same, this is an indication<br>
that, the double entry posting was done correctly. However, balancing between the<br>
debit totals and credit totals does not mean that, no errors have been committed.<br>
Nonetheless, trial balance serves as a useful tool in facilitating the preparation of<br>
financial statements. It provides a meaningful summary of important information<br>
one would to prepare the financial statements of a business. Moreover, trial balance<br>
is also used to check arithmetical accuracy of the ledger accounts, and to detect<br>
errors committed in the previous accounting process.<br>
The following are the basic steps that need to be followed when preparing a trial<br>
balance:<br>
(i)<br>
As already explained, the first step will involve determining the closing<br>
balances of all the ledger accounts, i.e., those found in sales ledger, purchases<br>
ledger and general ledger.<br>
(ii)	 Then prepare a three-column worksheet, one column for the account name<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
54<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
and the corresponding columns for debit and credit balances.<br>
(iii)	 Fill out the account name and the balance of such account in the appropriate<br>
debit or credit column.<br>
(iv)	 Then find the total for both the debit column and the credit column. Ideally,<br>
in a balanced error-free situation, the totals of debit and credit sides should be<br>
the same.<br>
(v)<br>
Once, it has been established that, the totals for debit and credit sides are<br>
same, the trial balance will be closed. If there is a difference between debit<br>
and credit sides, it is important to find and rectify errors, as will be discussed<br>
in chapter three of this book.<br>

<b>In this chapter, you have learned</b><br>
about books of prime entry defined as<br>
a diary–like documents used to record<br>
day to day business transactions. It<br>
is for this reason that they are also<br>
termed as book of original entries,<br>
day books and journals. Key issue<br>
was to elaborate how different types<br>
of business transactions are recorded<br>
in different types of books of prime<br>
entry. It has been shown that, different<br>
types of business transactions are<br>
recorded in different/specific type of<br>
day book – the basis for having terms<br>
like specialised journals. For example,<br>
credit sales are usually recorded in<br>
sales day book while credit purchases,<br>
in purchases day book. When dealing<br>
with returns; returns inwards (goods<br>
returned by customers), will be<br>
recorded in sales returns day book<br>
while returns outwards (purchases<br>
returns or goods returned to suppliers),<br>
in purchases returns day book. Other<br>
transactions outside the scope of<br>
these will be recorded in different<br>
types of journals. For example, cash<br>
transactions, in cash book and others<br>
in general journal.<br>
<b>The chapter has also elaborated</b><br>
the posting of different business<br>
transactions from the books of prime<br>
entry into ledger accounts. For<br>
example, credit customers (debtors),<br>
these are usually posted into sales<br>
ledger while creditors, in purchases<br>
ledger. Other types of entries that do<br>
not fall into the scope of the two are<br>
usually posted into general ledger.<br>
<b>Besides, you have learned how the</b><br>
balancing off the ledger accounts and<br>
the preparation of trial balance are<br>
done. The importance of trial balance,<br>
which contains a list of ledger accounts<br>
and their corresponding balances, has<br>
also been considered. Trial balance<br>
acts as a bridge towards the preparation<br>
of financial statements. Rather than<br>
referring to a large volumes of ledger<br>
accounts, it provides the convenience<br>
in terms of having a summarized<br>
picture of what has transpired for<br>
the whole accounting period. Trial<br>
balance is also useful in terms of<br>
checking arithmetical accuracy and<br>
compliance to the principle of double<br>
entry system when preparing financial<br>
statements.<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Three</b><br>
<b>Correction of</b><br>
accounting errors<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter you will learn about different types of errors and procedures<br>
involved in identifying and correcting them in books of accounts. The competencies<br>
developed in this chapter will enable you to apply relevant accounting principles<br>
in identifying different types of errors and make the necessary corrections in the<br>
books of accounts. This is important for preparing financial statements that show<br>
a true and fair view on the performance and financial position of the business.<br>
<b>Differentiating errors from frauds</b><br>
An error is an undeliberate mistake that has occurred or committed accidentally, and<br>
no one is intended to benefit. If done intentionally, it becomes fraud. Even though<br>
the employees in charge of accounting department are supposed to be very careful<br>
and accurate in recording the transactions, the occurrence of mistake or errors is<br>
likely.<br>
While recording the transactions in the books of accounts, it is quite likely that some<br>
mistakes may be committed. An error may occur while the transaction is initially<br>
recorded or while posting from journals to the ledger accounts or in addition/<br>
subtraction of some figures. Whenever an error occurs, it may either affect the<br>
arithmetical accuracy of the accounts or may defeat the basic principles of book-<br>
keeping. Hence, they need proper rectification by making necessary corrections in<br>
the books of accounts. Table 3.1 that follows provides different types of  errors that<br>
may occur at different stages of the accounting process.<br>

At the end of the accounting period, a trial balance is prepared to check the arithmetical<br>
accuracy of the accounts. If the trial balance does not balance, it implies that, there<br>
are arithmetical errors in the accounts which require detection and correction.<br>
However, even if the trial balance agrees, there may still be some errors - these kinds<br>
of errors might be difficult to detect. Their detection may be by chance or during the<br>
auditing process.<br>
Errors can also be understood well if one can differentiate them from frauds. Table<br>
<b>3.2 provides some of the important differences between the two by considering the</b><br>
presence or absence of intention or motives and objectives for their commitment.<br>
<b>Table 3. 2: Differences between errors and frauds</b><br>
<b>Error</b><br>
<b>Fraud</b><br>
(a)	 This is undeliberate mistake that<br>
have occurred during recording or<br>
posting process (they are not done<br>
intentionally)<br>
(a)	 This is an act that is intentionally<br>
carried out to benefit certain<br>
individuals.<br>
(b)	 Errors are considered as instances<br>
-therefore the doer does not plan<br>
to hide them – if discovered and<br>
corrected even the doer may be<br>
happy to see that they have been<br>
corrected<br>
(b)	 Fraud is an act of deception<br>
that is carried out with a sense<br>
of unfairness. The doer usually<br>
conceal it careful so that it cannot<br>
be discovered or corrected.<br>
(c)	 There is no benefit or motives<br>
behind its commitment – they occur<br>
by chances.<br>
(c)	 Frauds are purely alternation/<br>
manipulation of accounting records<br>
for one’s own benefits.<br>

<b>The need for correcting errors</b><br>
<b>Once errors are detected it is important</b><br>
that they are corrected accordingly so as<br>
to achieve the following objectives:<br>
(i)	 Generally, to present the correct<br>
accounting information.<br>
(ii)	 To present the correct picture of<br>
business performance in terms<br>
of profit or loss made during the<br>
accounting period.<br>
(iii)	To disclose the true and fair<br>
view of financial position of the<br>
business.<br>
<b>The classification of errors and</b><br>
procedures for their corrections<br>
<b>After detecting the errors, either as wrong</b><br>
entries or wrong amount, the procedure<br>
for correcting them involves two steps.<br>
<b>The first step is the identification of the</b><br>
correct double entry(ies) and the second<br>
is to make appropriate adjustment.<br>
<b>In accounting errors may arise due to</b><br>
arithmetical or clerical actions. Thus,<br>
we have two types of errors, arithmetical<br>
and clerical errors. Within these, there<br>
are two main categories of errors, those<br>
affecting the agreement of trial balance<br>
and others, which do not.<br>
(i)	 Arithmetical errors<br>
<b>Arithmetical errors arise due to</b><br>
transaction’s discrepancies between<br>
amounts in words and amount in<br>
figures. Also, they can arise due to<br>
wrong addition in the content of an<br>
invoice.<br>
(ii)	 Clerical errors<br>
<b>Clerical errors arise from wrong</b><br>
transaction posting due to copying<br>
or writing mistakes. Clerical errors<br>
are those errors which are generally<br>
committed by the clerical staff in<br>
recording transactions either in the<br>
books of prime entry or in the ledger<br>
accounts (books of final entry).<br>
<b>Errors not affecting the agreement of</b><br>
trial balance<br>
<b>When the totals in debit and credit sides</b><br>
of trial balance are equal, this does<br>
not provide assurance for correctness<br>
and accuracy in the posting of ledger<br>
accounts. There may still be some<br>
errors, which however, do not create a<br>
difference in the debit and credit totals<br>
of a trial balance. Despite of this, these<br>
errors distort the financial statements. In<br>
other words, the financial statements will<br>
provide misleading or false   information<br>
regarding the performance and financial<br>
position of a business. Such errors<br>
include the following:<br>
(i)	 Errors of omission: under<br>
this type of error, a transaction<br>
is completely omitted in the<br>
financial records. This means,<br>
a transaction has been done but<br>
was not recorded anywhere in the<br>
books of accounts. For example;<br>
purchases from MUPSA Co.<br>
<b>Ltd worth TZS 200,000 has not</b><br>
been recorded in any books of<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
63<br>
accounts. Since no any records<br>
has been made in books of<br>
account, then the trial balance<br>
would still balance.<br>
(ii)	 Errors of commission: these<br>
errors occur when the correct<br>
transactions’ amount is posted in<br>
wrong account but in the same<br>
class of accounts. For example,<br>
a sale of TZS 11,000,000 to<br>
<b>Chacha Godfrey is entered in</b><br>
the account of Kasim Godfrey.<br>
<b>Second example, payment of</b><br>
wages, TZS 400,000 posted to<br>
rent account. Noted is the fact<br>
that, in both cases the correct<br>
transaction<br>
amounts<br>
were<br>
posted to the wrong or different<br>
accounts of debtors and expenses<br>
respectively. Due to this, the trial<br>
balance will balance without the<br>
errors being noticed because both<br>
accounts’ balances will appear in<br>
the same sides.<br>
(iii)	 Errors of principle: these are<br>
errors which occur when the<br>
transactions are posted in a<br>
wrong class of account, but of the<br>
same balance characteristics. For<br>
example, a purchase of a non-<br>
current asset, such as a van being<br>
debited to an expenses account,<br>
such as motor expenses account.<br>
(iv)	 Errors of original entry: this<br>
type of error occurs where the<br>
original transaction is incorrectly<br>
recorded in the book of account<br>
with the incorrect amount both in<br>
the debit and credit entries (double<br>
entry system). For example, cash<br>
purchases of TZS 23,000,000<br>
being wrongly recorded in the<br>
cash book (original entry) as TZS<br>
32,000,000 and the same being<br>
posted to the purchases account.<br>
<b>The trial balance totals would</b><br>
still balance.<br>
(v)	 Compensating<br>
errors:<br>
these<br>
errors occur when two different<br>
mistakes such as posting the<br>
amount in the books of accounts<br>
are done but they offset or cancel<br>
each other. For example, sales<br>
account was over added by TZS<br>
70,000 and the purchases account<br>
was also over added by the same<br>
amount. These two errors would<br>
then offset or cancel each other in<br>
the trial balance. This is because<br>
the totals of both the debit and<br>
credit sides of the trial balance<br>
will be TZS 70,000 more.<br>
(vi)	 Complete reversal of entries:<br>
these errors occur when a<br>
transaction is posted in the<br>
correct accounts but in the wrong<br>
side. For example, cash sales of<br>
<b>TZS 800,000 being credited in</b><br>
cash book and debited in sales<br>
account. Based on this, the trial<br>
balance totals would still agree.<br>
(vii)	 Transposition<br>
errors:<br>
these<br>
errors occur when accounting<br>

<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
64<br>

officer accidentally reverses several adjacent digits when recording business<br>
transaction. For example, a credit sale of TZS 10,450,000 being entered<br>
accidentally in the books of accounts as TZS 10,540,000. For this, the trial<br>
balance totals would still balance.<br>

<b>Errors which affect the agreement of trial balance</b><br>
A business entity prepares a trial balance periodically, usually at the end of every<br>
reporting period. The general purpose of producing a trial balance is to ensure the<br>
entries in a company’s financial data are arithmetically correct. When trial balance<br>
does not agree, an effort is made to locate errors and rectify them. The following is<br>
a description of errors which affect the trial balance agreement.<br>
(i)	 Single entry<br>
Single entry is a violation of principle of double entry system as a business transaction<br>
is recorded only once i.e., no corresponding entry can be found in the books of<br>
accounts. This will cause a trial balance not to tally/agree. For example, cash sales<br>
of TZS 100,000 being recorded in the cash book only.<br>
(ii)	 Overcasting and undercasting<br>
Overcasting is used to describe the incorrect recorded amount that is higher than the<br>
actual amount of business transaction. In other words, the recorded figure has been<br>
overstated. Undercasting, on the other hand, describes the recorded amount that<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
70<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
is less than the actual amount of business transaction i.e., the recorded figure has<br>
been understated. These two types of errors usually occur due to incorrect additions<br>
in the books of accounts. When it is stated that, the purchases account has been<br>
undercasted/understated by TZS 1,000,000, this means that, the correct summation<br>
is less by this amount. Since the purchases account usually has a debit balance,<br>
the trial balance will not balance by TZS 1,000,000 with debit side having lesser<br>
amount than credit side by the same amount.<br>
(iii)	 Wrong amount posted<br>
The wrong amount is posted to one account related to the transaction. In other<br>
words, all of the accounts involved would not balance for the wrong amount. For<br>
example, purchase returns valued at TZS 680,000 being credited in the purchase<br>
returns account as TZS 860,000 while creditor account debited as TZS 680,000. This<br>
means, the purchase returns account had been posted with the wrong or incorrect<br>
amount (TZS 180,000) while the correct figure (TZS 680,000) posted accurately in<br>
the creditor account.<br>
<b>Correction of errors using suspense account</b><br>
When the trial balance totals are not equal, we should try our level best to find the<br>
errors, which resulted into such a disagreement. When they cannot be found, the<br>
trial balance totals can be made to agree with each other by inserting the difference<br>
in figure between the two sides (Dr. and Cr.). The recipient side being the one with<br>
smaller amount. The inserted figure is usually accompanied with a description<br>
“suspense account” to reflect the name of a newly created account. A suspense<br>
account can be defined as an account that is used temporarily to carry doubtful<br>
entries and discrepancies pending their analysis and permanent classification. It is an<br>
imaginary account opened for the purpose of just tallying/resolving the differences<br>
in totals found in the trial balance.<br>
In the statement of financial position, the balance of a suspense account will be<br>
shown either as liabilities or assets depending on whether it has a credit or debit<br>
balance respectively. The journal entries for the correction of accounting errors<br>
involving a suspense account can be presented as follows:<br>
(i)<br>
Debit the account which should have been debited (but is not debited) and<br>
credit the suspense account (which has been debited temporarily),<br>
(ii)	 Debit suspense account (which has been credited for the time being) and<br>
credit the account which should have been credited (but is not credited).<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>

<b>Exercise 3.2</b><br>
The following errors were discovered in the books of Mjimkongwe General<br>
Supplies after the trial balance extracted on 31st July, 2021 failed to balance.<br>
July 2	 Cash received TZS 600,000 from Upendo had only been posted on the<br>
receipt side of the cash book.<br>
July 5	 An item of TZS 250,000 for interest on bank overdraft was credited to<br>
interest account.<br>
July 8	 Goods sold to Vishandi for TZS 4,000,000 had been posted in purchases<br>
ledger as TZS 400,000.<br>
July 16	 A bill receivable for TZS 530,000 was posted to bill payable ledger.<br>
July 25	 Carriage on machinery, TZS 300,000 had been posted to carriage in­<br>
wards account.<br>
<b>Required:</b><br>
Prepare journal entries and suspense account to correct different errors as<br>
observed in the books of Mjimkongwe General Supplies.<br>
Revised income statement and statement of financial position<br>
Errors committed with regard to nominal accounts affect the profit or loss of the<br>
business. In contrast, errors in personal and real accounts have no effect on profit or<br>
loss of the business. Therefore, the profit figure has to be adjusted if there is an error<br>
discovered that affects items used in the calculation of profit. This includes all the<br>
items that appear in the income statement including but not limited to:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Sales and purchases,</b><br>
(ii)	 Sales returns and purchase returns,<br>
(iii)	 Carriage inwards and outwards,<br>
(iv)	 Opening and closing stock,<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
76<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
(v)	 All revenue items,<br>
(vi)	 All expenses items, and<br>
(vii)	 Depreciation.<br>
For an example, there must be adjustment when one of the pages is omitted in the<br>
sales day book in calculating the sales total for the month. This means that, the sales<br>
and revenue figure for the period were understated. As a result, even the net profit<br>
declared for the period would have been understated. Comparably, the statement<br>
of financial position is the one that is mostly affected by different types of errors<br>
identified in the chapter. This happens when the errors affecting the information<br>
contained in financial documents is incorrectly entered into financial database.<br>
Likewise, the understatement or overstatement of income statement items such as<br>
sales figure will also affect the statement of financial position. This is because, the<br>
net profit figure will be included as part of owner’s equity.<br>
Correction of accounting errors may also involves dealing with previously issued<br>
financial statements. This may include errors in the recognition, measurement,<br>
presentation or disclosure in financial statements caused by mathematical mistakes,<br>
and mistakes in applying the principle of double entry system. It may also include,<br>
the oversight of facts existing when the financial statements were being prepared. In<br>
this case, the prior period financial statements should be restated. The restatement<br>
requires the accountant to:<br>
(a)	Reflect the cumulative effect of the error on periods prior to those presented<br>
in the carrying value of assets and liabilities as at the beginning of the first<br>
period.<br>
(b)	Make an offsetting adjustment to the opening balance of retained earnings for<br>
that period.<br>
(c)	Adjust the financial statement for each prior period presented, to reflect the<br>
error correction.<br>

<b>In this chapter you have learned about</b><br>
correction of accounting errors. This is<br>
one among the important adjustments<br>
in accounting, which is supposed<br>
to be done even for unintentional<br>
mistakes. Errors should never just be<br>
accepted. The chapter has introduced<br>
the meaning of errors i.e., undeliberate<br>
mistakes committed in the process<br>
of preparing financial records. This<br>
is different from frauds in which<br>
mistakes are committed intentionally.<br>
<b>Unlike errors, frauds are committed</b><br>
with bad motives of gaining some<br>
benefits.<br>
<b>Different classes and types of errors</b><br>
have been identified including, those<br>
which do not affect the agreement<br>
of trial balance and those, which<br>
affect the trial balance. Examples of<br>
errors, which do not affect the trial<br>
balance include, errors of omission,<br>
commission,<br>
principles,<br>
original<br>
entry, compensating errors, complete<br>
reversal of entries and error of<br>
transposition. Errors which affect<br>
the trial balance, on the other hand,<br>
include single entry, where the figure<br>
in the transaction is overcasted or<br>
undercasted and where the wrong<br>
amount is posted. The correction of<br>
different types of errors have also<br>
been considered including the use of<br>
suspense account in which doubtful<br>
entries and discrepancies will be<br>
posted pending their analysis and<br>
eventually necessary correction to<br>
be done. The importance of general<br>
journal in the correction of accounting<br>
errors have also been elaborated. The<br>
chapter has provided a number of<br>
examples, activities and exercises to<br>
enable students understand more the<br>
concept of corrections of accounting<br>
errors.<br>

<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Four</b><br>
<b>Reserves and</b><br>
provisions/<br>
allowances<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter you will learn about the concepts of reserves and provisions/<br>
allowances, classification and proper methods for their estimation and effects on<br>
different items as reported in financial statements. The competencies developed will<br>
enable you to apply the basic principles of reserves and provisions in determining/<br>
estimating fair value of reserves and provisions to be recorded in the books of<br>
accounts.<br>
<b>Nature and classification of reserves</b><br>
<b>Reserves are part of profits or gains that</b><br>
have been set aside to achieve specific<br>
objectives such as paying for bonuses,<br>
expected<br>
legal<br>
settlement,<br>
repairs<br>
and maintenance or long-term debts.<br>
<b>Reserves may also be used by businesses</b><br>
for strengthening their financial position,<br>
buying fixed assets (non-current assets)<br>
or developing and expanding a business.<br>
<b>As a concept, reserves are commonly</b><br>
used in companies’ business settings.<br>
<b>This is different from sole proprietorship</b><br>
where the decision to retain profit or<br>
spend profits on personal use rest with<br>
one individual. For a company, the<br>
decision on how a company’s profit<br>
should be spent, is usually made by<br>
a governing body of a company that<br>
is elected by shareholders in case<br>
of a public listed companies. This<br>
governing body is known as Board of<br>
<b>Directors (BOD), and its main function</b><br>
is to oversee proper management of the<br>
company on behalf of the shareholders.<br>
<b>The BOD will usually meet regularly to</b><br>
discuss and decide on important matters,<br>
which form the basis of the company’s<br>
policies.<br>
<b>A good example of decision made by</b><br>
<b>BOD in relation to the creation of reserve</b><br>
accounts is on how the company’s profit<br>
should be utilised. BOD may decide that,<br>
a certain portion of the profit should be<br>
distributed to shareholders. This portion<br>
of a company’s profit that is distributed<br>
to shareholders is called dividends.<br>
<b>The remaining profit is considered as</b><br>
reserve that is called retained earnings.<br>
<b>Retained earnings may be designed to</b><br>
achieve different objectives including,<br>
paying future dividends in case there<br>
is a loss, or profit for the year is not<br>
enough. Generally, the company’s BOD<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
89<br>
may decide to create different types of<br>
“reserves” from the undistributed profit.<br>
<b>Notably, all these reserves and retained</b><br>
earnings belong to shareholders. This<br>
issue is reflected well in the statement<br>
of financial position by listing them as<br>
part and parcel of owners/shareholders’<br>
equity.<br>
<b>The reserve created from revenue profit</b><br>
is known as revenue reserves. Revenue<br>
reserves can either be general reserves<br>
or specific reserves. A general reserve is<br>
designed to fulfil various business needs.<br>
<b>For example, meeting contingencies,</b><br>
offsetting future losses, and enhancing<br>
the working capital of a company. On the<br>
other side, specific reserves are created for<br>
a specific purpose in a business. Specific<br>
reserves cannot be used for any other<br>
purpose apart from the aim for which<br>
they were created. Examples of specific<br>
reserves include, investment fluctuation<br>
reserves and debenture redemption<br>
reserves. Investment fluctuation reserves<br>
are aimed at adjusting the difference<br>
between the book value and market<br>
value of investment while redemption<br>
reserves are for paying long-term loans<br>
or debentures.<br>
<b>Another</b><br>
category<br>
of<br>
reserve<br>
is<br>
named as capital reserves. These are<br>
usually created from capital-oriented<br>
transactions. A good example would<br>
include gains or profit from the sale<br>
of non–current assets. These types of<br>
reserves are never used for distribution<br>
of profits in the form of dividends but<br>
to conserve it and improve business<br>
financial stability. Besides gains/profits<br>
on disposal of non-current assets, capital<br>
reserves may be raised from revaluation<br>
surpluses of non-current assets and re-<br>
issue of shares (share premium) and<br>
profits obtained prior to incorporation of<br>
a company. A share premium is obtained<br>
when the company sells its shares for the<br>
price that is higher than the price stated<br>
in a share. The price stated in a share<br>
certificate or paper value is also known<br>
as par value or face value of a company’s<br>
shares.<br>
<b>Consider that, a company has a total</b><br>
of 1,000,000 shares, and each share is<br>
valued at TZS 500 and that company<br>
intends to sell its shares to the public<br>
to raise the needed capital. If a share<br>
is sold at exactly TZS 500, then there<br>
is no premium. However, if a share is<br>
sold, say at TZS 510, then there is a<br>
premium of TZS 10 for a share sold.<br>
<b>The company may then open an account</b><br>
called premium account to keep record<br>
of collected funds. Assuming that,<br>
all the shares are successful sold at<br>
premium, then the total premium will be<br>
<b>TZS 10,000,000 (TZS 10 X 1,000,000</b><br>
shares). This premium is a good example<br>
of capital reserves, which may be used<br>
to pay for different expenses incurred for<br>
selling shares to the public. It cannot be<br>
distributed as dividends to shareholders.<br>

financial statements<br>

<b>The nature and classification of provisions/allowances</b><br>
A provision/allowance refers to any funds set aside from a company’s profit for the<br>
probable future expenses or a reduction in the asset value, although the exact amount<br>
is unknown at present. Basically, an entity has to recognise or make a provision, if<br>
it is probable that, an outflow of cash or other economic resources will be required<br>
to settle the expenses, liability, replenishment of assets or adjusting of loss in the<br>
future. If an outflow is not probable, accounting practice is to treat the liability<br>
as a contingent. Contingent liabilities’ existence is out of the entity’s control. An<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
92<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
example of a contingent liability is when<br>
there is a legal proceeding in the court<br>
of law against the business, and that the<br>
ruling may compel the business to settle<br>
a liability.<br>
<b>A provision is a liability of uncertain</b><br>
timing or amount. In order for a provision<br>
to be recognised or recorded in books of<br>
accounts, the following conditions must<br>
be fulfilled: There must be a present<br>
obligation as a result of past events.<br>
<b>This obligation will probably result into</b><br>
outflow of resources and the obligation<br>
can be reliably estimated. For example,<br>
if the business wants to make a provision<br>
for bad debts, there must be some debts<br>
which are likely not to be collected by the<br>
business. If these debts are not collected,<br>
they will result into loss of money due<br>
from debtors. Finally, the expected loss<br>
in terms of bad debts must be estimated.<br>
<b>Essentially a provision is an amount</b><br>
charged against revenue as an expense.<br>
<b>A provision normally results into a</b><br>
reduction in the value of either an<br>
asset or a known liability. Common<br>
examples are provisions for audit fees,<br>
tax payables, depreciation, bad/doubtful<br>
debts and discounts allowed.<br>

<b>Doing business with customers on credit</b><br>
is normally associated with default risk<br>
– this result into bad debts, which is<br>
also known as irrecoverable debts. They<br>
are specific debts, which the business<br>
has all the reasons to believe that they<br>
are not going to be paid. For that matter<br>
they have to be written off in books of<br>
accounts as a loss. Possible reasons for<br>
debtors’ complete failure to pay off their<br>
debts are death, insolvency, bankruptcy<br>
and other business financial difficulties.<br>
<b>In some instances, a debt that was</b><br>
previously written off may be recovered.<br>
<b>In such circumstances, the recovered</b><br>
bad debts are recognised as an income in<br>
the statement of profit or loss. Therefore,<br>
accounting entries for bad debt written<br>
off is that it has to be debited to the<br>
statement of profit or loss and credited<br>
to the individual debtors’ accounts in the<br>
subsidiary ledger. The total bad debts are<br>
credited to the trade receivables account<br>
in the general ledger. If a bad debt is<br>
subsequently recovered, it is debited<br>
to cash/bank account and credited to<br>
an account called bad debt recovered<br>
account. Thereafter, it will be closed by<br>
transferring it as income to the statement<br>
of income.<br>

<b>Provision/allowance for doubtful debts</b><br>
When preparing financial statements, there are various accounting principles one<br>
is required to comply with including, the conservatism/prudence concept. This<br>
requires that, the amount of expected losses need to be recognised or recorded in<br>
the books of accounts but not the anticipated income. Based on this, all debts which<br>
are expected not to be paid (i.e., doubtful debts) will need to be recognised in the<br>
statement of profit or loss and then deducted in the trade receivables in the statement<br>
of financial position. Thus, to avoid overstating the value of assets that is required to<br>
be reported at its net realisable value, an amount of doubtful debts is to be estimated.<br>
However, it is practically impossible to determine accurately the actual amount of<br>
the trade debtors who will possibly turn out to be bad in the future. Drawing from<br>
business experience of dealing with debtors, one may be able to estimate the amount<br>
of total debt that will be paid in full or only part or those who will default.<br>
Common sense indicate that, the longer a debtor stays without settling his/her debts,<br>
the more likely that, the debt will turn out to be bad. Therefore, in order to obtain<br>
the amount of provision for doubtful debts, an analysis can be made on individual<br>
debtors against the age of their debts. From the analysis, one can decide on the<br>
probability of becoming bad. This procedure seems appropriate for businesses with<br>
very few debtors. Another way of estimating the amount for provision for doubtful<br>
debts is through experience, where, an estimation is made on the percentage of<br>
the total amount of debtors who will ultimately prove to be bad debts. This can<br>
be achieved by drawing up an ageing schedule, showing for how long the debts<br>
have been outstanding. The guiding principle is that, due to their higher chances of<br>
default, the older debtors should be associated with higher percentage estimates of<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
95<br>
bad debts. Based on this, the following table (known as debtors ageing schedule)<br>
indicates the estimates for provision of doubtful debts.<br>

Alternatively, the amount for the provision of doubtful debts can be computed<br>
as an estimated percentage of the total trade receivables account. This simplified<br>
approach is the one that is commonly preferred by many organisations compared to<br>
the detailed process associated with debtors ageing schedule. In most cases the rate<br>
or percentage is determined using judgement or business experiences or industry<br>
standards.<br>
Creation, increasing and decreasing of provisions for bad debts<br>
The provision/allowance for doubtful debts account shows the total allowance for<br>
accounts receivable that are expected to be written off. When provision for doubtful<br>
debts is to be made for the first time, the whole amount of provision is treated as an<br>
expense for that financial year. In case there is a portion of the trade receivable which<br>
has been proved to be a bad debt, it has to be deducted first from the trade receivables<br>
balance before the amount of the provision for doubtful debts is determined.<br>
The situation will be different when there is already a provision for doubtful debts<br>
that was created in the previous financial period. In case the provision for doubtful<br>
debts for the current financial period is greater than the existing balance, it is<br>
considered that there is an increase in provision for doubtful debts. Therefore, the<br>
increased amount (the excess amount) is charged as an expense in the statement<br>
of profit or loss for the period in which the increased provision is made. To the<br>
contrary, when there is a decrease in provision for doubtful debts, the amount of the<br>
decrease in provision is added back as a gain to the statement of profit or loss for the<br>
period in which the decrease in provision is made.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
96<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Accounting entries for provision for doubtful debts</b><br>
Accounting entries are usually made to record creation, an increase or decrease<br>
in provision for doubtful debts. For a creation of a provision for doubtful debts,<br>
statement of profit or loss account is debited (i.e., it adds the amount of operating<br>
expenses) and a provision for doubtful debts account is credited. In subsequent<br>
years, the provision for doubtful debts may increase or decrease, depending on the<br>
current year’s estimations for debts that are considered doubtful. For an increase<br>
in provision, the existing provision for doubtful debts is credited and a debit entry<br>
is made in the statement of profit or loss with the amount of increase. In case of<br>
a decrease in provision for doubtful debts, the accounting entries will be; debit<br>
the provision for doubtful debts account and credit the statement of profit or loss<br>
with the amount of decrease. In the statement of financial position, the balance in<br>
the provision for doubtful debts will be deducted from the total trade receivables<br>
account.<br>
<b>Example 4.3</b><br>
Mahenge Sanga is a trader dealing with consumables. He sells goods on cash and<br>

<b>Exercise 4.2</b><br>
Mrs Krishna Patel is a wholesaler. Patel is worried about an increase in default of<br>
her trade receivables in the future. Following a prudent principle, Mrs Patel de­<br>
cides to make a provision for trade receivables equivalent to 2% of outstanding<br>
trade receivables at the reporting date from 30th September 2020. On 30th Sep­<br>
tember, 2021, Patel determines that, the previous provision for bad debts has been<br>
overestimated and decides to recalculate it to be the equivalent of 1% of outstand­<br>
ing trade receivables. The balances of trade accounts receivables balances at the<br>
end of various accounting periods are as follows.<br>
<b>Required</b><br>
<b>Show the following:</b><br>
(a)	 Journal entries for three years to record transactions for the provision for<br>
doubtful debts.<br>
(b)	 For each of the three years above, prepare the provision for doubtful debts<br>
account, statement of profit or loss account (extracts) and the statement of<br>
financial position (extracts) as at the end of each year.<br>
<b>Provision/allowance for discount on debtors</b><br>
In practice, business enterprises allow cash discounts to their customers as a means<br>
of encouraging them to pay their debts on time. The discount period may spill over<br>
into the following accounting period for the sales made during the current year.<br>
Since it is not certain that the debtor will pay the business on time, it is also prudent<br>
to provide for possible loss in terms of discounts allowed to debtors who will pay the<br>
business within the prescribed discount period. Accounting treatment for provision<br>
for discount on trade receivables/debtors is treated in the same way as provision for<br>
doubtful debts. Their difference is on the computation of provision for discounts on<br>
debtors which is based on the “good debtors”; that is, after deducting the provision<br>
for doubtful debts.<br>
Provision for discount on debtors is created by debiting the statement of profit or<br>
loss and crediting the provision for a discount on debtors account. Any increase<br>
or decrease in provision is adjusted through the provision for discount on debtors.<br>
In the statement of financial position, the balance of the provision for discount on<br>
<b>Dates</b><br>
<b>Sept. 30, 2020</b><br>
<b>Sept. 30, 2021</b><br>
<b>Sept. 30, 2022</b><br>
<b>TZS</b><br>
30,400,000<br>
34,200,000<br>
42,800,000<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>

<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
debtors account is deducted from the trade receivables figure after deducting the<br>
amount for provision for doubtful debts. Finally, the amount for discount allowed<br>
can be posted directly to statement of profit or loss as an expense; or can be debited<br>
to the provision for discount allowed.<br>
<b>Example 4.4</b><br>
The provision for discount on debtors’ accounts showed a credit balance of TZS<br>

During 2021, the discounts allowed were TZS 400,000 and sundry debtors were<br>
TZS 30,000,000 on 31st December 2021. The same percentage of provision for<br>
doubtful debts and discounts was maintained.<br>

1,500,000<br>
<b>Good debtors</b><br>
28,500,000<br>

28,500,000<br>
(Multiplied by 2)<br>
x 2<br>
712,500<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Five</b><br>
<b>Depreciation and</b><br>
disposal of non-<br>
current assets<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter you will learn about the concepts of depreciation and disposal of<br>
non -current assets and how the two relate to each other. You will also learn the<br>
different principles and methods used to determine depreciation and its recording<br>
in the books of accounts. The competencies developed will enable you to apply<br>
relevant principles of depreciation and disposal of non-current assets to determine<br>
the true and fair view of business performance and its financial position.<br>
<b>Nature and types of non-current assets</b><br>
<b>A non-current asset is an asset whose</b><br>
useful life is expected to last longer than<br>
one accounting period (at least a year).<br>
<b>It is a resource an organization acquires</b><br>
with the aim of using in its business<br>
operations for a long term period, not<br>
available for resale. In summary, the key<br>
features of a non-current asset (fixed<br>
asset) are as follows:<br>
(a)	It is held for use in the production<br>
or supply of goods or services<br>
or for rental to others or for<br>
administrative usage,<br>
(b)	It is expected to be used for more<br>
than one accounting period/year.<br>
<b>Non–current assets can have either</b><br>
physical or non–physical presence. As<br>
a result, we have tangible non-current<br>
assets and intangible non-current assets.<br>
<b>Tangible assets as the ones that can be</b><br>
vividly seen, touched or felt. In contrast,<br>
intangible assets  means that, they cannot<br>
be seen, touched or felt. Tangible non-<br>
current assets including land, building,<br>
machinery, vehicles computers and<br>
other equipment are commonly known<br>
as property plant and equipment (PPE).<br>
<b>Examples of intangible assets include,</b><br>
goodwill,<br>
patents,<br>
trademarks<br>
and<br>
copyrights.<br>
<b>The third category of non-current</b><br>
assets are termed as natural resources.<br>
<b>These include resources that occur</b><br>
naturally, and are derived from the<br>
earth. Examples of natural resources<br>
include timber, fossil fuels, oil fields<br>
and minerals. Natural resources are also<br>
known as wasting assets since once<br>
removed from the ground or physically<br>
consumed they can not be replaced. As a<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
106<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
matter of illustration, reference is made<br>
by focusing on natural gas to show how<br>
natural resources can be recorded in the<br>
books of accounts. This type of non-<br>
current asset must be mined or pumped<br>
out of the ground for it to be used as<br>
source of energy or fuel. The value of<br>
natural gas is usually recorded on the<br>
balance sheet at the cost of acquisition<br>
plus exploration and development costs,<br>
less accumulated depletion. Depletion<br>
is a process of allocating the cost of<br>
extracting natural resources until their<br>
exhaustion similar to depreciation a used<br>
in accounting for PPE. As for  intangible<br>
assets,<br>
the<br>
allocation/charging<br>
of<br>
expenses over their useful time is called<br>
amortization.<br>
<b>In this book however, we will focus more</b><br>
on the meaning and use of depreciation<br>
to account for PPE, how it effect their<br>
valuation and relevant entries in the<br>
books of accounts until their disposal.<br>
<b>Valuation of non-current assets</b><br>
<b>For accounting treatment, any expenditure</b><br>
associated with the acquisition of non-<br>
current assets is categorized as capital<br>
expenditure. Remember, a non-current<br>
asset can be acquired either through<br>
purchasing of a ready-made asset or<br>
through self-construction. Examples of<br>
expenditure incurred for acquisition of<br>
non-current assets include payments for<br>
purchasing land, ready made building,<br>
and furniture and equipment. It also<br>
includes payment for acquisition of<br>
intangible assets like goodwill, patents<br>
and trademarks. A good example of the<br>
self-constructed asset is construction of<br>
an office building or a showroom using<br>
own resources. Furthermore, all costs<br>
incurred in bringing a non-current asset to<br>
a point it is ready for use are capitalised;<br>
they are considered to be part of the cost<br>
of an asset. Examples of such expenses,<br>
in addition to the price paid, include<br>
transport/inward cost, fees paid to a<br>
lawyer in relation to asset acquisition and<br>
asset installation costs.<br>
<b>In the books of accounts, the valuation</b><br>
of non-current asset is done initially<br>
at cost and subsequently, using cost or<br>
revaluation model. Cost is the amount<br>
of cash or cash equivalents paid or the<br>
fair value of the other consideration<br>
given to acquire an asset at the time of<br>
its acquisition or construction. Where<br>
applicable, it is the amount attributed<br>
to that asset when initially recognised<br>
in<br>
accordance<br>
with<br>
the<br>
specific<br>
requirements of the standards for the<br>
determination of the cost of the asset. The<br>
following example provides alternative<br>
costing approaches for recognition of an<br>
asset in the books of accounts:<br>
(a)	Cost as the amount paid to acquire<br>
the asset; a firm pays TZS 500,000<br>
to buy a table, or<br>
(b)	The value of anything else given<br>
in exchange for the asset; a firm<br>
gives out a computer with the fair<br>
value of TZS 1,500,000 in order<br>
to get a table, or<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
107<br>
(c)	The valuation method given by<br>
other standards for something<br>
given in exchange for the asset;<br>
a firm gives out a share options<br>
worth TZS 900,000 to get a table<br>
in return.<br>
<b>Since tangible non-current assets are</b><br>
normally used for more than one year, an<br>
entity may incur subsequent cost in the<br>
life span of an asset apart from the initial<br>
acquisition cost. In this case, the costs of<br>
an asset are usually categorised into two<br>
types, the initial costs and subsequent<br>
costs. Initial costs are those which are<br>
incurred during acquisition of an asset<br>
for the first time while subsequent costs<br>
are those incurred to maintain the asset<br>
or to renovate or upgrade it.<br>
<b>Initial costs and accounting for</b><br>
acquisition of non – current assets<br>
<b>These are the costs incurred initially for</b><br>
bringing the asset to the location and in<br>
the condition necessary for its intended<br>
use. These include acquisition costs,<br>
construction costs, and erection costs of<br>
the non-current asset. Non-current assets<br>
such as property, plant and equipment<br>
qualifying for recognition as an asset are<br>
initially measured at their acquisition<br>
cost. In establishing initial costs of a<br>
non-current asset, the following costs<br>
are included:<br>
(a)	The purchase price.<br>
(b)	Costs directly attributable to<br>
bringing the asset to its location<br>
and in the condition so as to make<br>
it available for its intended use,<br>
(c)	Initial estimate of the costs of<br>
dismantling and removing the<br>
item and restoring the site on<br>
which it is located.<br>
<b>In contrast, the following costs are NOT</b><br>
included in the definition of cost of non-<br>
current asset as defined above:<br>
(a)	Administration<br>
and<br>
general<br>
overhead costs; such as office<br>
rent.<br>
(b)	Costs of launching a new product<br>
or service; such as advertising.<br>
(c)	Expenses on opening a new<br>
business facility or conducting<br>
business at a new place e.g.,<br>
expenses of an inaugural function.<br>
(d)	Costs of relocating e.g., costs of<br>
shifting a factory following a<br>
government order.<br>
(e)	Initial losses when the asset<br>
operates at a low capacity. For<br>
instance, a plant has a capacity of<br>
1,000MW, but initially it operates<br>
only at 400MW capacity. If at that<br>
operating capacity the generated<br>
gross profit is not sufficient to<br>
cover fixed costs the loss is not<br>
included.<br>
(f)	 Costs of incidental operations not<br>
necessarily related with bringing<br>
the asset to its required location<br>
and condition. In this case, the<br>
asset is capable of operating in the<br>
manner intended by management<br>
even without such operations.<br>

<b>Measurement of cost after initial</b><br>
recognition of assets<br>
<b>After initial recognition of an asset,</b><br>
an entity can choose to recognise non-<br>
current assets using either the cost model<br>
or the revaluation model depending on its<br>
accounting valuation policy. Whichever<br>
valuation model that will be chosen,<br>
it shall be applied to an entire class of<br>
property, plant and equipment.<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Cost model</b><br>
<b>In this model, after initial recognition of a</b><br>
non-current asset items such as property,<br>
plant and equipment, that asset is carried<br>
at its initial cost less any accumulated<br>
depreciation<br>
and<br>
any<br>
accumulated<br>
impairment<br>
losses.<br>
<b>Accumulated</b><br>
depreciation means that, the amount<br>
is charged for the use of an asset in an<br>
ordinary operating environment since its<br>
acquisition to a point where the valuation<br>
takes place. An impairment loss refers to<br>
a significant fall in the value of an asset<br>
due to reasons other than ordinary usage<br>
of such assets. Such reasons may include<br>
but not limited to time passage, physical<br>
damage of an asset, and technological<br>
changes.<br>
(iii)	 Revaluation model<br>
<b>In this model, after recognition of an asset</b><br>
for the first time, an item of property,<br>
plant and equipment whose fair value<br>
can be measured reliably is required to<br>
be carried at a re-valued amount. Such<br>
a re-valued amount is the fair value at<br>
the date of the revaluation less any<br>
subsequent accumulated depreciation<br>
and subsequent accumulated impairment<br>
losses. It is important that revaluation is<br>
done regularly to ensure that the carrying<br>
amount does not differ materially from<br>
the value that would be determined using<br>
fair value at the end of the reporting<br>
period. The carrying value is measured<br>
as the original cost of the asset, minus<br>
accumulated depreciation, amortization<br>
or impairment costs made against the<br>
asset<br>
<b>When an entity uses revaluation model,</b><br>
the intention is to establish the fair value<br>
of an asset. The fair value of an asset is<br>
found if there is a market-based evidence<br>
from<br>
registered<br>
property<br>
valuers.<br>
<b>Alternatively, it can also be determined</b><br>
by using income or a depreciated<br>
replacement cost approach. The income<br>
approach considers the present value<br>
of the future economic benefits or cash<br>
flows from the use of an asset while<br>
depreciated replacement cost refers to<br>
the cost required to acquire similar asset<br>
at the date of valuation less accumulated<br>
depreciation charges.<br>
<b>If it happens that, the carrying value</b><br>
of an asset increases as a result of<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
110<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
a revaluation, the increment is recognised in the income statement as other<br>
comprehensive income and it is accumulated in the equity under the heading of<br>
revaluation surplus. The increase shall be recognised in the profit or loss statement<br>
to the extent that it reverses a revaluation decrease of the same asset previously<br>
recognised in the profit or loss. However, if an asset’s carrying amount is decreased<br>
as a result of a revaluation, the decrease shall be recognised in the profit or loss<br>
statement. The decrease is recognised in the part of other comprehensive income to<br>
the extent of any credit balance existing in the revaluation surplus associated with<br>
that asset. It is meant to reduce the amount accumulated in equity under the heading<br>
of revaluation surplus.<br>

<b>Nature and objectives of depreciation</b><br>
<b>Nature of depreciation</b><br>
Generally, depreciation refers to the actual decrease of the fair value of an asset. For<br>
example, value of a machine or equipment may decrease each year as it is used in<br>
generating income or due to different reasons such as wear and tear. The concept<br>
extends to include the process of allocating a share of the costs of non-current assets<br>
in each accounting period to reflect the use of an asset in generating revenue in<br>
line with the matching concept. Allocation of an asset’s depreciable is usually done<br>
very systematically over its useful life. Therefore, the depreciable amount is the<br>
cost of an asset or other amount substituted for cost less its residual or scrap value.<br>
For example, if the machinery cost is TZS 78,000,000 and its residual/scrap value<br>
is TZS 6,000,000, then the depreciable cost is TZS 72,000,000 (i.e., 78,000,000-<br>
6,000,000).<br>
Determination of depreciation will start when the assets is made available for use<br>
i.e., it is in the location and condition necessary to be used/operated in the manner<br>
intended by management. Once the depreciable amount is established, it has to be<br>
allocated on a systematic basis over its useful life. Along with this, the residual value<br>
and the useful life of an asset shall be reviewed at least at each financial year-end,<br>
and if expectations differ from previous estimates, the change(s) shall be accounted<br>
for as a change in an accounting estimate. By definition, the useful life of an asset<br>
is the period over which an asset is expected to be available for use by an entity or<br>
the number of production or similar units expected to be obtained from the asset by<br>
an entity. Based on this, the useful life can be considered either in terms of number<br>
of years or in terms of number of units expected to be produced by the asset. On<br>
the other hand, the residual value of an asset is the estimated amount that an entity<br>
would currently obtain from disposal of the asset, after deducting the estimated<br>
costs of disposal, when the asset is at the age and in the condition expected at the<br>
end of its useful life.<br>
When charging depreciation on PPE, each part of these items is charged separately<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
113<br>
provided that it has significant cost in relation to the total cost of the items. The<br>
allocation of depreciation on an asset will continue as long as an asset is in use. It<br>
does not cease even if the asset becomes idle or is retired from active use unless the<br>
asset is fully depreciated. Recognition of depreciation continues even when the fair<br>
value of the asset exceeds its carrying amount, provided that the residual value does<br>
not exceed its carrying value. Similarly, repair and maintenance of an asset do not<br>
negate the need to depreciate it. However, depreciation charge can be zero when an<br>
organisation charges depreciation based on number of units produced, particularly<br>
when there is no production.<br>
If it happens the residual value of an asset increases to an amount equal to or greater<br>
than the asset’s carrying amount, the asset’s depreciation charge is zero till when<br>
its residual value subsequently decreases to an amount less than asset’s carrying<br>
amount. Depreciation charge can cease, but only when an asset is withdrawn from<br>
ordinary usage and classified differently (held for sale). It ceases at the date that the<br>
asset is classified as held for sale (or included in a disposal group that is classified<br>
as held for sale) and the date that the asset is derecognised, i.e., removed from the<br>
entity’s statement of financial position.<br>
<b>Example 5. 4</b><br>
Determine whether depreciation will be charged or not in the following scenarios:<br>
1.	 The carrying value of the machine is TZS 40,000,000 and its residual value is<br>
TZS 20,0000,000. Its fair value is TZS 50,000,000.<br>
2.	 The carrying value of the machine is TZS 20,000,000 and its residual value TZS<br>
21,000,000.<br>
<b>Solution 5.4</b><br>
1.	 In this scenario, depreciation will be charged because the carrying value exceeds<br>
the residual value i.e., TZS 40,000,000 &gt; TZS 20,000,000, despite the fact that,<br>
the fair value of TZS 50,000,0000 exceeds the carrying value, TZS 40,000,000.<br>
2.	 In this case, the depreciation charge will be zero because the residual value of<br>
TZS 21,000,000 &gt; the carrying value TZS 20,000,000.<br>

<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>

<b>Activity 5.1</b><br>
<b>Mkaka</b><br>
<b>Enterprises</b><br>
<b>Limited</b><br>
has<br>
equipment which is in working<br>
condition with valuation of TZS<br>
120,000,000. The equipment was<br>
classified as held for sale on 2nd April,<br>
2022. The financial statement of the<br>
company ends on 31st December,<br>
2022.<br>
<b>Required</b><br>
<b>Briefly explain if this equipment will be</b><br>
recognized in the financial statements<br>
and subsequently depreciated.<br>
<b>Objective of charging depreciation on</b><br>
non-current assets<br>
<b>Businesses hold non-current assets</b><br>
in order to support the generation of<br>
revenue. For instance, a manufacturing<br>
firm may need to construct or rent<br>
a factory building in order to fix<br>
manufacturing machines for production.<br>
<b>Producers maintain warehouses to store</b><br>
finished goods ready for resale. In the<br>
course of production or storage, these<br>
assets lose value. Thus, it may be useful<br>
to think of depreciation as a charge of<br>
the cost of those items which improve<br>
the revenue-generating capacity against<br>
the profit of the business. Non-current<br>
assets are acquired at a cost. However,<br>
it would be incorrect to charge the whole<br>
cost in the first year of purchase. The<br>
asset is going to help generate income<br>
for many years. The matching principle<br>
in accounting requires that the expenses<br>
be recognised in the year in which the<br>
revenue is earned (cost is matched with<br>
the relevant revenue). If the revenue<br>
from an asset is going to be earned over<br>
a number of years, then its cost should<br>
also be allocated over the same number<br>
of years. Hence, it is logical to charge<br>
the cost of an asset to revenue over the<br>
useful life of the asset. Physical wear<br>
and tear and obsolescence also result in<br>
the depreciating value of an asset.<br>
<b>As it is for other costs such as material</b><br>
and labour, the depreciation on non-<br>
current assets is meant to match the use<br>
of an asset with the sales revenue. Thus,<br>
the purpose is not to record the decrease<br>
in the value of an asset as it would occur<br>
from the adjustment of revaluations.<br>
<b>Also, the purpose of depreciation is not</b><br>
to set aside some funds to replace the<br>
asset at a later stage. Instead, it is a fact<br>
that indirectly, the profits are reduced by<br>
the depreciation amount.<br>
<b>Causes of depreciation</b><br>
<b>Depreciation is caused by various</b><br>
factors which give rise to a reduction in<br>
the value of a tangible non-current asset.<br>
<b>These include physical deterioration,</b><br>
economic factors, time and depletion.<br>
<b>Physical deterioration</b><br>
<b>The physical deterioration of a tangible</b><br>
non-current asset may be due to wear<br>
and tear and erosion, rust or decay. Wear<br>
and tear are very common in tangible<br>
non-current assets. When assets such as<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
115<br>
motor vehicle, machinery, fixtures and<br>
fittings are used, their physical conditions<br>
deteriorate, hence they wear out. Metals<br>
in motor vehicles or machinery will<br>
rust away while wood will eventually<br>
rot. Decaying of an asset can be due<br>
to elements of nature or the lack of<br>
proper attention. Therefore, some of the<br>
tangible non-current assets last many<br>
years, while others last for only a few<br>
years. The same applies to buildings, of<br>
which some are lasting for a longer time.<br>
<b>For the case of land, it may be eroded or</b><br>
wasted away by the action of wind, rain,<br>
sun and other elements of nature.<br>
<b>Deterioration due to economic factors</b><br>
<b>Depreciation is caused by economic</b><br>
factors<br>
when<br>
a<br>
non-current<br>
asset<br>
owned by a business being put out of<br>
use even though its physical condition<br>
is still good. The two main economic<br>
factors are usually obsolescence and<br>
inadequacy. Obsolescence occurs when<br>
an asset is put out of use because it is<br>
out of date (outdated). For instance, the<br>
development in science and technology<br>
caused significant evolution in the<br>
electronic devices used in the commercial<br>
music industry. The usefulness life of<br>
such equipment becomes so short not<br>
because of physical deterioration. For<br>
instance, the moment a new version of<br>
music instrument is released the old<br>
equipment becomes obsolete, hence<br>
taken out of use by musicians. The other<br>
economic factor causing depreciation<br>
is inadequacy, which arises due to<br>
the asset’s low capacity compared to<br>
prevailing economic demands. This<br>
happens when an asset is no longer used<br>
because its capacity cannot cope with<br>
the growth and changes in the size of<br>
the business. The replacement of the old<br>
<b>Wami bridge with a larger and stronger</b><br>
bridge to cope with increasing vehicle<br>
traffic is an example of depreciation of the<br>
old bridge due to inadequacy. The same<br>
example applies with the replacement of<br>
the old ferry/boat at Kigamboni due to<br>
increasing passengers in the area.<br>
<b>Time passage</b><br>
<b>Time is a factor that causes depreciation</b><br>
of non-current assets, particularly when<br>
its usage access is time bound. Even in<br>
the case of wear and tear, erosion, etc.,<br>
and for obsolescence and inadequacy<br>
to take place, time is needed. However,<br>
there are non-current assets whose usage<br>
is connected with the time in a different<br>
way. It includes the right to use assets<br>
which have a legal life fixed in terms of<br>
years. Once the years elapse, such assets<br>
are considered to have been depreciated.<br>
<b>The term amortization is applied instead</b><br>
of depreciation in those assets whose<br>
usage is connected with time.<br>

<b>Methods for estimation and accounting for depreciation</b><br>
There are various methods that can be adopted to charge depreciation of an asset.<br>
These methods include, but not limited to, the straight-line method, the diminishing<br>
balance method and the units of production method. However, the choice of a<br>
depreciation method will depend on the pattern in which an asset is used or it is<br>
expected to be used to generate future economic benefits over its useful life. Once<br>
a certain method is opted it has to be applied consistently from time to time, unless<br>
there is a change in the expected pattern of those future economic benefits.<br>
<b>Straight-line Method</b><br>
Straight-line method of depreciation is the method which results into a constant<br>
depreciation charge over the useful life provided that residual value of an asset does<br>
not change. It is calculated as a fixed amount or fixed percentage of the depreciable<br>
value of the asset. The computation formula is as follows.<br>
<b>Example 5.5</b><br>

<b>Diminishing or reducing balance method</b><br>
The diminishing balance method is an approach which charges different<br>
amount of depreciation in every year. The amount is decreasing from year to<br>
year over the useful life of an asset. It is calculated as a percentage of the written<br>
down or book value of the asset i.e., cost minus accumulated depreciation. The<br>
method is also known as the reducing balance method.<br>
Depreciation = (Cost – Accumulated depreciation) x Depreciation rate<br>

<b>The units of production method</b><br>
In the units of production method, the depreciation charge depends on the units of<br>
output; it charges depreciation based on the expected use of an asset or output. The<br>
method is appropriate when the economic benefits derived from the assets are<br>
proportional with the units produced and the pattern of production is not uniform<br>
from time to time. Therefore, the depreciation charge is reflecting the use of an asset<br>
to produce a certain level of output. The cost of the non-current asset is allocated<br>
proportionally to the production achieved, as shown in following formula:<br>

<b>Sum of the years’ digits</b><br>
The sum of years’ digits method depreciates the non-current assets at a faster rate<br>
than the straight-line method, but less than the reducing balance method. It charges<br>
higher depreciation expense in the early years of an asset’s useful life and declines<br>
from year to year. It is most suitable when the value of an asset declines much in<br>
the early years of its useful life. The calculation of the depreciation is obtained by<br>
dividing the remaining useful life of an asset by the sum of the years of the expected<br>
useful life. Thereafter, it is multiplied by the depreciation base to determine the<br>
depreciation expense.<br>
<b>Example 5.8</b><br>

use for 5 years. The calculations of the depreciation over the five years using sum of<br>
