<b>Revaluation method</b><br>
Previous methods of calculating depreciation assume fewer and high value non-<br>
current assets. With fewer non-current assets, it is not difficult to write up the<br>
necessary accounts for depreciation for each item. However, some businesses,<br>
because of their nature and the kind of activities they carry out, maintain low-cost<br>
non-current assets. Engineering and garage businesses for instance, have a lot of<br>
spanners, screw drivers, small tools and other low-cost non-current assets. Similarly,<br>
hospitals and medical laboratories have glass items and smaller instruments such as<br>
test tubes and glasses. With these non-current assets, depreciation charges can better<br>
be determined using the revaluation method. The principle applied is to revalue<br>
these items at the beginning and end of the financial year. The difference is the<br>
depreciation to be charged in the statement of income.<br>
<b>Example 5.9</b><br>
Tibakanya Timber Ltd which operate in Kasenyi deals with production of timber<br>
and resale. It maintains a number of small tools including chain-saws and screw<br>
drivers to facilitate production of timber. The following information relates to these<br>
items as at 31st Dec. 2021<br>

<b>Depreciation provisions and the replacement of assets</b><br>
There is a widespread misconception that making a provision for depreciation means<br>
that, money is invested somewhere to finance the replacement of the asset when it<br>
is put out of use. Depreciation is simply a bookkeeping entry, and the end result<br>
is that, it lowers the net profits as the provisions have been charged to the income<br>
statement. Nevertheless, it is not surprising to find misconception regarding this<br>
among many people including, accounting students, teachers and accountants. They<br>
often think that, a provision is the same as money kept or invested somewhere with<br>
which to replace the asset eventually. Never make that mistake – in fact businesses<br>
do not do so in practice. Usually, businesses use the money to finance working<br>
capital to generate higher returns than keeping it. Cash can better be used in terms<br>
of re-investing in banks or other securities to generate more wealth for the owners.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
125<br>
Besides, with the availability of long-term financing from banks and other financial<br>
markets, it has become practically easier for businesses to borrow funds for buying<br>
non-current assets at relatively lower cost and at short notice. This has made the<br>
issue of maintaining a sinking fund expensive, outdated and unnecessary. A cautious<br>
owner may take out less drawings if the net profit is lower, but that is no justification<br>
for arguing that, depreciation results in funds being available to replace the fixed<br>
asset later on!<br>

<b>Recording of depreciation in books of accounts</b><br>
When preparing financial statements, especially the statement of profit or loss, the<br>
matching principle has to be adhered to. That is, income earned has to be matched<br>
with the expenses incurred to generate that income. Depreciation is part of these<br>
expenses which represent part of the cost of non-current asset that has been used in<br>
generating income.<br>
After computing the depreciation charges for the period, it has to be recorded in books<br>
of accounts. Normally, a separate accumulated depreciation account is prepared for<br>
each group of non-current asset. This account is credited with the annual depreciation<br>
charges and debited to the specific non-current asset depreciation expense account.<br>
Depreciation expenses are then transferred to a statement of profit of loss as<br>
expenses. The balance of the accumulated depreciation for each non-current asset<br>
(which always has a credit balance) is then deducted from the corresponding cost or<br>
revalued amount of a non-current asset in the statement of financial position.<br>
The following illustration shows how depreciation is recorded in books of accounts<br>
and its effect in the income statement and in the statement of financial position.<br>

<b>Example 5.10</b><br>
Mr. Mwakangale is in his farming business. On 1st April 2016 he purchased a<br>
Valmet tractor for TZS 80,000,000 and later on he bought a Massey Ferguson tractor<br>
for TZS 64,000,000 on 1st January 2017. The estimated useful life of the Valmet<br>
tractor is 10 years with a residual value of TZS 20,000,000 while the useful life of<br>
Massey Ferguson tractor is estimated to be 12 years with the residual value of TZS<br>
16,000,000. Mr. Mwakangale uses a straight-line method of allocating depreciation<br>
per year and prepares his financial statements annually on 31st December.<br>
<b>Required</b><br>

<b>Disposal of non-current assets</b><br>
Disposal of non-current assets refers to the process involved in derecognising the<br>
non-current assets from the books of accounts. When non-current assets are acquired,<br>
they are recognised in the books of accounts and when they are disposed, they are<br>
derecognised from the books of accounts. The disposal can be due to winding up of<br>
the useful life of an asset or asset becoming obsolete or any other genuine reason.<br>
The following subsections discuss the nature, procedures and accounting treatment<br>
associated with the disposal of tangible non-current assets.<br>
<b>The nature of disposal of non-current assets</b><br>
A non-current asset can be disposed of at any time from when it is acquired to the<br>
end of its useful life, despite the intention to use it to the end. A disposal of non-<br>
current asset can be done through discarding, selling or exchanging with another<br>
non-current asset. From accounting perspective, the disposal of non-current assets<br>
requires eliminating non-current assets from the books of accounts. It involves<br>
passing journal entries that results into completely removing the asset together with<br>
its corresponding records from the statement of financial position. This process is<br>
known as derecognition, which is the opposite of recognition made when the asset<br>
was acquired for the first time. Derecognition of a non-current asset is also required<br>
when it is stolen or lost because of unforeseeable events like fire or theft. The overall<br>
accounting concept behind disposal is to reverse the records associated with the<br>
asset, including accumulated depreciation, in the books of accounts.<br>
Derecognition means that items of non-current assets such as property, plant and<br>
equipment which were recognised as assets of the business entity are removed from<br>
accounting records. The carrying value of a non-current asset is derecognised when<br>
the asset is disposed of or when no future economic benefits are expected from the<br>
asset. In case a non-current asset is disposed of for a consideration or price, it can<br>
result into gain or loss. The gain or loss is simply obtained by taking the difference<br>
between the net proceeds from the non-current asset and the carrying value (net<br>
book value) of an asset at the time of disposal.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>

<b>Exercise 5.5</b><br>
1.	 Identify three possible ways of disposing a non-current asset, then provide<br>
their advantages and disadvantages.<br>
2.	 Explain scenarios that may cause a non-current asset to be derecognised from<br>
the books of accounts.<br>
3.	 Explain the reasons for derecognising a disposed non-current asset.<br>
Accounting for disposal of non-current assets (fixed assets)<br>
When a non-current account is disposed of, a disposal account is opened. The<br>
account is used to record all entries related to the disposal (or sale) of an asset. The<br>
account is also used to determine profit or loss on the disposal. When the current<br>
asset is disposed through sale, the accounting treatment will involve two main steps,<br>
which are (i) removal of the existing records associated with the asset from books of<br>
accounts and (ii) calculating profit or loss from the disposal.<br>
The calculation of profit or loss from the disposal of an asset is very important<br>
because it is unlikely that, the asset will be disposed of (through sale) at the value<br>
that is equal to its carrying value. The amount of profit or loss from the disposal<br>
of an asset is included in the statement of profit or loss and other comprehensive<br>
income. Similarly, the firm is required to establish whether there is profit or loss<br>
when an asset is disposed through a part exchange deal. Part exchange deal is an<br>
agreement that facilitates the seller of a new asset to accept the old asset from the<br>
buyer as part of the payment of the new asset. For instance, the seller of the new cars<br>
or vans accepts an old asset as part of the considerations on the sale of a new car or<br>
van to the customer. In this case, organisation recognises the value of the new asset<br>
and derecognises the carrying amount of the old asset given out.<br>
Generally, there are four events an entity must record in its books of accounts in<br>
relation to the disposal of non-current assets, which include the following:<br>
(i)<br>
To eliminate the non-current asset from the books of accounts by crediting<br>
the non-current asset account. Asset disposal reduces the balance of the<br>
asset’s account.<br>
<b>Dr. Asset disposal account</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Cr. Non-current asset account</b><br>
xxx<br>
Being removal of the non-current asset from the statement of financial<br>
position<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
129<br>
(ii)	 To close the accumulated depreciation account associated with the disposed<br>
off asset by debiting it.<br>
<b>Dr. Accumulated depreciation</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Cr. Non-current asset disposal account</b><br>
xxx<br>
Being removal of accumulated depreciation from the statement of<br>
financial position<br>
(iii)	 To record receipt of any proceeds from disposal of an asset, whether in the<br>
form of cash or another asset as part exchange.<br>
<b>Dr. Cash (Asset receivable) account</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Cr. Asset disposal account</b><br>
xxx<br>
Recording cash receipt (receivable) on disposal of an asset.<br>
(iv)	 To determine and to record any gain or loss from disposal.<br>
This is done by balancing the asset disposal account, hence transferring the<br>
profit or loss to the income statement.<br>
<b>Disposal of non-current asset at the end of useful life</b><br>
Usually, non-current assets are disposed at the end of its useful life. At that point, the<br>
carrying value of an asset is its salvage value. Therefore, when the proceeds from<br>
the disposal are equal to the net book value, then there is neither gain nor loss on<br>
disposal. Meanwhile, if proceeds from the sale exceed the net book value, there is a<br>
gain on disposal and its vice versa is a loss.<br>
<b>Example 5.11</b><br>
On 1st January, 2021, the books of Sundra Co Limited had the following net book<br>

vehicle got accident. It was very unfortunate for the company that the motor vehicle<br>
was not covered by insurance. Based on company’s policy, depreciation is charged<br>
on a full year basis using straight line method.<br>
<b>Required</b><br>
Prepare asset and asset disposal accounts for recording all these events.<br>

<b>Disposal of non-current asset before the end of useful life</b><br>
A non-current asset may be disposed before the end of its useful life. When the non-<br>
current asset is sold in this scenario, it is necessary to be certain that depreciation has<br>
been provided for up to the time of sale. Usually, before recording the sale, an entry<br>
must be made to record depreciation up to the date of disposal. The depreciation<br>
figure will depend on the policy of the business, whether to record a partial or a full<br>
year’s depreciation or none at all.<br>

<b>A note on the effect of change of depreciation methods</b><br>
A company may consider changing the use of particular method of depreciation<br>
after using it for some time. The reason for this may include, a significant change in<br>
the pattern of future economic benefits from non – current asset under consideration.<br>
For example, if an asset loses much of its value early on, the business might decide<br>
to switch from using straight line method to accelerated or diminishing balance<br>
method. This change is necessary as it improve the quality of information presented<br>
in financial statements with regard to depreciation charges. However, in line with<br>
the convention of consistency (covered in chapter one), such change in accounting<br>
estimation would require full disclosure in the footnotes accompanying the financial<br>
statements. What is required is the justification and quantification showing financial<br>
effects of such changes. Auditors consider this as matter of importance, and normally<br>
pay close attention to any changes to see if they are justifiable. Their concerns are<br>
valid since if management are allowed to make changes as per their wishes, it would<br>
be difficult to evaluate properly the performance of business from time to time, and<br>
to make comparison with other similar businesses in the industry.<br>
This chapter has focused on depreciation and disposal of non-current asserts. It<br>
has introduced the concepts of depreciation and non-current assets. Regarding<br>
depreciation, the chapter has considered various methods for charging it and<br>
ways of treating it in the statement of financial performance and the resultant<br>
accumulated depreciation in the statement of financial position. The depreciation<br>
methods discussed in the chapter include, the straight line, reducing balance, sum<br>
of the years’ digits, units of production and revaluation. Regardless of a method<br>
adopted, the aim is to spread the cost associated with the acquisition of an asset<br>
over its useful life time in accordance with the matching concept.<br>
As for the question of non-current assets, the chapter has provided the basis for<br>
understanding and applying different principles used for valuation of non-current<br>
assets and their reporting in the financial statements. Issues like acquisition, types<br>
of disposal of non-current assets, and their accounting treatment and the resulting<br>
effect in different financial records and financial statements, are well covered. The<br>
chapter has shown that, when the asset is acquired for the first time, it is normally<br>
recognised (capitalised) in the books of accounts and when disposed off, it is<br>
usually derecognised. The derecognition of non-current assets involves passing<br>
journal entries that will ultimately result into either profit or loss on the disposal.<br>
Eventually, this will be transferred to the income statement and its accumulated<br>
depreciation in the statement of financial position.<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Six</b><br>
<b>Recognition of</b><br>
revenue<br>
and expenses<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter, you will learn the meanings of revenue and expenses, different<br>
criteria and principles used to recognize revenue and expenses as well as how to<br>
record them properly in the books of accounts. The competencies developed in<br>
this chapter will enable you to prudently recognise the correct amounts of revenue<br>
and expenses to be recorded in the books of accounts hence determining the true<br>
and fair view of business performance and its financial position.<br>
<b>Concept of revenue</b><br>
<b>Revenue is the gross inflow of economic</b><br>
benefits (cash receivables and other<br>
assets) arising in the ordinary course<br>
of business activities of an entity (such<br>
as sales of goods or services, interest,<br>
royalties and dividends). Those inflows<br>
should result in an increase in equity,<br>
other than those contributed by equity<br>
holders. One should also exclude the<br>
collections made on behalf of third<br>
parties such as sales taxes, Value Added<br>
<b>Tax (VAT) and other types of taxes on</b><br>
goods and services collected on behalf<br>
of the government. Why exclude them?<br>
<b>For the same reason, they do not account</b><br>
for the economic benefits of the business<br>
entity or increase in owners’ equity. In<br>
the context of principal – agent business<br>
relationship, the same logic applies.<br>
<b>The agent’s gross inflows of economic</b><br>
benefits include the amount collected on<br>
behalf of the principal, which do not add<br>
value to the agent’s equity. Therefore,<br>
the amount collected on behalf of the<br>
principal should not be considered as<br>
revenue by the agent. However, the<br>
commission received by a person as a<br>
result of fulfilling his/her responsibility<br>
as agent, should be recognised as<br>
revenue.<br>
<b>The following are implications flowing</b><br>
from this definition:<br>
(i)	 Revenue should be stated before<br>
deduction of costs of sales. For<br>
example, if goods are sold for<br>
<b>TZS 100,000,000 that costed</b><br>
the seller TZS 60,000,000 to<br>
manufacture, the revenue is TZS<br>
100,000,000, not TZS 40,000,000<br>
(i.e. TZS 100,000,000 less TZS<br>
60,000,000).<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
144<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
(ii)	 Revenue is recognised when<br>
received from sale or provision<br>
of goods and services in ordinary<br>
course of business activities. If an<br>
entity, for example, disposes of its<br>
property, plant, and equipment;<br>
the proceeds on disposal are<br>
not treated as ordinary revenue.<br>
<b>Instead, the profit or loss on</b><br>
disposal will be treated as part<br>
and parcel of other income.<br>
(iii)	 Sales taxes that are collected<br>
from the customer and remitted<br>
to Tanzania Revenue Authority<br>
(TRA) are not ‘revenue’. For<br>
example, if goods are sold for<br>
<b>TZS</b><br>
100,000,000<br>
inclusive<br>
of recoverable sales taxes of<br>
10%, then the revenue is TZS<br>
90,000,000 not TZS 100,000,000.<br>
(iv)	 If the seller is acting as an agent,<br>
rather than the principal in a<br>
transaction, the revenue the seller<br>
should recognise is the amount<br>
of commission receivable rather<br>
than the gross amount collected<br>
from the customer. For example,<br>
a travel agent sells a holiday<br>
ticket to customers for TZS<br>
100,000,000 plus a commission<br>
of TZS 10,000,000. This means,<br>
the customer would be paying<br>
<b>TZS 110,000,000 and the travel</b><br>
agent remitting TZS 100,000,000<br>
to the entity providing the holiday<br>
tickets. Based on this, the travel<br>
agent would recognise revenue as<br>
<b>TZS 10,000,000.</b><br>
<b>Criteria for revenue recognition</b><br>
<b>By definition, recognition is the process</b><br>
of formally incorporating an item in the<br>
financial statements of an entity as an<br>
asset, liability, revenue or expense. An<br>
important question to address in relation<br>
to the concept of revenue recognition<br>
include, when should a business consider<br>
revenue as being earned? Generally,<br>
revenue is recognised when it is probable<br>
that, future economic benefits will flow<br>
to the entity and these benefits can be<br>
measured reliably. This chapter identifies<br>
the circumstances in which these criteria<br>
will be met and, therefore, revenue will<br>
be recognised. It also provides practical<br>
guidance on the application of these<br>
criteria.<br>
<b>Revenue is recognised only when it</b><br>
is probable that the economic benefits<br>
associated with the transaction will flow<br>
to the entity. In some cases, this may<br>
not be probable until the consideration<br>
is received or until the uncertainty<br>
is removed. For example, it may be<br>
uncertain that, a foreign government<br>
authority will grant permission to remit<br>
the consideration from a sale in a foreign<br>
country. When permission is granted,<br>
the uncertainty is removed, and revenue<br>
is recognised. However, when an<br>
uncertainty arises about the collectability<br>
of an amount already included in<br>
revenue, the uncollectible amount or the<br>
amount in respect of which recovery has<br>
ceased to be probable is recognised as<br>
an expense rather than as an adjustment<br>
of the amount of revenue originally<br>
recognised.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
145<br>
The following sales revenue categories have specific recognition criteria that must<br>
be met entirely for the revenue to be recognized in the financial statements:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>The sale of goods,</b><br>
(ii)	 The rendering of services, and<br>
(iii)	 The use of entity’s assets by others yielding interest, royalties and dividends.<br>
<b>Recognition of revenues from sale of goods</b><br>
Revenue from the sale of goods shall be recognised when all the following conditions<br>
have been satisfied as shown in figure 6.1:<br>
Figure 6. 1: Essential criteria for recognition of revenue from sale of goods<br>
As can be observed, Figure 6.1 provides the five important criteria for recognition<br>
of revenue from the sale of goods. All five conditions must be met, and according<br>
to IFRS, conditions (1) and (2) are referred to as performance. Fulfilment of the<br>
1.  Risks and rewards of ownership heve been<br>
transferred from the seller to the buyer<br>
3.  The collection of payment from goods or<br>
services is reasonably assured<br>
4.  The amount of revenue can be<br>
reasonably/reliably measured<br>
5.  Costs of revenue can be reasonably/reliably<br>
measured<br>
<b>Essential criteria/conditions for recognition of</b><br>
revenue from the sale of goods<br>
2.  The seller loses control over the goods sold<br>

performance obligations occurs when<br>
the seller has done what is expected<br>
to deserve payment. Condition (3) is<br>
referred to as collectability. In this,<br>
the seller must have a reasonable<br>
expectation that he/she will be paid for<br>
the performance. Conditions (4) and (5)<br>
are referred to as measurability. This is<br>
aligned well with the matching principle;<br>
the seller must be able to match the<br>
revenues to the expenses. Hence, both<br>
revenues and expenses should be able to<br>
be reasonably measured.<br>
<b>Understanding of the five criteria</b><br>
presented in figure 6.1 can further be<br>
enhanced, as explained in the following<br>
parts:<br>
1.	 Risks and rewards of ownership<br>
have been transferred from the<br>
seller to the buyer: the seller is seen<br>
as having transferred to the buyer<br>
significant amount of risks and<br>
rewards of ownership of the goods<br>
when the legal title or the possession<br>
of the goods has been transferred<br>
to the buyer. Examples on risks of<br>
ownership include, obsolescence,<br>
damage, and the inability of an asset<br>
to operate at its optimum levels.<br>
<b>Rewards of ownership include,</b><br>
future economic benefits or revenue<br>
from the use of the asset or increases<br>
in the value of the asset.<br>
2.	 The seller loses control over the<br>
goods sold. This is reflected in the<br>
seller ceasing to have managerial<br>
involvement to the degree usually<br>
associated with ownership, and<br>
effective control over the goods<br>
sold. Consider a scenario where<br>
a company named Infotech Plc, a<br>
manufacturer of electronic products<br>
enters into a distribution agreement<br>
with Msimbazi Stores. The following<br>
being the key terms and conditions<br>
of the agreement:<br>
(a)	Msimbazi Stores will obtain title<br>
to the goods and will sell them to<br>
retailers.<br>
(b)	Msimbazi Stores will earn a<br>
fixed margin on the products<br>
sold to retailers but will have no<br>
authority in establishing the sale<br>
price of the goods for retailers.<br>
(c)	Msimbazi Stores has the right to<br>
return the goods to Infotech if<br>
they remain unsold for 2 years.<br>
<b>Accordingly, Infotech plc sold</b><br>
goods worth TZS 10,000,000 to<br>
<b>Msimbazi Store. When should</b><br>
the sale be accounted for? In this<br>
case, the sale to Msimbazi Stores<br>
cannot be recognised as a sale as<br>
<b>Infotech Plc retains continuing</b><br>
managerial involvement with the<br>
goods by being able to set the sales<br>
price. Also, Msimbazi Stores has<br>
the option to return the goods, so<br>
the risks and returns are not fully<br>
transferred. Therefore, Infotech<br>
<b>Plc should continue recognising</b><br>
the inventory on its statement<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
147<br>
of financial position. Infotech<br>
should recognise the revenue<br>
only when substantially all the<br>
risks and rewards of ownership<br>
have been transferred, which<br>
will be when Msimbazi Stores<br>
sells the goods to a third party.<br>
3.	 The collection of payment from goods<br>
or services is reasonably assured in<br>
the sense that, it is probable that,<br>
the economic benefits associated<br>
with the transaction will flow to the<br>
entity/seller. Consider a situation<br>
where a business named Queensland<br>
<b>Ltd operating in currency Y, has</b><br>
made sales to Bongoland Ltd,<br>
which operates in currency X. Due<br>
to political reasons, it is uncertain<br>
whether the government in which<br>
<b>Bongoland Ltd operates will permit</b><br>
to transact with Queensland Ltd<br>
and will remit any consideration<br>
from this transaction. In this case,<br>
<b>Queensland Ltd cannot recognise the</b><br>
revenue since the economic benefits<br>
associated with the transaction may<br>
not flow to this company.<br>
<b>At times this may not be probable</b><br>
until the uncertainty is removed. In<br>
this case, revenue will be recognised<br>
only after the uncertainty has been<br>
removed.<br>
4.	 The amount of revenue can be<br>
reasonably/reliably<br>
measured.<br>
<b>For example, Pleasant Ltd sells</b><br>
10,000 mobile phones to Smart Co.<br>
<b>However, they are still negotiating</b><br>
the value of the mobile phones<br>
which have been sold. In this case,<br>
<b>Pleasant Ltd cannot recognise any</b><br>
sales revenue until the value of the<br>
10,000 mobile phones is determined.<br>
5.	 Costs of revenue can be reasonably<br>
measured. The costs incurred or<br>
to be incurred in respect of the<br>
transaction should be able to be<br>
measured reasonably or reliably. If it<br>
is not possible to measure expenses<br>
reliably, then any consideration<br>
received from the sale of goods<br>
should be recognised as a liability.<br>
<b>For example, Machine Tools Ltd has</b><br>
sold a plant worth TZS 25,000,000<br>
to Saini Carpentry Ltd., soon after<br>
delivering the plant the buyer paid<br>
the entire amount as stated in the<br>
sales contract. Within this sales<br>
contract, it has been stated that, the<br>
seller i.e., Machines Tools Ltd is the<br>
one to bear the installation expenses.<br>
<b>This is expected to be of considerable</b><br>
value. However, as on the statement<br>
of financial position date, neither<br>
has the plant been installed nor is<br>
it possible to reasonably or reliably<br>
estimate the expenses involved.<br>
In this case (Machine Tools Ltd vs.<br>
<b>Saini Carpentry Ltd), the amount</b><br>
received (TZS 25,00,000) will not<br>
be reflected as revenue but as a<br>
current liability. This is similar to<br>
advance payment received from<br>
Saini Carpentry Ltd.<br>

<b>Recognition of revenues from rendering services</b><br>
Recognition of revenue from rendering services depends upon whether the outcome<br>
of a transaction can be reliably estimated by considering the following criteria:<br>
(a)<br>
If the outcome can be estimated reasonably/reliably, revenue from services<br>
will be recognised by considering the stage of completion in rendering the<br>
services.<br>
(b)	 However, if the outcome cannot be estimated reliably, revenue will be<br>
recognised only to the extent of the expenses recognised that are recoverable.<br>
Figure 6.2 summarises the criteria for the recognition of revenue from rendering<br>
services.<br>
Figure 6. 2: Criteria for estimating revenue outcome in a reliable manner<br>
Further explanations to clarify key issues presented in figure 6.2 are given in the<br>
following sections.<br>
(a)<br>
Criteria number 1: reliable estimates of revenue can be made when both<br>
the parties to the contract agree on three important matters including, each<br>
party’s enforceable rights about the services to be rendered or received,<br>
consideration to be exchanged, as well as the manner and the terms of the<br>
settlement.<br>
(b)	 Condition number 2: it is probable that, the economic benefits associated<br>
with the transaction will flow to the entity. For example, ABC Company<br>
recognised revenue from client XYZ, however, some issues have emerged<br>

(c)	Criteria number 3: the stage of<br>
completion of the transaction at<br>
the statement of financial posi­<br>
tion date, is normally used when<br>
the outcome of the transaction<br>
can be reasonably/reliably meas­<br>
ured. A good example for this<br>
includes, a transaction involving<br>
a service contract that is com­<br>
pleted over an extended period<br>
of time. In this case, the seller’s<br>
performance obligations cannot<br>
be immediately completed to de­<br>
serve the full amount of consid­<br>
eration. Sometimes, it may take<br>
several reporting periods. The<br>
provision by FRS is that, revenue<br>
should be recognised depending<br>
on the stage of completion of a<br>
contract/service.<br>
(d)	Condition number 4: is about the<br>
ability to measure reasonably/re­<br>
liably the costs of the transaction<br>
and the cost of completing the<br>
transaction. In the early stages of<br>
rendering services when the out­<br>
come of a transaction is not cer­<br>
tain, revenue will be recognised<br>
only to the extent of costs in­<br>
curred. Logically, it is assumed<br>
that, at a minimum, these are the<br>
costs more likely to be recovered<br>
from the client. If it is not prob­<br>
able that the costs will be recov­<br>
ered, no revenue is recognised,<br>
and all costs are immediately ex­<br>
pensed.<br>
<b>Recognition of revenues from</b><br>
interest, dividends and royalties<br>
<b>When the assets of the entity are released</b><br>
to be used by other persons for certain<br>
charge or fee in return, the amount to be<br>
received by the entity can be interest,<br>
royalties or dividends, depending with<br>
the nature of the asset. Interest is received<br>
as charges when the entity advances<br>
assets in the form of loan or debts while<br>
dividends is a reward for holding of<br>
investments in shares. On the other<br>
hand, royalty is the income received<br>
when a business allows other businesses<br>
to use its intangible assets e.g., know-<br>
how, trademarks, patents and copy<br>
rights. If the royalty is payable based<br>
on the number of copies, the number of<br>
compact discs (CDs) for instance, then<br>
it has to be recognised on that basis<br>
only. Recognition of these revenues is<br>
determined by different conditions i.e.,<br>
when it is probable that, the economic<br>
benefits associated with the transaction<br>
will flow to the entity, and the amount<br>
of the revenue can be measured reliably.<br>

for recognising interest, royalties and<br>
dividend are summarised in figure 6.3.<br>

Figure 6. 3: Basis of recognising interest, royalties and dividends<br>
Further clarification on different items presented in figure 6.3 are considered by<br>
referring items from left to right. By definition, effective interest method refers to<br>
the technique used to calculate the actual interest rate in a period based on the book<br>
value of a loan/debt at the beginning of the accounting period. For example, if the<br>
book value of the loan/debt decreases, so too will the amount of related interest, and<br>
vice versa i.e., if the book value increases, so too will the amount of related interest.<br>
This method is used to account for premiums and discounts when transacting. A<br>
premium occurs when investors are willing to pay more than the face value of the<br>
asset because its stated interest rate is higher than the prevailing market interest rate.<br>
On the other hand, discount on asset occurs when investors are only willing to pay<br>
less than the face value of a bond, because its stated interest rate is lower than the<br>
prevailing market rate.<br>
For example, assume, on 1st January 2020, Mdoki Co., purchased a bond worth<br>
TZS 10,000,000. The bond attracts an interest of 5% every year. On 31st December,<br>
2022, it will be redeemed for TZS 11,660,000. The effective interest rate is 10%. In<br>
this case, the total revenue is not only interest but also the redemption premium. The<br>
total amount receivable as interest and premium on this bond will be determined as<br>
follows.<br>
<b>TZS “000”</b><br>
<b>On account of interest (TZS 10,000,000 x 5%) x 3 years</b><br>
1,500<br>
<b>On account of premium on redemption (TZS 11,660,000 – TZS</b><br>
10,000,000)<br>
1,660<br>
<b>Total amount receivable</b><br>
3,160<br>

As for methods for recognition of dividend and royalty, as shown in figure 6.3, both<br>
are recognised using accrual basis of accounting. However, after observing certain<br>
conditions. For dividends, only when it is certain that the board of directors has<br>
decided/declared to pay dividends to the shareholders of the company. For royalty,<br>
according to the substance of the agreement. For example, assumes that, on 1st July,<br>
2021, Mr. Ongara agreed with the publisher of his book that, the royalty on the sale<br>
of his new book will be paid in a particular pattern. For the sales made in month 1,<br>
the royalty will be payable on day 1 of month 3. This means that, for the sales made<br>
in November, 2021, he will receive a royalty on 1st January, 2022 and for sales made<br>
in December, 2021 he will receive a royalty on 1st February, 2022. In this case, the<br>
financial statements for the year to 31st December, 2021 will recognise revenue in<br>
the form of royalty for all sales made between 1st July, 2021 and 31st December,<br>
2021. This is because the royalty has accrued even on sales made in November,<br>
2021 and December, 2021 even if it has not been paid till the statement of financial<br>
position date.<br>

<b>The nature of accounts for income</b><br>
Revenue or income is money that has been earned by the business. Therefore, the<br>
accounts used to record business transactions relating to income generally will<br>
be tracking incoming money both from operational and non-operational business<br>
activities. Examples of income accounts include, product sales, rendering of services,<br>
rent, and interest received. The increase in accounts used to record business revenue<br>
will be reflected by credit entries in relevant accounts. The reason why revenues<br>
are credited is that they increase the shareholders’ equity of a business. As it<br>
is commonly known, shareholders’ equity has a natural credit balance. Thus,<br>
an increase in equity can only be caused by transactions that are credited. The<br>
foundation of this reasoning is the accounting equation, stated as follows:<br>
The accounting equation appears in the structure of the statement of financial<br>
position where assets (with natural debit balances) offset liabilities and<br>
shareholders’ equity (with natural credit balances). When a sale occurs, the<br>
revenue (in the absence of any offsetting expenses) automatically increases<br>
profits – in return profits increase shareholders’ equity.<br>
The nature and meaning of accruals and prepayments relating to revenues<br>
Accruals are revenues earned that impact a company’s net income, although cash<br>
has not yet been received. Moreover, accrued revenue commonly represents revenue<br>
that has not yet been received. The basic idea behind the use of accrued revenue<br>
principles is that revenue should be recognized as earned before actual cash payment<br>
is subsequently received. Therefore, accrued revenue is recognized on the date the<br>
sale occurs and then included in a firm’s gross revenue on the income statement.<br>
Accounts receivable must be included on the balance sheet as either a short-term<br>
or long-term asset depending on the terms of payment. The recognition of accrued<br>
revenue is necessary to properly match revenues with expenses, where the failure to<br>
recognize accrued revenue would show lower revenue and profits.<br>
<b>Income received in advance or deferred revenue</b><br>
Income received in advance also known as deferred revenue represents a prepayment<br>
by its customers for goods or services that have yet to be delivered. The basic rule for<br>
this unearned revenue under the accrual basis of accounting is that income related<br>
to the current year is to be recorded as income for the current year. The excess<br>
income received in advance will need to be recorded in the same financial year by<br>
<b>Assets = Liabilities + Shareholders’ equity</b><br>

reducing the income, which is received in advance and shown in the statement of<br>

<b>Accounts to effect adjustments for accruals and prepayments</b><br>
(a)<br>
<b>Adjustments of accrued income (outstanding income)</b><br>
Income can also accrue continuously and therefore, there is a need to recognize it in<br>
the financial year in which it has accrued regardless of actual receipts of the cash. In<br>
other words, the date of actual receipt of the income is not so important. Consider<br>
the following example:<br>
Mwenge had business premises that were lying vacant for a relatively long period.<br>
However, recently they were able to release them for an agreed rent of TZS 400,000<br>
per month with effect from 1st April 2020. The following rents were received by<br>
<b>Mwenge:</b><br>
<b>Period</b><br>
<b>Rent received on</b><br>
<b>Amount (TZS)</b><br>
1st April, 2020 to 31st Dec., 2020 (9 months)<br>
1st January, 2020<br>
3,600,000<br>
1st Jan., 2021 to 31st March, 2022 (15 months) 1st April, 2021<br>
6,000,000<br>
The reporting period for Mwenge is 31st, March each year.<br>
Necessary adjustments and financial records: the rent for the 9 months from 1st<br>
April 2020 to 31st December 2020 must be accounted for as received income. Rent<br>
for the 3 months from January 2021 to March 2021 should be considered as accruals<br>
even though it had not actually been received by Mwenge. Mwenge needs to record<br>
an amount of TZS 1,200,000 (TZS 400,000 x 3) in its accounts as rent receivable for<br>
the year ended 31st March 2021.<br>

Note that, the accrued rent income of TZS 1,200,000 is shown as a current asset in<br>
the statement of financial position. Correspondingly, the same amount of accrued<br>
rent (TZS 1,200,000) is added to the figure of rent income appearing in the statement<br>
of profit or loss. Therefore, accrued rent has an impact on profit and net assets.<br>
Accrued rent income would lead to a creation of current asset in the statement of<br>
financial position and increase in income in the profit or loss statement. Hence the<br>
following two effects are noticed:<br>
(i)<br>
Increase in income = increase in profit for the current period,<br>
(ii)	 Creation / increase in asset = increase in net assets.<br>

<b>Adjustment of income received in advance</b><br>
A firm of lawyers received TZS 10 million on 24th March 2021 as fees for a case<br>
that it was handling and booked the same as revenue for the period. This amount<br>
covered 2 court hearings requiring approximately similar time and effort. Up to the<br>
end of the reporting period on 31st March 2021, only 1 hearing was complete. The<br>
second hearing took place on 30th April 2021.<br>
From the above example, the necessary adjustments and financial records at the<br>
reporting date should consider the fact that only half of the work was complete.<br>
Therefore, only half of the fees were supposed to be accounted. The fees for both the<br>
hearings received in advance must be accounted in the following manner:<br>
(i)<br>
Half of the fees i.e., TZS 10 million x 1/2 = TZS 5 million is treated as<br>
revenue in 2021.<br>
(ii)	 The remaining TZS 5,000,000 is treated as income received in advance.<br>
Note that, the fees for both the hearings were received in advance. Thus, journal<br>
entries for income received in advance and the treatment in financial statements will<br>
be presented as follows:<br>

<b>Concept of expenses and recognition</b><br>
criteria<br>
<b>The definition of expenses encompasses</b><br>
losses as well as those expenses that<br>
arise during the ordinary activities of<br>
an entity. Examples of expenses arising<br>
in the ordinary course of the entity’s<br>
activities include the cost of sales,<br>
wages, and depreciation. They usually<br>
take the form of outflow or depletion of<br>
assets such as cash and cash equivalents,<br>
inventory,<br>
property,<br>
plant,<br>
and<br>
equipment. Essentially, expenses are a<br>
cost of operations that a company incurs<br>
to generate revenue. A proverb, “it costs<br>
money to make money”, holds. It is also<br>
important to note that, businesses can<br>
write off some tax-deductible expenses<br>
in their income tax returns if they meet<br>
the<br>
<b>Tanzania</b><br>
<b>Revenue</b><br>
<b>Authority’s</b><br>
guidelines.<br>
<b>For a businessperson, it is critically</b><br>
important to understand the nature of<br>
different expenses incurred by his/<br>
her business. Remember, among the<br>
main goals of business management<br>
is to maximize profits. One way to<br>
achieve this is by boosting revenues<br>
while keeping expenses in check.<br>
<b>Sometimes businesses do slash some</b><br>
costs they consider to be unnecessary<br>
to help a business enterprise make even<br>
more money from sales. However, if<br>
expenses are cut too much, it could<br>
have a detrimental effect. For example,<br>
the company may decide to pay less in<br>
advertising to reduce its marketing costs,<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
158<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
but this may have a negative effect like<br>
lowering the company’s visibility and<br>
ability to reach out to potential customers.<br>
<b>To understand the importance of costs</b><br>
supporting the operation of a business<br>
and the ability to record them properly,<br>
it is important to understand well the<br>
different types and form of costs that are<br>
usually being incurred by the business.<br>
<b>Recognition of expenses</b><br>
<b>Expenses are recognised in the income</b><br>
statement when a decrease in future<br>
economic benefit related to a decrease<br>
in an asset or an increase of a liability<br>
has arisen. In this case, the decrease<br>
or increase needs to be the one which<br>
can be measured reliably. This means,<br>
in effect, that recognition of expenses<br>
occurs<br>
simultaneously<br>
with<br>
the<br>
recognition of an increase in liabilities or<br>
a decrease in assets. Examples include<br>
the accrual of employee entitlements or<br>
the depreciation of equipment.<br>
<b>Expenses are recognised in the income</b><br>
statement based on a direct association<br>
between the costs incurred and the<br>
earning of specific items of income.<br>
<b>This process is commonly referred to as</b><br>
the matching of costs with revenues. It<br>
involves the simultaneous or combined<br>
recognition of revenues and expenses<br>
that result directly and jointly from the<br>
same transactions or other events. For<br>
example, the various components of<br>
expense making up the cost of goods<br>
sold are recognised at the same time as<br>
the income derived from the sale of the<br>
goods.<br>
<b>When economic benefits are expected</b><br>
to arise over several accounting periods<br>
and the association with income can<br>
only be broadly or indirectly determined,<br>
expenses are recognised in the statement<br>
of profit of loss and other comprehensive<br>
income based on systematic and rational<br>
allocation procedures. This is often<br>
necessary in recognising the expenses<br>
associated with the use of assets such<br>
as property, plant, equipment, goodwill,<br>
patents, and trademarks. In such cases,<br>
the expense is referred to as depreciation<br>
or<br>
amortisation.<br>
<b>These</b><br>
allocation<br>
procedures are intended to recognise<br>
expenses in the accounting periods in<br>
which the economic benefits associated<br>
with these items are consumed or expire.<br>
<b>An expense is recognised immediately</b><br>
in the statement of profit or loss and<br>
other comprehensive income when<br>
expenditure<br>
produces<br>
no<br>
future<br>
economic benefits. In other words, this<br>
occurs when, and to the extent that,<br>
future economic benefits do not qualify<br>
or cease to qualify for recognition in the<br>
financial position as an asset. An expense<br>
is also recognised in the statement of<br>
profit or loss and other comprehensive<br>
income in those cases when a liability<br>
is incurred without the recognition of an<br>
asset, as when a liability under a product<br>
warranty arises.<br>
<b>In real-world of business, you may find</b><br>
people mentioning expenses as being<br>
different from expenditures – what is the<br>

<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>

159<br>
difference? This question is addressed in the following section.<br>
<b>Major difference between expenses and expenditures</b><br>
Expenditure is a payment or the incurrence of a liability, whereas an expense<br>
represents the consumption of an asset. Thus, a company could make a<br>
TZS10,000,000/= expenditure of cash for a non-current asset, but the TZS<br>
10,000,000/= asset would only be charged to expense account over the term of<br>
its useful life. Thus, expenditure generally occurs upfront while the recognition<br>
of an expense might be spread over an extended period. Table 6.1 is used to<br>
distinguish expenses from expenditures.<br>
<b>Table 6. 1: Differences between expenses and expenditures</b><br>
<b>Expenses</b><br>
<b>Expenditures</b><br>
(a)	 An<br>
expense<br>
recognises<br>
the<br>
consumption of a cost.<br>
(b)	 Expenses are generally associated<br>
with the short-term operations of<br>
a business.<br>
(c)	 They are the costs that are<br>
incurred to earn revenues.<br>
(a)	 An<br>
expenditure<br>
represents<br>
the<br>
disbursement of funds.<br>
(b)	 Expenditures are associated with<br>
the acquisition of assets intended to<br>
be used for a long period of time.<br>
(c)	 They are the costs spent on purchases<br>
or growth of fixed assets.<br>
<b>Types of expenses</b><br>
When preparing income statement and a balance sheet, accountants usually make a<br>
clear distinction between operational and non–operational expenses. What do these<br>
terms mean? The answer to this question is given in the following parts.<br>
(i)	 Operating expenses – this is an expense directly related to the company’s<br>
main activities, such as the cost of goods sold, rent, and administration<br>
expenses. Operating expenses are associated with the primary operational<br>
activities of a business. These activities include the production of goods<br>
or provision of services and the ongoing administration of a business.<br>
(ii)	 Non-operating expenses – these expenses that are indirectly related to<br>
the business’s core operations. Non-operating expenses are not associated<br>
with the firm’s primary operating activities. Examples of non-operating<br>
expenses include, interest expenses and lawsuit losses.<br>
<b>Basic methods for recognizing expenses</b><br>
There are two basic methods, which are used to recognize expenses namely, cash<br>
and accrual basis of accounting.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
160<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
(i)	 Cash basis – cash basis of accounting refers to a major accounting method<br>
that recognises revenues and expenses at time cash is received or paid out.<br>
This method is commonly used by small retail traders due to its simplicity and<br>
the small number of business transactions they are dealing with.<br>
(ii)	 Accrual basis – under this method, income is usually recognized at the time the<br>
revenue is earned and records expenses when liabilities are incurred regardless<br>
of the time of receiving or paying cash. The accrual basis of accounting is<br>
commonly used by different business entities, especially large-scale business<br>
operations.<br>
Difference between cash basis and accrual basis of accounting<br>
Cash and accrual basis of accounting may be differentiated based on different criteria<br>
including, level of simplicity/difficulty in their application. As a result, different<br>
types of businesses may find it easy to apply compared to others. The two methods<br>
also offer different results, and different levels of accuracy. Differences between the<br>
two methods of accounting are summarized in table 6.2.<br>
Table 6. 2: Differences between cash and accrual basis of accounting<br>
<b>Cash basis of accounting</b><br>
<b>Accrual basis of accounting</b><br>
(a)<br>
<b>Immediate recognition of revenue</b><br>
and expenses as cash changes<br>
hands.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>Easier to apply because it follows</b><br>
the timing of cash flows.<br>
(c)<br>
<b>Mostly used by sole traders due to</b><br>
its simplicity.<br>
(a)	 Recognition is based on anticipated<br>
revenue and expenses provided the<br>
business has earned or incurred<br>
them, respectively.<br>
(b)	 Portrays<br>
more<br>
accurately<br>
the<br>
nature and occurrence of business<br>
transactions.<br>
(c)	 Commonly used by the Government<br>
and large-scale business entities<br>
e.g. the public companies.<br>

<b>Accounting for expenses</b><br>
The posting of expenses is usually done by debiting relevant accounts depending<br>
on the nature of the transactions. The normal expense account is characterised by<br>
having a debit balance. Following this, an important question to consider would<br>
be, why expenses account is debited instead of being credited? Different answers<br>
can be provided including, relating it with the effect expenses have on the basic<br>
accounting equation. Normally, expenses cause the owner’s equity to decrease.<br>
Since the owner’s equity normal balance is on the credit side of the account, and<br>
expense must be recorded as a debit to reduce the amount in that account.<br>
For example, consider a business with only one type of asset, cash TZS 1,000,000<br>
which corresponds with the value of its owner’s equity. If this firm pays for<br>
advertisement TZS 250,000 the company will be required to reduce its cash by TZS<br>
250,000 by debiting the amount to the advertisement expenses account. Likewise, in<br>
the balance sheet, the owner’s equity will be reduced to TZS 750,000.<br>
In most cases, the posting of business transactions in the form of expenses largely<br>
depends on the mode of payments, as explained in the following relevant journal<br>
entries:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>If mode of payment is cash or cheque</b><br>
Dr.<br>
<b>Expenses account</b><br>
xxxx<br>
Cr.<br>
<b>Cash/bank account</b><br>
xxxx<br>
(ii)	 If incurred expenses not paid immediately<br>
Dr.<br>
<b>Expenses account</b><br>
xxxx<br>
Cr.<br>
<b>Account payable account</b><br>
xxxx<br>
(iii)	 If other incurred liabilities not yet paid.<br>
Dr.<br>

Cr.<br>
<b>Other liabilities</b><br>
xxxx<br>
The question of the timing of payments both outstanding and prepaid expenses<br>
usually have different treatment in the books of accounts. Key issues related to this<br>
are considered in the following sections.<br>
Outstanding expenses – by referring to the current or existing accounting period,<br>
these reflect the types of expenses, which have been incurred i.e., the business has<br>
used the goods or services but for which payment has not been done. They reflect<br>
the repayment obligations i.e. the amount of money owed to vendors for expenses<br>
incurred but not yet paid. For example, ABC Company has succeeded in marketing<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>

its products through advertising media namely Universal TV. However, payment<br>
for the same has not been done until the time when the company is preparing its<br>
financial statements. ABC Company is expected to consider this and other similar<br>
expenses as outstanding expenses.<br>
Corresponding to the previous example, when exactly do businesses make payments<br>
for goods and services consumed in generating income? The answer to this may<br>
differ from one business to another. However, in practice invoices are usually<br>
received/paid after the financial year has ended. In certain cases, organisations may<br>
prefer to pay a supplier in a lump sum rather than pay them on a weekly/monthly<br>
basis. When company acquires goods by orders, sometimes may prefer to make bulk<br>
payment for a contract in the middle of the year – covering even a few months of<br>
the following year.<br>
<b>Treatment of outstanding expenses in financial statements</b><br>
Based on the accrual basis of accounting, outstanding expenses will be considered<br>
as part and parcel of total expenses to be matched with generated revenues in the<br>
statement of profit or loss and other comprehensive income. This is considered<br>
fair since outstanding expenses have also been used or incurred in generating the<br>
revenue or income that is currently being considered. In the statement of financial<br>
position, outstanding expenses will be incorporated as a current liability with other<br>
components in this area e.g., creditors. These reflect the credit balance drawn from<br>
personal accounts found in ledger accounts. The following is a summary of the<br>

Revenues and expenses are essential elements of financial statements in<br>
determining the performance of any business whether small or large, they assist<br>
in determining profits obtained or losses suffered during the accounting year. To<br>
properly report the results of business, revenues and expenses need to be well<br>
recognised and properly treated. Revenues should be recognised when earned<br>
and not when related cash is received, and expenses should be recognised when<br>
incurred and not when cash is paid and should be in the same period as revenue to<br>
which they relate. Recognition of revenues and expenses are based on how they<br>
can be reliably measured as well as being probable to happen.<br>
The chapter also identified accrued revenues and outstanding expenses which<br>
are revenues earned, but no cash has been received and expenses incurred but no<br>
cash paid for them respectively. The chapter also explain the concept of income<br>
received in advance and prepaid expenses. Income received in advance are the<br>
payments (cash received) while goods have not yet been supplied, or services<br>
not rendered likewise the prepaid expenses are cash paid pending for goods or<br>
services to be received. Adjustments for accruals and prepayments should be<br>
done in both statements of profit or loss and other comprehensive income and<br>
statement of financial position.<br>
The chapter also has enlightened the two methods of accounting for revenues<br>
and expenses namely cash basis and accrual basis. When cash basis is used, only<br>
the cash received and paid expenses are recognised in determining profit or loss<br>
while accrual basis recognises revenues and expenses when they are earned and<br>
incurred respectively and not when cash is received or paid out.<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Seven</b><br>
<b>Preparation</b><br>
of financial<br>
statements<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter, you will learn about the concept of financial statements, their<br>
objectives and how to prepare complete set of a business financial statements. The<br>
competencies developed in this chapter will enable you to identify and describe<br>
the different elements of financial statements and prepare them according to<br>
established accounting standards.<br>
<b>The meaning of financial statements</b><br>
<b>Financial statements are written reports</b><br>
prepared by the management of a<br>
business or company that capture its<br>
financial activities and performance<br>
in a summarized format. The IFRS,<br>
conceptual framework for financial<br>
reporting, defines financial statements as<br>
a particular form of financial reports that<br>
provide information about the reporting<br>
entity’s assets, liabilities, equity, income<br>
and expenses. Financial statements are<br>
usually prepared periodically; quarterly,<br>
half a year or on yearly basis. However,<br>
many companies are required by the law<br>
to prepare and publish their financial<br>
statements at the end of accounting<br>
period, covering a total of 12 months.<br>
<b>Financial statements are also referred</b><br>
to as, the general-purpose financial<br>
statements. They are termed as such<br>
because they are intended to meet the<br>
needs of different users who are not in a<br>
position to demand an entity to prepare<br>
tailored reports according to their<br>
information needs. Primarily, financial<br>
statements are prepared to meet the needs<br>
of external users including, shareholders,<br>
lenders and other creditors.<br>
<b>Note that, the users of financial</b><br>
statements (also known as users of<br>
accounting information) are many.<br>
<b>Even their interest and objectives</b><br>
for demanding financial statements<br>
are so diverse. This makes difficult<br>
for management to prepare financial<br>
reports that meet the specific needs<br>
and objectives of each type of users.<br>
<b>To extend their usefulness, financial</b><br>
statements or general-purpose financial<br>
statements are prepared to incorporate<br>
all important information that reflects<br>
the business activities of the company,<br>
its financial performance, financial<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
172<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
position and cash flows. In order to enhance understand-ability and usefulness of<br>
financial statements to a wide range of users, accounting principles, concepts and<br>
standards are used to guide the preparation of such statements. For instance, IFRS<br>
provides useful recommendations, including specific formats to be used as well as<br>
the contents to be included in different types of financial statements. However, it is<br>
the users themselves that are supposed to identify key information they need and<br>
make analysis to inform their different decisions.<br>
<b>The scope and purpose of financial statements</b><br>
The financial statements of a company usually are composed of five basic<br>
components as described in table 7.1. These include the income statement (also<br>
known as the statement of financial performance or statement of profit or loss and<br>
other comprehensive income), the statement of financial position (also known as<br>
balance sheet), statement of cash flows, statement of changes in equity, and notes to<br>
the financial statements. As their respective names suggest, they are prepared for the<br>
purpose of providing information about the financial performance (also known as<br>
the results of business operations), financial position and cash flows of the business.<br>
The purposes of different types of financial statements are summarised in table 7.1<br>
as follows.<br>
Table 7. 1: Types of financial statements and their purposes<br>
<b>S/N</b><br>
<b>Name of the financial</b><br>
statement<br>
<b>Purpose</b><br>
1.<br>
<b>Income statement</b><br>
<b>To determine the profitability of the business by</b><br>
matching revenue and expenses incurred in the<br>
process of generating revenue.<br>
2.<br>
<b>Statement of financial</b><br>
position<br>
<b>Shows the value or financial worthiness of the</b><br>
business at the time of reporting by listing types<br>
and value of assets, liabilities/debts and equity/<br>
capital supporting the business operations.<br>
3.<br>
Statement of cash flows Shows the nature of cash receipts and cash<br>
disbursements by categories i.e., operating,<br>
financing and investing activities.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
173<br>
<b>S/N</b><br>
<b>Name of the financial</b><br>
statement<br>
<b>Purpose</b><br>
4.<br>
<b>Statement of changes in</b><br>
equity<br>
<b>Reports the changes in owners’ capital/equity</b><br>
due to different related transactions e.g., retained<br>
profit/earnings, other reserves, issue of new shares<br>
and payment of dividends over an accounting<br>
period.<br>
5.<br>
<b>Notes to the financial</b><br>
statements<br>
<b>Additional</b><br>
information<br>
included<br>
with<br>
the<br>
published financial statements of a company<br>
that helps explain how a company arrived at its<br>
financial statement figures. These supplemental<br>
notes also disclose the detailed assumptions made<br>
by accountants when preparing the financial<br>
statements. They are essential to fully understand<br>
these documents.<br>
<b>The objective of financial statements</b><br>
The International Accounting Standards state that, the objective of general-purpose<br>
financial statements is to provide information about the financial position, financial<br>
performance, and cash flows of an entity that is useful to a wide range of users in<br>
making economic decisions. To meet that objective, financial statements provide<br>
information about an entity’s assets, liabilities, equity, income and expenses. They<br>
also show the gains or losses, contributions and distributions to owners (in their<br>
capacity as owners), and cash flows. The components of financial statements, along<br>
with other information, assist users of financial statements in predicting the entity’s<br>
future cash flows and, in particular, their timing and certainty.<br>
Users of financial statements are not different from those who were described in<br>
<b>chapter one; they include persons who have interest in the existence and survival</b><br>
of the business directly or indirectly. These are the persons who read the financial<br>
statements of a reporting entity prepared either as a fulfilment of law requirements<br>
or just based on entity’s own accord. However, in the preparation of general purpose<br>
financial statements, major targets are the external stakeholders such as shareholders,<br>
lenders and creditors. These persons are critically important for the company since<br>
it is from them that the business can raise funds to support operational and strategic<br>
activities. In turn, the users would want to have periodical feedback on the financial<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
174<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
performance and financial position of the business to know how management<br>
actually utilizes the resources they have contributed.<br>
<b>The elements of financial statements</b><br>
The IFRS conceptual framework of financial reporting, identifies the five elements<br>
contained in the financial statements namely: assets, liabilities, equity, income and<br>
expenses. The basic elements specifically related to financial position (balance sheet)<br>
include assets, liabilities and equity. Those specific to the statement of profit or loss<br>
(income statement) are income and expenses while the information contained in the<br>
cash flow statement reflects both, income statement elements and some elements of<br>
the statement of financial position. The meanings of different elements of financial<br>
statements are provided in table 7.2 that follows.<br>
<b>Table 7. 2: Basic elements of financial statements</b><br>
<b>S/N</b><br>
<b>Elements</b><br>
of financial<br>
statements<br>
<b>Meanings</b><br>
<b>Definitions of the elements relating to financial position</b><br>
1.<br>
<b>Assets</b><br>
Refers to present economic resources controlled by the entity<br>
as a result of past events. An economic resource is a right that<br>
has the potential to produce economic benefits. Assets can<br>
be non-current assets (fixed assets) or current assets. Non-<br>
current assets have useful life of more than one accounting<br>
period/year, and they can be in a tangible or intangible form.<br>
<b>Examples of tangible non-current assets are land, property,</b><br>
machines, fixtures and motor vehicles. Examples of intangible<br>
non-current assets are trademarks, goodwill and patents.<br>
<b>Meanwhile, current assets are those resources which can be</b><br>
sold or consumed within one period/year. Examples of current<br>
assets are cash, stock (inventory), prepayments and debtors<br>
(accounts receivable).<br>
2.<br>
<b>Liabilities</b><br>
<b>A present obligation of the entity to transfer an economic</b><br>
resource as a result of past events. An obligation is a duty or<br>
responsibility that the entity has no practical ability to avoid.<br>
Liabilities can be non-current liabilities which are the long-<br>
term obligations to the business entity or current liabilities<br>
which are short term obligations to be settled within a year.<br>
Examples of long-term obligations are issued bonds, mortgage<br>
loan etc while examples of current liabilities are creditors<br>
(accounts payables) and bank overdraft.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>

<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
175<br>
3.<br>
<b>Equity</b><br>
It is the amount supplied to the business by the owners, either<br>
through injecting capital or through retaining any amount of<br>
profit derived by the business. Also referred as the residual<br>
interest in the assets of the entity after deducting all its<br>
liabilities.<br>
Definitions of the elements relating to financial performance<br>
4.<br>
<b>Income</b><br>
<b>Refers to the increase in economic benefits during the</b><br>
accounting period in the form of inflows or enhancements<br>
of assets or decreases of liabilities that result in increases in<br>
equity, other than those relating to contributions from equity<br>
participants.<br>
5.<br>
<b>Expenses</b><br>
<b>Refers to the decrease in economic benefits during the</b><br>
accounting period in the form of outflows or depletions of<br>
assets or incurrence of liabilities that result in decreases in<br>
equity, other than those relating to distributions to equity<br>
participants.<br>

While referring to activity 7.1, one of the key issues that should have been observed<br>
is that, the names of the reporting entities indicate the form of business organizations<br>
i.e., companies. This is relatively different from the sole proprietors’ financial<br>
statements as observed in the early stages of learning bookkeeping e.g., lower<br>
levels of secondary education. The following section considers the differences in<br>
preparing the financial statements between sole trader and a company.<br>
The differences between financial statements of a sole trader and a company<br>
As you may recall, business can be organized into different forms including sole<br>
trader or sole proprietorship and a company. The sole proprietorship is a kind of<br>
business that is owned by a single person with no partners while for the company,<br>
several persons may own it through ownership of shares. It is important to understand<br>
the major differences between the two, since their differences also affect the ways<br>
in which their financial statements are being prepared. Further details on the key<br>
differences between sole proprietorship and a company are given in table 7.3, which<br>
is followed by details on the differences in their financial statements.<br>
Table 7. 3: Key differences between sole proprietorship and a company<br>
<b>S/N.</b><br>
<b>Distinguishing</b><br>
features<br>
<b>Company</b><br>
<b>Sole proprietorship</b><br>
1.<br>
<b>Ownership</b><br>
<b>Several</b><br>
or<br>
many<br>
individuals denoted by<br>
ownership of shares<br>
<b>Single person with no</b><br>
partners<br>
commonly<br>
adopted by small retail<br>
establishments/shops and<br>
individual<br>
enterprises/<br>
consulting<br>
firms<br>
e.g.,<br>
accountants,<br>
lawyers/<br>
attorneys and pharmacists.<br>

<b>As a result, on selling the</b><br>

also sell the (net) assets. No<br>
legal condition to prepare<br>
financial statements – it the<br>
decision by owners to do<br>
so.<br>
As noted in table 7.3, in many cases, probably due to their small sizes, most of<br>
the sole proprietorships tend to generate smaller amounts of revenue and incur few<br>
expenses compared to complex organizations like companies. Also, tax payment<br>
and reporting for a sole proprietorship flows through the owner’s personal tax return,<br>
with a separate form used to itemize the major classes of revenues and expenses<br>
incurred by the business. In other words, there is no separate tax return for the<br>
business, since there is no separate business entity. As a result, the income statement<br>
of a sole proprietorship does not include income taxes expense (since its profits are<br>
included in the owner’s personal income tax return).<br>
Furthermore, no salaries expense paid to the proprietor is recorded on a proprietorship’s<br>
income statement, since the proprietor receives all the net income of the business.<br>
This is the owner’s remuneration. Besides, in the statement of financial position,<br>
the owner’s equity section will have only one item, the owner’s equity account or<br>
capital. In contrast, for the company shareholders’ fund section, several items will be<br>
presented including, share capital plus retained earnings and other income and capital<br>
reserves. In order to compare and understand the differences in financial statements<br>
between sole proprietorship and a company, the following section first introduces the<br>
financial statements of a sole proprietor.<br>

<b>Financial statements of a sole proprietor</b><br>
For a sole proprietor of relatively small size, probably only limited set of financial<br>
statements would be prepared. In most cases, these would include the income<br>
statement and statement of financial position. Example 7.1 that follows provides the<br>
illustration of statement of financial position of a sole proprietor, namely Allan Hongo<br>
as at 31st December, 2021. It reflects the key idea of the accounting equation i.e.,<br>
total assets = total capital + total liabilities. It can be observed that, the non-current<br>
assets (fixed assets) are shown at their cost less the accumulated depreciation, which<br>
equals to the net book value of the assets. Current assets, on the other hand, are<br>
shown at their historical costs. Liabilities are also shown as non-current liabilities<br>
and current liabilities. For the capital, this starts with opening capital at 1st January,<br>
2021 to which, profit for the year is added and from which the drawing during the<br>
period is deducted to arrive at the closing capital at 31st December, 2021.<br>

<b>Financial statements of a company</b><br>
Unlike a small business (a typical of most sole proprietorship), a company would<br>
prepare a complete set of financial statements in addition to the two statements<br>
already covered so far. For a public company, besides the statements of financial<br>
position (balance sheet) and income statement, additional financial reports include,<br>
the statement of cash flows and statement of changes in equity. Different types<br>
of financial statements, which are usually prepared by the company, can now be<br>
considered.<br>
<b>Statement of financial position</b><br>
As noted earlier, the statement of financial position of a company will be slightly<br>
different from that of sole proprietor especially in the equity section. Let us consider<br>
the following statement of financial position of a company named Udzungwa Ltd<br>
and associated notes, which clarify certain matters presented in the statement.<br>

<b>Statement of financial performance</b><br>
The statement of financial performance is also known as the income statement. For<br>
the listed companies, this financial report is identified as ‘statement of profit or loss<br>
and other comprehensive income’ or simply ‘comprehensive income statement’. In<br>
simple words, comprehensive income is the income of a company that includes both<br>
operating profit and income from other sources like return on investments. The two<br>
can be presented in the same section or separately as a statement of profit or loss,<br>
and a statement of other comprehensive income. Example 7.4 that follows is used as<br>
illustration of the income statement of a company.<br>
Example 7.4: The structure and contents of statement of financial performance<br>
of a company as reflected in Udzungwa Ltd is as follows<br>

<b>Notes on accounting policies</b><br>

included in administrative expenses.<br>

<b>Notes to the financial statements</b><br>
Notes to the financial statements also referred to as disclosure notes or footnotes to<br>
the financial statements are presented at the end of financial statements to provide<br>
further details clarifying certain information revealed in the financial statements.<br>
They are reflected well at the end of the given illustrations of financial statements<br>
of Udzungwa Ltd, numbered as 1, 2 and 3. The accounting standards provide that,<br>
notes to the financial statements intend to present information about the basis of<br>
preparation of the financial statements and the specific accounting policies used. By<br>
definition, accounting policies are the specific principles and procedures adopted<br>
by the accountants when preparing financial statements. Examples of accounting<br>
policies include measurement systems or basis, procedures for presenting disclosures<br>
and accounting methods such as depreciation methods.<br>
