Introduction to the statement of cash flows and changes in owners’ equity<br>
<b>The meaning of statement of cash flows</b><br>
To understand the meaning of statement of cash flows, it is important to compare<br>
it with income statement. The income statement shows the profit of the company<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
189<br>
based on the accrual basis of accounting where revenues and expenses are matched<br>
to show the results of operations in the accounting period to which they relate.<br>
Under this, revenue is usually recognised in the accounting period in which it has<br>
been earned and expenses when they have actually been incurred in generating the<br>
associated income. Thus, the income statement does not provide the amount of net<br>
cash that the company has (cash revenue minus cash expenses). Statement of cash<br>
flows is designed to determine whether sufficient cash/funds are available as and<br>
when needed. Therefore, statement of cash flows is very useful in evaluating cash<br>
position and liquidity of the company. It can also be useful in analysing the cash<br>
receipts and payments from the various activities of a company. Therefore, one can<br>
use it as a tool for short term financial analysis and planning, and decision making on<br>
different matters e.g., how much should be distributed to shareholders as dividends.<br>
From the meaning above, the objectives of preparing statement of cash flows can be<br>
summarised as follows:<br>
(i)<br>
To determine inflow and outflow of cash and the cash equivalents obtained<br>
from the different kind of activities.<br>
(ii)	 To seek out various reasons responsible for change in cash balances during<br>
the accounting period.<br>
(iii)	 It helps in depicting the position of the company in terms of liquidity and<br>
solvency.<br>
(iv)	 It also helps in determining the requirement and the corresponding<br>
availability of cash for business in future.<br>
<b>Presentation of the statement of cash flows</b><br>
Statement of cash flows is usually presented neatly and in a systematic manner to<br>
show both the inflows and outflows of cash according to the nature of the business<br>
activities namely; operating activities, investing activities, and financing activities.<br>
This is reflected in the three main sections used to present statement of cash flows,<br>
as discussed in the following sections.<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Cash flows from operating activities</b><br>
The amount of cash flows arising from operating activities is a key indicator of the<br>
extent to which the operations of the entity have generated sufficient cash flows<br>
its ordinary course of activities. Management and providers of finance are highly<br>
interested to know this because generated funds are the ones used in repaying<br>
loans, maintain the operating capability of the entity, pay dividends and make new<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
190<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
investments without recourse to external sources of funds. The principal revenue-<br>
producing operating activities that determine the profit or loss include the following:<br>
(a)	Cash receipts from the sale of goods and the rendering of services,<br>
(b)	Cash receipts from royalties, fees, commissions and other revenue,<br>
(c)	Cash payments to suppliers for goods and services;<br>
(d)	Cash payments to and on behalf of employees,<br>
(e)	Cash receipts and cash payments of an insurance entity for premiums and<br>
claims, annuities and other policy benefits,<br>
(f)	 Cash payments or refunds of income taxes unless they can be specifically<br>
identified with financing and investing activities, and<br>
(g)	Cash receipts and payments from contracts held for dealing or trading pur­<br>
poses.<br>
(ii)	 Cash flows from investing activities<br>
The separate disclosure of cash flows arising from investing activities is important<br>
because the cash flows represent the extent to which expenditures have been made<br>
for resources intended to generate future income and cash flows. Only expenditures<br>
that result in a recognized asset in the statement of financial position are eligible<br>
for classification as investing activities. Examples of cash flows (both inflows and<br>
outflows) arising from investing activities include the following:<br>
(a)	Cash payments to acquire property, plant and equipment, intangibles and<br>
other long-term assets.<br>
(b)	Cash receipts from sales of property, plant and equipment, intangibles and<br>
other long-term assets.<br>
(c)	Cash advances and loans made to other parties.<br>
(d)	Cash receipts from the repayment of advances and loans made by other<br>
parties.<br>
(iii)	 Cash flows from financing activities<br>
The separate disclosure of cash flows arising from financing activities is important<br>
because it is useful in predicting claims on future cash flows by providers of capital<br>
to the entity. Examples of cash flows arising from financing activities include the<br>
following:<br>
(a)	Cash proceeds from issuing shares or other equity instruments,<br>
(b)	Cash payments to owners to acquire or redeem the entity’s shares,<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
191<br>
(c)	Cash proceeds from issuing debentures, mortgages and other short-term or<br>
long-term borrowings,<br>
(d)	Cash repayments of amounts borrowed,<br>
(e)	Cash payments by a lessee for the reduction of the outstanding liability<br>
relating to a finance lease.<br>
New terms in financing activities include, debentures and mortgages. Debentures<br>
are used as debt instrument in the sense that, a way in which a company may<br>
raise capital to meet the expenses of an upcoming project or to pay for a planned<br>
expansion from other persons besides the equity holders. The exchanges are legally<br>
formalized through the use of certificate, which states how much money the investor<br>
provided to the business (principal), the interest rate to be paid and the schedule of<br>
payments. Investors usually receive their principal back when the debenture matures<br>
(i.e., at the end of its term). During, the loan period, the company will be paying only<br>
the interest (a percentage of the face value of the certificate, or loan amount) and<br>
then the full principal (the loan amount) will be paid when the certificate matures.<br>
However, the company (borrower) can redeem or buy back the debentures at any<br>
time depending on the agreement with the debenture holders. Mortgage, on the other<br>
hand, is a type of loan used to acquire or maintain a home, land, or other types of<br>
real estate. The borrower agrees to pay the lender over time, typically in a series<br>
of regular payments that are divided into principal and interest. The property then<br>
serves as collateral to secure the loan.<br>
<b>Methods for preparing the statement of cash flows</b><br>
There are two methods which are used to prepare and present statement of cash<br>
flows, as explained below:<br>
(i)<br>
The direct method, in which major classes of gross cash receipts and gross<br>
cash payments are disclosed.<br>
(ii)	 The indirect method, in which profit or loss is adjusted for the effects of<br>
transactions of non-cash nature i.e., any deferrals or accruals of past or<br>
future operating cash receipts or payments, and items of income or expense<br>
associated with investing or financing cash flows.<br>
The major difference between the two methods can be observed in the way in which<br>
cash flows from operating activities is determined. As already noted, the indirect<br>
method will start with the net income or profit that has been determined using the<br>
accrual-based accounting and adjusting for different items that indicate the company<br>
received or spent cash during its normal business activities. On the other hand, the<br>
direct method uses actual cash inflows and outflows from the company’s operations,<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
192<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
instead of modifying the operating sections of financial statements normally<br>
prepared by using accrual-based accounting. Through illustrations and examples,<br>
you will understand well how the two methods are used to determine cash flows<br>
from operating activities in the preparation of the statement of cash flows.<br>
<b>Direct method of cash flow statement</b><br>
In determining the cash flows from operating activities, the direct method focuses<br>
more on the specific cash flows items that define the typical receipts and payments<br>
of cash in the business to include the following:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Receipts received from Customers,</b><br>
(ii)	 Payments paid to Suppliers,<br>
(iii)	 Payments paid to Employees<br>
(iv)	 Interest Payments, and<br>
(v)	 Income Tax Payments.<br>

from operating activities using direct method can be presented as follows:<br>

<b>The indirect method of presenting cash flows statement</b><br>
Determining the cash flows from operating using the indirect method, as already<br>
noted, starts with the identification of the net profit figure from the income statement<br>
prepared under accrual-based accounting. Thereafter, it will be adjusted by adding<br>
and/or subtracting, the different items that indicate the receiving and/or payments<br>
of money by the business. These items are provided by other types of financial<br>
statements namely, the income statement and balance sheet. The first category of<br>
these items includes the changes (increase or decrease) of current assets as presented<br>
in the statement of financial position. Remember, these items i.e., cash, debtors,<br>
inventory/stocks, and different types of current liabilities are the ones that closely<br>
define the daily operations of the business.<br>
For example, assume that, at the beginning of January, 2022, XYZ Company, reported<br>
debtors amounting to TZS 150,000 – however, at the end of the same month, the<br>
debtors in the books of books were reported as TZS 90,000. By interpretation, the<br>
decrease of debtors, is used to indicate that, some of the debtors have paid their due<br>
debts within the month. For the company, this means it has received cash from its<br>
debtors, TZS 60,000 (i.e., TZS 150,000 – TZS 90,000).<br>
Furthermore, let assume that, at the beginning of the same month, XYZ Company<br>
reported as having creditors valued at TZS 200,000. However, at the end of the<br>
month their records showed that, creditors totalled TZS 100,000. Interpretatively, the<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
195<br>
company will be considered as having paid some of its creditors within the month.<br>
The change in the amount owed to creditors between two different periods is an<br>
indication that, XYZ Company used some of its money to settle its debts obligations.<br>
Therefore, changes in the balances of different types of assets and liabilities are seen<br>
as affecting cash balances throughout the year. Under the direct method of preparing<br>
cash flows statement they are considered either as additions or subtractions from the<br>
net income to arrive at the amount of operating cash flow.<br>
The second items to adjust net income include the non-cash items as reported in<br>
the income statement in respect of which, no cash movements were involved. For<br>
example, depreciation costs, deferred income tax, and gain/loss on disposal of assets.<br>
Depreciation though charged in income statement as operating expenses; it does not<br>
reflect the actual cash payment by the company in a particular accounting period.<br>
Remember, depreciation is just an accounting technique for spreading the costs of<br>
assets over its useful lifetime. The same applies to deferred income tax, until such tax<br>
is paid, the company cannot claim to have spent cash to settle its tax liability. In other<br>
words, no cash was reduced in order to pay tax liability. As for gain/loss on disposal<br>
of assets, this is simply a notional figure calculated by deducting depreciation costs<br>
from the realized value of asset that has been sold. Thus, it reduces the figure of<br>
cash received on disposal of asset. Moreover, the whole of disposal value received<br>
in cash is usually shown as income from investing activities.<br>
Thus, using the indirect method of presenting statement of cash flows, the cash flow<br>
from operating activities will be presented as follows:<br>
<b>Details</b><br>
<b>TZS</b><br>
• Start with net income from the income statement.<br>
xxxx<br>
•	 Add back non-cash expenses, such as depreciation,<br>
amortization, and depletion.<br>
xxxx<br>
• Remove the effect of gains and/or losses from disposal of<br>
non-current assets, as cash from their disposal is shown under<br>
investing cash flows.<br>
xxxx<br>
• Adjust for changes in current assets and liabilities to remove<br>
accruals from operating activities.<br>
xxx<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>

<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
The use of direct and indirect methods in real world of business<br>
Practically, the direct method of preparing statement of cash flows is used by<br>
small businesses with relatively few business transactions. However, most of the<br>
businesses of the well-established business have a complex and voluminous business<br>
transactions – some of them in the form of cash and others, as credit transactions.<br>
Moreover, most of these businesses use accrual-based accounting to capture their<br>
financial records. Thus, the use of indirect method is reported to be more dominant<br>
due to its convenience and simplicity in obtaining the required information, which<br>
is readily available in their counting system. A comprehensive illustration on how to<br>
prepare the cash flows statement using indirect method can now be considered, after<br>
presenting the basic steps used to prepare the statement of cash flows.<br>
<b>Steps used to prepare the statement of cash flows</b><br>
From the proceeding discussion, five major items, which act as inputs for preparing<br>
the statements of cash flows include; net income, non-cash expenses (e.g.,<br>
depreciation and amortization), and tax liabilities. Others include, gains/losses on<br>
disposal of assets, changes in the value of current assets, and current liabilities, as<br>
well as, the value of non–current assets and long-term liabilities. Remember that, all<br>
this information can easily be obtained from the company’s income statement and<br>
balance sheet. Having all the necessary information, the statement of cash flows can<br>
be prepared by following five basic steps, that follows:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Determine the cash flows from operating activities,</b><br>
(ii)	 Determine the cash flows from financing activities,<br>
(iii)	 Determine cash flow from investing activities,<br>
(iv)	 Determine net increase or decrease which is obtained by adding amounts<br>
from all the cash flow activities.<br>
(v)	 Add the opening balance of cash to the amount determined in the previous<br>
step.<br>
The following example 7.7 will be used to demonstrate the preparation of statement<br>
of cash flows using the basic five (5) steps that have been identified.<br>

liabilities and noncash items.<br>

<b>The statement of changes in equity</b><br>
The statement of changes in equity summarises all the transactions that the<br>
organisation has had with its owners/shareholders. The statement explains the<br>
changes in a company’s share capital, accumulated reserves and retained earnings<br>
over the reporting period. In other words, it shows how the change in the equity<br>
section of the statement of financial position of a company has come about. For<br>
example, it may show an increase due to profit or a decrease due to loss or dividend<br>
payments during the year. It may also show any increase due to new share issues.<br>
In summary, the statement of changes in owners’ equity will show either of the<br>
following:<br>
(i)<br>
Whether shareholders have maintained their original investment in the<br>
organization.<br>
(ii)	 If shareholders’ capital has been added or reduced over a particular period.<br>
(iii)	 The levels of profit earned by the organization that has been reinvested into<br>
the business and the portion that has been paid out to owners/shareholders<br>
in the form of dividends.<br>
The structure of the statement of changes in equity can now be presented as follows:<br>

<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
This chapter has provided relevant materials about preparation of financial<br>
statements. The chapter has explained the major differences between the<br>
financial statements of a sole trader and those of a company. Key features for the<br>
differences include, the size of the business, volume and complexities of business<br>
transactions, ownership structure and tax legal liability associated with the form<br>
of business entity. For example, due to their small size, fewer transactions and less<br>
complicated mode of doing business, most of the sole traders will prepare two<br>
basic financial statements. These are called statement of financial performance<br>
(i.e., income statement) and statement of financial position (i.e., balance sheet). On<br>
the other hand, due to voluminous (big) business transactions and complexities,<br>
companies usually prepare additional statements namely, statement of cash flows<br>
and statement of owners’ equity. The chapter has shown that, in the balance<br>
sheet, the main difference can be observed in the equity section that reflect the<br>
ownership of the business. For sole trader, there is only one item, the owner’s<br>
capital section while for the company, there is shareholders fund section which<br>
contains several items including, share capital, retained earnings as well as other<br>
income and capital reserves.<br>
Regarding the income statement, the company’s statement will be extended to<br>
include income tax liability, which is paid by the company. Note that, legally sole<br>
proprietor business entities are not required to pay tax but their owners (the sole<br>
traders) are the ones required to pay income tax. The company’s income statement<br>
also shows the amount of profit that is distributable to its owners and the amount<br>
reserved as retained earnings in a particular accounting period. Concerning the<br>
additional financial statements, the statement of cash flows is designed to show the<br>
amount of cash generated by the business. It is very useful in this regard compared<br>
to statement of income since the latter is usually prepared using accrual-based<br>
principles rather than cash-based accounting. On the other hand, statement of<br>
changes in equity provides important information to understand how the change<br>
in the equity section of the balance sheet has come about. The chapter provides<br>
a number of class activities and exercises to enhance better understanding of key<br>
issues of interest to this topic.<br>
<b>Chapter summary</b><br>

1.	 What are the key features that differentiate sole proprietorship from a company?<br>
2.	 How do the identified features, in 1 above, affect the structure and contents of a<br>
company’s financial reports compared to that of a sole trader?<br>
3.	 Identify all important types of general-purpose financial statements of a company<br>
owned by several shareholders.<br>
4.	 What are the objectives of preparing each type of financial statement identified<br>
under (3) above?<br>
5.	 What are differences between statement of cash flows and statement of changes<br>
in owners’ equity.<br>
6.	 Cash flows statements are a waste of time to prepare since all the necessary<br>
information provided by this statement are provided in the income statement. Do<br>
you agree with this statement? Comment giving relevant examples to support<br>
your argument.<br>
7.	 Differentiate direct method from indirect method of preparing the statement of<br>
cash flows.<br>
8.	 You are the accountant trainee of Kumekucha company, what are the basic<br>
inputs and steps you will follow when preparing the statement of cash flows?<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Eight</b><br>
<b>The concept of financial statements</b><br>
analysis and related objectives<br>
<b>Analysis is the act of studying or</b><br>
examining something in detail, in order<br>
to discover or understand more about<br>
it. In many cases, analysis is associated<br>
with evaluation and interpretation of<br>
key issues. This simply means, giving<br>
opinion or judgment, which in many<br>
cases is supported with logical arguments<br>
about the quality and performance of<br>
the item being analysed. Interpretation<br>
involves sense making or attaching<br>
meanings and explanations to the results<br>
obtained. Financial statements analysis<br>
and interpretation therefore, involves<br>
different activities of rearranging data<br>
presented in the financial statements<br>
to represent a certain kind of logical<br>
relationships and do some computations<br>
using different types of accounting tools.<br>
<b>This process is concluded by doing some</b><br>
evaluation of obtained results, making<br>
judgement and drawing conclusions to<br>
support decision making by different<br>
users of accounting information.<br>
<b>The objectives of financial statements</b><br>
analysis<br>
<b>Let us consider a situation where a</b><br>
lender (bank) has been approached by<br>
an individual or company requiring<br>
loan to expand its business. Though<br>
banks’ main business is to advance<br>
loans to individuals and businesses,<br>
it will not extend loans carelessly<br>
without determining the loan applicant’s<br>
creditworthiness i.e., whether a person<br>
or company is suitable enough to receive<br>
financial credit (loan). The judgement<br>
for this considers the company’s ability<br>
<b>Financial statements</b><br>
analysis and<br>
interpretation<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter you will learn different concepts and principles related to financial<br>
statements analysis and interpretation. You will also learn about important tools<br>
used in financial statements analysis and how to interpret the results obtained<br>
from this process. The competencies developed will enable you to identify and<br>
describe important concepts and principles associated with financial statements<br>
analysis and interpretation, and apply them appropriate in generating information<br>
useful in decision making by different stakeholders interested in the affairs of the<br>
businesses.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
216<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
to repay back the borrowed funds with<br>
interest in the future. The answer for this<br>
can be determined using different ways<br>
including analysing the performance<br>
and financial position of the business<br>
being considered for a loan. Remember,<br>
financial statements provide important<br>
information about business activities i.e.,<br>
operating, financing and investing. When<br>
this information is subjected to further<br>
analysis it can provide useful insights on<br>
the creditworthiness of the business.<br>
<b>Another example; let us consider</b><br>
creditors or suppliers of goods or<br>
services on credit to the company. When<br>
approached to supply goods or services<br>
they will also need some assurance that,<br>
they will be paid in time or without much<br>
delay. How will they be able to determine<br>
this? Among others, financial statements<br>
analysis can provide some insights on<br>
the ability of the company to fulfil its<br>
credit repayment obligations as specified<br>
within the terms of credit. The difference<br>
between a bank and creditor is on the<br>
timing of repaying the credit obligations.<br>
<b>While many bank loans are of relatively</b><br>
longer time period, suppliers for goods<br>
and services on credit expect to be repaid<br>
within relatively shorter period say after<br>
a month, two or three. Therefore, though<br>
their objectives appear to be the same i.e.,<br>
to be repaid well in due time, the focus<br>
of their examination might be relatively<br>
different. For creditors, emphasis might<br>
be on short term ability while for bankers<br>
the long-run financial solvency of the<br>
company. Financial solvency is ability of<br>
the company to meet its long-term fixed<br>
expenses and to accomplish long-term<br>
expansion and growth. This might be<br>
important for the bank especially when it<br>
intends to issue long term loan.<br>
<b>Other examples may also be considered</b><br>
including potential investor. This might<br>
be a company or individual interested<br>
to acquire shares or part of the business<br>
by supplying additional capital. In<br>
this case, the potential investor is the<br>
prospective owner of the business,<br>
and not the lender. This person will be<br>
interested to know whether the business<br>
he/she intends to invest in has potential<br>
for profit generation and long – term<br>
sustainability. In other words, this person<br>
will need to be assured with the ability of<br>
the business to provide sufficient return<br>
from the invested funds. As observed<br>
for banker and creditor in the preceding<br>
discussions, the potential investors can<br>
obtain some useful information to assist<br>
in their investment decisions by carrying<br>
out financial statements analysis.<br>
<b>From the above, one can clearly see that,</b><br>
there are different objectives for doing<br>
financial statements analysis, depending<br>
on the objective of a person interested<br>
in the concerned business. Nonetheless,<br>
it is possible to classify the objectives of<br>
financial statements analysis to obtain<br>
broader categories that cut across the<br>
board. In this book, five major objectives<br>
of financial statements analysis are<br>
identified and discussed accordingly, as<br>
follows:<br>

(ii)	 Aid in decision making – financial<br>

to<br>

<b>Trend analysis is the analysis</b><br>
that consider the performance of<br>
business and its financial position<br>
over a span of time to evaluate<br>
the developments. The period<br>
under consideration may be five,<br>
eight, ten or even more years. This<br>
analysis provides a clue on what<br>
the future may hold.<br>

analysis<br>
helps<br>
in<br>
assessing<br>
and predicting bankruptcy and<br>
probability of business failure.<br>
<b>In business world, a business</b><br>
becomes bankrupt when it fails<br>
to operate as expected including<br>
failure to honour its financial<br>
obligations or make payment to<br>
its creditors or suppliers.<br>
(v)	 Assessment of the operational<br>
efficiency – financial statement<br>
analysis helps in judging the<br>
company’s efficiency in terms of<br>
its operations and management.<br>
<b>They help judge how well the</b><br>
company is able to utilize its<br>
assets and profit earning power.<br>
<b>As noted above, especially in objective</b><br>

financial statements analysis, there is<br>
a need of making comparisons using<br>
different bases. This is the subject that is<br>
being covered in the following section.<br>

<b>The need of making comparisons in</b><br>
financial statements analysis<br>
<b>Merely calculated ratios will not provide</b><br>
enough explanations about the financial<br>
performance and financial position of<br>
a business. For example, if financial<br>
ratio reveals that, a retail business has<br>
generated TZS 100,000 in sales revenue<br>
per square metre of floor space, this<br>
information would not be sufficient<br>
enough to tell whether this particular level<br>
of performance is good, bad or average.<br>
<b>However, when using ratios to compare</b><br>
with benchmark i.e., standard or point<br>
of reference against which our obtained<br>
ratio can be compared, it is possible to<br>
make meaningful interpretation on key<br>
issue of interest to our analysis. There<br>
are three important bases of comparison<br>
that are considered in this chapter. These<br>
include the following; past periods for<br>
the same business, comparison with<br>
other similar businesses in the same<br>
industry and planned performance for<br>
the business.<br>
<b>Past periods – assessing the trends in</b><br>
business performance<br>
<b>By comparing the same computed</b><br>
financial ratios between two or more<br>
financial periods of the company, it is<br>
possible to detect whether there has<br>
been an improvement or deterioration in<br>
performance over the period. Indeed, it is<br>
often useful to track particular ratios over<br>
time (say, five or ten years) to see whether<br>
it is possible to detect trends. This kind of<br>
analysis is usually termed as horizontal<br>
or trends analysis. For instance, as it will<br>
be discussed in the following section and<br>
subsequent ones, it is possible to calculate<br>
gross profit ratio in year one, compare<br>
it with the same computed ratio in year<br>
two and year three. Thereafter, observe<br>
the performance trend with respect to<br>
this ratio over the three years and then<br>
interpret those results to conclude in<br>
which year the gross profit is higher and<br>
the possible reasons for such results.<br>
<b>Similar</b><br>
businesses<br>
–<br>
assessing<br>
performance within the same industry<br>
<b>In a competitive environment, a business</b><br>
must consider its performance in relation<br>
to that of other businesses operating in<br>
the same industry. Survival may depend<br>
on its ability to achieve comparable<br>
levels of performance. A useful basis for<br>
comparing a particular ratio, therefore, is<br>
the ratio achieved by similar businesses<br>
during the same period. Alternatively,<br>
especially<br>
in<br>
developed<br>
countries,<br>
comparison<br>
can<br>
be<br>
done<br>
against<br>
the industry performance averages.<br>
<b>Industrial averages are standard industry</b><br>
performance ratios which are established<br>
by some dedicated service providers<br>
doing periodical analysis of performance<br>
in the industry.<br>
<b>Planned performance as bases for</b><br>
controlling business<br>
<b>Planned performance in the form of ratios</b><br>
may be used as basis for exercising control<br>
of business. This is usually achieved by<br>
comparing the targets that management<br>
developed before the start of the period<br>

under review against actual results. This may be a useful way of revealing the level<br>

Financial ratios as a tool for financial statements analysis<br>
Generally, ratios express a mathematical relationship between one quantity and<br>
another. They show the number of times one value contains or is contained within<br>
the other. The relationship between the quantities or values is usually expressed in<br>
the form of  ; the result can be presented as a percentage/rate/simple proportion/<br>
fraction (e.g., 40%; 1:2; ¼), respectively. Therefore, financial ratios attempt to<br>
measure the relationships among selected financial data extracted from a given set<br>
of financial statements – reflecting the concept of breaking down data to provide a<br>
more refined result.<br>
<b>Usefulness of ratio analysis</b><br>
When financial ratios are logically interpreted, they can be very informative for<br>
different types of decision-making problems. This is because financial ratios<br>
simplify task of making comparison of business performance either by looking into<br>
the achievement made by the business over time (trend analysis) or by comparing<br>
with other similar businesses (cross sectional) or simply by looking at the already<br>
established industry performance levels.<br>
Ratios are also useful where businesses under consideration are of different sizes<br>
especially in terms of capital employed to support business operations. In the<br>
a<br>
b<br>

<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
220<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
absence of ratios, it would be very difficult to make meaningful comparison. In other<br>

<b>Liquidity ratios</b><br>
Liquidity refers to how easily a particular category of assets can be converted into<br>
cash. Generally, current assets including debtors and stocks/inventory are considered<br>
as more liquid asset since they can be converted into cash within a relatively shorter<br>
period of time. However, non–current assets like property, plant and equipment are<br>
not as easily convertible into cash. Best financial management practices involve<br>
being aware of the extent to which the current assets owned by your business can<br>
easily be turned into cash. This is crucial since the liquidity of your business is very<br>
important in judging how quickly the business can be able to settle its short term<br>
repayment obligations not only relating to current liabilities but also the long-term<br>
liability as they become current i.e., ready to be settled off.<br>
Remember by definition, current liabilities are supposed to be settled within a near<br>
future; not exceeding the period of 12 months or a year. It is the cash and other near<br>
cash items (highly liquid assets) that can be useful in paying debts under current<br>
liabilities. Liquidity ratios have been designed to assess the ability of the firm to<br>
fulfil its current or near future repayment obligations without major problems.<br>
Notably, liquidity position is not defined by only hard cash – besides cash in hands,<br>
it includes bank deposits and other current assets which can easily be converted into<br>
cash. They include not only debtors and stocks /inventory but also different types<br>
of short – term financial securities also known as marketable securities like treasury<br>
bills as well as high-yield savings accounts.<br>
Liquidity ratios are of particular interest to creditors or suppliers of goods and<br>
services. Others include employees and lenders/banks. The latter would be interested<br>
specially to judge the ability of the business to service loan i.e., paying interest<br>
charges and portion of the principal amount in due time. Meanwhile, creditors are<br>
interested because they expect the business to pay within relatively shorter period so<br>
that they can continue having sufficient amount of working capital to support their<br>
business. For employees, they want to know whether their employer will be able to<br>
pay for their salaries and other benefits without much delay.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
222<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Types of liquidity ratios</b><br>
There are two major types of liquidity ratios namely current ratio and quick ratio.<br>
Current ratio is computed by relating the total current assets to total current<br>
liabilities. It is assumed that, current assets will be converted into cash to meet or<br>
settle current liabilities when due. Its formula is presented as follows:<br>
<b>Current assets</b><br>
<b>Current ratio =</b><br>
<b>Current liabilities</b><br>
How is current ratio interpreted? Conventionally, a number greater than 1 is much<br>
preferred. This indicates that, the business can settle its current liabilities without<br>
any difficulty. Ratio that is less than 1 is closely associated with potential problems<br>
in meeting payment obligations in due time.<br>

<b>Answer to part (b)</b><br>
Note that, when you are asked to provide comments, basically you are expected to<br>
give interpretation about the meaning behind the obtained results. The key issue is,<br>
what does the ratio tell you? Giving comments also means you should give an eval­<br>
uation. For example, is the result obtained good enough – if not why? Your answer<br>
should resemble the one that follows:<br>
Current ratio is a useful test of the short-term-debt paying ability of the business.<br>
Generally, a ratio of 1:1 or higher is considered as satisfactory. In our case, Mbongo<br>
Plc has a current ratio of 2.75:1, which means that, its current assets are 2.75 times<br>
higher than its current liabilities. Thus, the company can pay off its current liabilities<br>
successfully, by 2.75 times, and still remain with satisfactory level of liquidity to<br>
support its ongoing business operations.<br>
However, in real world of business, financial analysts usually act cautiously with<br>
the derived ratios like the one obtained above. Usually, they will expand their as­<br>
sessment to consider the nature and quality of individual current assets and current<br>
liabilities. Sometimes, it may happen that, significant part of current assets consists<br>
of slow-moving items. The inventory of this nature usually takes longer time to<br>
sale or simply they are obsolete. In this case, the business with high current ratio of<br>
that nature may not be able to pay its current liabilities as they become due. To the<br>
contrary, business with low current ratio may be able to pay its current obligations<br>
promptly as they become due if a large portion of its current assets consists of high­<br>
ly liquid assets such as cash, bank balance, marketable securities and fast-moving<br>
inventories.<br>
Quick ratio is also known as acid test ratio; it is designed to measure the ability<br>
of business to pay its short-term debts very fast – using its most liquid assets only.<br>
The least liquid current asset like inventory or prepayments (prepaid expenses) are<br>
excluded. This is because the sales of finished goods depend highly on customer<br>
demands. If the need for the good is low, then the inventory stock will increase and<br>
not be quickly converted into cash. As for prepaid expenses, these are not expected<br>

<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>

<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>

to be translated into cash. Back to our earlier example of Mbongo Plc, the least liq­<br>
uid current thus would be inventory/stock since it will take longer time to convert<br>
it into cash compared to debtors. The highly liquid assets for computing quick ratio<br>
thus include, accounts receivable and cash only. This ratio measures the quality of<br>
current assets rather than quantity.<br>

Suppose a quick ratio for Mbongo Plc was less than 1, say 0.5:1. This would indicate<br>

liquid assets. As discussed in current ratio, this ratio should also be interpreted<br>

assurance that, the business under consideration is having a strong liquidity position.<br>
It is very important to examine closely the quality of some current assets, in our<br>
case, the debtors to see the potential for business to collect the debts in due time.<br>
Debts which have remained overdue for relatively longer period than agreed are<br>
more likely to default. A particular business may have a low value debtors hence<br>
low quick ratio but if, it has significant number of fast-moving inventories, it may<br>
end up being very efficient in paying its debts as they fall due compared to one that<br>
has higher quick ratio. As a rational financial analyst, it is therefore very important<br>
to exercise high level of professionalism and good judgement to understand and<br>
evaluate well the liquidity of a business.<br>
Another important issue to note is that, there is a limit for usefulness of higher quick<br>
ratio in judging the financial position of the business. A higher quick ratio, say of<br>
4:1 is not recommendable since it indicates that, the business is holding too much<br>
money in its hands. This money could have been used wisely to generate more<br>
wealth for the owners. For example, it could be invested in shorter term financial<br>
securities such as treasury bills, which can generate returns within relatively shorter<br>
period, in 3 to 12 months’ period.<br>
<b>Profitability ratios</b><br>
Profitability ratios measure the ability of a business to generate profit i.e., revenue<br>
in excess of expenses. Profitability ratios attempt to measure the overall efficiency<br>
in using economic resources or assets employed to support business operations. The<br>
ratios attempt to measure the rate of profitability per shilling value of sales or assets.<br>
The figures used to compute most of the profitability ratios exclude interest and tax<br>
charges thus the term operating profit or Earnings Before Interest and Tax (EBIT).<br>
This is done purposely to avoid or isolate the effect of financing decisions and tax<br>
policies on operational performance related decisions. In other words, the use of<br>
EBIT is done to avoid distortions, which may arise due to having different capital<br>
structures and taxation rules, which may differ across companies. Capital structure is<br>
nothing but the combinations of equity versus debt in financing business operations.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
226<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
Who are interested in profitability ratios? These ratios are of primary interest to<br>
both owners and management of the company. Other external users of accounting<br>
information like lenders are also interested in these ratios because in the long–run,<br>
they indicate the sustainability and going concern of the business. Profitability ratios<br>
can be divided into two major categories namely profit margins and return ratios. The<br>
profit margins represent the firm’s ability to translate sales revenue into profits while<br>
returns ratios are used to evaluate its overall ability to generate adequate profits for<br>
the owners of the business. In this book, five major categories of profitability ratios<br>
mostly used in businesses decisions are considered. They include the following:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Gross profit margin,</b><br>
(ii)	 Operating profit margin,<br>
(iii)	 Net profit margin,<br>
(iv)	 Return on assets (ROA),<br>
(v)	 Return on equity (ROE), and<br>
(vi)	 Return on capital employed (ROCE).<br>
<b>Gross profit margin</b><br>
Gross profit margin is used to measure business profitability by relating two<br>
financial items namely, gross profit and the company’s sales revenue. The gross<br>
profit is usually determined in the income statement. It reflects the residual of net<br>
sales minus cost of goods sold (COGS). Interpretatively, the larger the gross profit<br>
margin the better for the business and its owners. This ratio is computed by taking<br>
gross profit divide by net sales – usually presented in percentage.<br>
<b>Operating profit margin</b><br>
Operating profit refers to the profit before interest and tax, also known as earnings/<br>
profit before interest and tax (EBIT). Operating profit is derived by deducting operating<br>
expenses from gross profit. Examples of operating expenses incurred in generating<br>
income include, rent, wages and salaries, electricity and insurance. Operating profit<br>
margin ratio is determined by relating operating profit to sales revenue of a business.<br>
Among others, ability of the management to control its operating expenses can be<br>
judged using this ratio, which is associated with company’s operational efficiency.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
227<br>
<b>Net profit margin</b><br>
Net profit margin ratio is the percentage of firm’s net profit relative to its sales<br>
revenue. This ratio is different from operating profit ratio because it is determined<br>
by incorporating interest expenses and income tax. This ratio therefore shows exactly<br>
the proportion of each shilling of sales revenue counted as net income after all the<br>
expenses have been paid.<br>

net sales while the cost of inventory or merchandise sold is TZS 71.25 out of TZS<br>
100 net sales, i.e., net sales = gross profit + cost of goods sold. In other words, the<br>
percentage margin is 28.75% on net sales and is available now to cover for operating<br>

Do they indicate good or poor performance? Note that for all three types of profit<br>
margins, the higher the ratios, the better, and lower ratios are not preferred. However,<br>
to provide more useful answer, more information will be needed including, comparing<br>
with what has been achieved in the past. Based on this, the management and other<br>
stakeholders will prefer higher ratios that keep on increasing over time. Another<br>
benchmark would be the average performance for the industry. If these ratios are<br>
well above the average performance in the industry, then we could state confidently<br>
that, the company is doing better compared to the industry average performance.<br>
<b>Return on assets</b><br>
Return on assets (ROA) is calculated by dividing net operating income (also known<br>
as earnings before interest and tax (EBIT) by total assets. This ratio is normally<br>
expressed in percentage (EBIT/Total Assets x 100%). Interpretatively, ROA<br>
attempts to measure how efficiently a business is able to utilize its total assets to<br>
generate profit during a particular accounting period. Since most business’s assets<br>
are maintained for one important objective; to generate revenue and hence profit, this<br>
ratio helps both management and investors to see how well the business converts its<br>
investments in assets into profits.<br>
<b>Return on equity</b><br>
Return on equity (ROE) is calculated by taking net profit after interest and tax divide<br>
by shareholders’ equity. This ratio is of great interest to investors or shareholders<br>
due to its ability to assess the return on their invested funds. Potential shareholders<br>
and investors are also interested in this ratio because it can assist them to forecast<br>
the potential capability of business to continue generating income. If what they see<br>
is a bright future, then they can rationally decide to invest in a particular business<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
230<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
of their interest. As it is for other profitability ratios, the higher the ROE the better<br>
for both existing and potential shareholders and investors. For management, ROE is<br>
equally important since it reflects their performance, whether they have acted in the<br>
best interest of shareholders and other types of investors.<br>
<b>Return on capital employed</b><br>
Return on capital employed (ROCE) is a profitability ratio that measures how<br>
efficient a business is, in generating profits from employed capital. The computations<br>
involve dividing the net operating profit by capital employed. Capital employed<br>
can be defined in different ways. One among the most used approach is, adding<br>
shareholders’ equity and all long-term borrowings (non-current liabilities).<br>
Alternatively, you can subtract current liabilities from total assets to obtain the same<br>
results. In computing ROCE, the measure of profit that is recommended to use is, the<br>
earnings before interest and tax (EBIT). Note that, interest expenses (i.e., the cost<br>
of loaned capital) and tax (i.e., corporate tax) are not part and parcel of operating<br>
expenses. The two are excluded to avoid mixing them with operating expenses<br>
hence limiting our evaluation to the operational efficiency of the business. It is also<br>
seen as the best practice because it isolate the effect of different decisions namely,<br>
operational versus financing decisions.<br>
<b>Return on capital employed = Profit before interest and tax</b><br>
<b>Capital employed</b><br>

<b>Financial leverage or solvency ratios</b><br>
Financial leverage or solvency ratios have been designed to assess the ability of a<br>
company to pay its long-term liabilities such as debt and the interest on that debt.<br>
These ratios attempt to measure financial health of the company in terms of its ability<br>
to manage operations into the foreseeable future. Ratios at this level focus more on<br>
the capital structure of the business i.e. the combinations or proportions of equity<br>
and debt used by the business to finance its assets and overall operations. Therefore,<br>
financial leverage is concerned with the need to know the overall debt load of a<br>
company compared to its assets and owners’ equity. This is useful as it reflects how<br>
much of the business assets are actually owned by the shareholders or creditors. If<br>
owners’ equity is higher than overall debts, shareholders are considered as owning<br>
the majority of the company’s assets.<br>
Consider the accounting equation where A = C + L (A = Assets; C = Capital and L =<br>
Liabilities). Supposed L = 0, it means A = C; the financial leverage of this business<br>
equals to zero. On the other hand, the business that is highly leverages means it has<br>
more debts than equity. Financial leverage ratios are of major interest to creditors<br>
and investors due to their ability to indicate the risks involved when advancing<br>
loans or making investment decisions. For example, a bank that is approached by a<br>
business for a loan, among others, will use financial leverage ratios to see the extent<br>
to which the business is overburdened by other liabilities. Thereafter, it will decide<br>
whether to advance loan to a business or not. Financial leverage ratios that are used<br>
in this kind of decision include, debt ratio, equity ratio, debt to equity ratio, capital<br>
gearing ratio and interest cover/coverage ratio. The latter is also known as times<br>
interest earned ratio (TIE).<br>
The basic formula to compute different types of financial leverage ratios are<br>
presented in the following section.<br>

<b>How do we interpret financial leverage ratios?</b><br>
Different interpretations are associated with each type of financial leverage ratios.<br>
An overview of the meanings of each ratio also shedding some lights on how to<br>
interpret the identified ratios is given in table 8.1 that follows.<br>
Table 8. 1: Financial leverage ratios and their interpretations<br>
<b>S/N</b><br>
<b>Type of ratio</b><br>
<b>Meanings/interpretations</b><br>
1.<br>
<b>Debt ratio</b><br>
<b>Indicates the percentage of assets financed by debt as</b><br>
opposed to equity.<br>
2.<br>
<b>Equity ratio</b><br>
<b>Indicates the percentage of assets financed by owners’</b><br>
equity as opposed to liabilities or debts.<br>
3.<br>
<b>Debt to equity ratio</b><br>
<b>Indicates the relationship between total liabilities</b><br>
and total equity. It echoes the relationship of capital<br>
structure items of the business. A capital structure is<br>
a combination of equity and debt that makes up the<br>
financing of a company or business<br>
4.<br>
<b>Capital gearing ratio</b><br>
<b>Expresses the relationship between a company’s long</b><br>
– term borrowings and its owners’ funds or capital.<br>
5.<br>
Interest cover or TIE Indicates the number of times, the profit covers the<br>
interest charge – the higher the ratio the better. This<br>
ratio is very important to the lenders because it help<br>
them to know if the client is credit-worthy and will<br>
be able to repay the principal and interest.<br>

Meanings and interpretation from computed financial leverage ratios<br>
As for the debt and equity ratios, the debt ratio of 46% means that, lenders have<br>
contributed 46% of the total assets of ABC Company while the remaining 54% has<br>
been contributed by owners or shareholders. With respect to debt-to-equity ratio,<br>
the ratio stands at 85% (i.e., 0.85 or 0.85: 1), which can be interpreted as, for every<br>
TZS 85/= debt in the capital structure of the business, a corresponding TZS 100<br>
is equity or shareholders’ contribution. More specifically, it implies that for every<br>
TZS 100 in the capital structure of a business, TZS 54.05 or 54.05% (computed as<br>
100/185 x100) come from equity financing and TZS 45.95 or 45.95% (computed as<br>
85/185 x 100) come from debt financing. A lower debt to equity ratio indicates that<br>
the business is receiving lower amount of financing from lenders or creditors versus<br>
funding through equity via shareholders, and vice versa.<br>
What does the capital structure as provided by the above ratios translate? Generally,<br>
a high equity ratio and a low debt ratio indicates a strong financial position of the<br>
company and greater security for creditors. In the example given, ABC Company<br>
has equity ratio of about 54%, which is more likely to be considered favourably.<br>
This is in line with the general understanding that equity ratio of 50% is a reasonable<br>
ratio. This means that, less than 50% (to be specific, about 46%) of ABC Company<br>
are owned by creditors. Suppose, the equity ratio would have been very low, say 15<br>

percent. This would indicate that, the company is heavily depending on creditors/<br>
debts to support its business operations, which is very risky.<br>
A large portion of debts in the total capital employed are more likely to reduce<br>
creditors’ interest to invest more in the company. It will also mean that, the company<br>
is incurring significant amount of costs in servicing loans (interest expenses). Failure<br>
to meet this obligation would results into going concerns problem. The same can be<br>
said with regard to debt to equity ratio. A ratio of 1 or 1:1 means that, creditors and<br>
stockholders equally contribute to the assets of the business. Debt to equity ratio of<br>
less than 1 indicates that the portion of financing provided by shareholders is greater<br>
than the portion of financing provided by lenders or creditors. Alternatively, debt<br>
to equity ratio that is greater than 1 indicates that the portion of assets provided by<br>
creditors is greater than the portion of assets provided by stockholders.<br>
Generally, creditors would like to see a low debt to equity ratio because a low ratio<br>
i.e., less than 1 means greater protection for their money. However, it should be<br>
understood that, company should think of the balance or proper mix between debts<br>
and equity. Taking reasonable amount of loans/debts may prove to be beneficial<br>
when the objective is to increase shareholders’ wealth if they can be used efficiently.<br>
This may be the case to the extent that, borrowed funds are injected into business<br>
operations to generate profit, which is greater than the cost of borrowed funds (i.e.,<br>
interest expenses).<br>
However, it is important to note that, the importance of debt to equity ratios<br>
varies from one industry to another. This is because, different norms have been<br>
developed and used for different industries. Ratio that is ideal for one industry may<br>
be worrisome for another industry. For example, in the banking industry, the debt to<br>
equity ratio is inevitably very high in order to finance the lending business. Banks<br>
have to collect more and more deposits and savings (which are liabilities or debts<br>
to the banks) in order to build up sufficient loanable funds for on-lending purposes.<br>
Shareholders contribute a small proportion of the bank financing just for finance<br>
initial start-up activities while most of the funding is raised from the public. Thus,<br>
it is not surprising that, the debt to equity ratio in banks are very high, sometimes<br>
at the ratio of 9:1 depending on the minimum capital requirement mandated by the<br>
banking regulatory authorities. Moreover, in some businesses, the debt to equity<br>
ratio of 2.5: 1 is acceptable. Nonetheless, a debt to equity ratio of 1:1 is normally<br>
considered as satisfactory for most of the companies to indicate that, they are less<br>
risky by lenders.<br>

<b>Example 8.6</b><br>
This example focuses on interest coverage ratio. Let us assume you are still working<br>
to decide on the creditworthiness of ABC Company. However, your supervisor is<br>
now interested to know the interest coverage ratio of the client and the interpreta­<br>
tions arising out of it. Additional information to assist you in this regard is also given<br>
as follows:<br>
<b>Net operating income</b><br>
2,570,000<br>
<b>Interest expenses</b><br>
(320,000)<br>
<b>Net income before tax</b><br>
2,250,000<br>
<b>Income tax (30%)</b><br>
675,000<br>
<b>Net income</b><br>
1,575,000<br>
<b>Solution:</b><br>
<b>Interest coverage/TIE ratio =</b><br>
<b>Interpretations</b><br>
TIE ratio of 8.03 times for ABC Company means that, the interest expenses of the<br>
company are 8.03 times covered by its net operating income before interest and tax<br>
(EBIT). This ratio is very important for lenders – the MCBL Bank Ltd in our case.<br>
A high TIE ratio ensures a periodical interest income for lenders while the company<br>
with low TIE ratio indicates that, it may face difficulties in raising funds from their<br>
business operations to finance its interest expenses. Fortunately, this appears not to<br>
be the case for ABC Company.<br>
As a general rule, a ratio of 2 or higher is considered to be adequate to protect the<br>
creditors’ interest in the business. A ratio of less than 1 means the company is likely<br>
to have problems in paying interest on its borrowings. However, a very high TIE<br>
may be the result of the fact that the company is unnecessarily careful about its debts<br>
and is not taking full advantage of the credit facilities available to them to support<br>
its business operations:<br>
<b>Profit before interest and tax  x1 00%</b><br>
<b>Interest expenses</b><br>
2,570,000  x1 00%<br>
320,000<br>
<b>Interest coverage or TIE = 8.03 times</b><br>
=<br>

<b>Efficiency/Activity ratios</b><br>
Activity or efficiency ratios attempt to measure how well the business utilizes its<br>
assets to generate sales. Some of the ratios also indicate how a company is able to<br>
manage well its liabilities. Turnover ratio is another common name used to identify<br>
ratios in this category especially those attempting to indicate the number of times<br>
particular assets convert into cash or sales. Typical activity or efficiency ratios in­<br>
clude the following:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Inventory ratio or stock turnover (stock velocity),</b><br>
(ii)<br>
Debtors’ turnover ratio or receivable turnover (debtor’s velocity),<br>
(iii)	 Debtors’ collection period,<br>
(iv)	 Creditors’ turnover ratio or payable turnover ratio (creditor’s velocity),<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Debt payment period, and</b><br>
(vi)	 Total assets turnover.<br>

associated with each other are presented in table 8.2 that follows:<br>

Further analysis to evaluate the performance of Pendeza Textile Company<br>
A thorough understanding of how Pendeza Textile Company performs would need<br>
knowing other important issues surrounding its business operations. Generally<br>
speaking, a high inventory turnover ratio indicates fast moving inventories while<br>
low ratio indicates slow moving or obsolete inventories in stock. A low ratio may<br>
also be the result of maintaining excessive inventories needlessly. Maintaining<br>
excessive inventories unnecessarily indicates poor inventory management because<br>
it involves tiding up funds that could be used in other business operations. However,<br>
this kind of interpretations should also compare with general experiences observed<br>
in similar companies operating in the same industry. Average industry performance<br>
ratios would indicate whether Pendeza Textile Company is performing well or not.<br>
For example, if the industry ratio is 35 times a year, then it will be possible to<br>
conclude that, Pendeza Textile Company is underperforming.<br>

245<br>
Nonetheless, comparisons among the different ratios indicate that, the company is<br>
not performing well. For example, significant differences or gaps can be highlighted<br>
by looking into inventory turnover ratios versus debtors’ turnover and collection<br>
periods. While the company sales more frequently its stock of goods (29.5 after<br>
every 12.4 days), the collection efforts lag behind – it takes about 3.9 frequencies<br>
a year, i.e., after every 3.1 months. Differences are also observed with regard to<br>
debtors’ turnover ratios versus creditors’ turnover ratios. Poor performance for<br>
debtors’ turnover ratios, as already shown, exceed the creditors turnover ratios. The<br>
analysis shows that creditors are being paid 1.5 in a year – roughly after every 8<br>
months. Generally, it is expected that, the two types of turnover ratios (i.e., debtors’<br>
turnover ratios and creditors’ turnover ratios) should not differ significantly from<br>
each other. Why? Because cash collected from sales proceeds (which include goods<br>
sold on credit to debtors) is the ones which will be used in liquidating the company’s<br>
debts namely, the trade creditors who supplied the goods sold by the company.<br>
For activity and efficiency ratios, appropriate recommendations would need the<br>
company to review its credit policies and terms of payment, among others. The<br>
objective should be to reduce the credit period for customers such that it does not<br>
exceed one-month period if Pendeza Textile Company is working in groceries/<br>
apparel industry. Collection efforts should also be intensified in order to have<br>
sufficient funds to pay for creditors. Thus, it is important to reduce the payment<br>
period closer to creditors’ payment period – at least a month. This would in turn<br>
improve the image and creditworthiness of Pendeza Textile Company before its<br>
creditors.<br>
With asset turnover ratio, an average of 0.28 sales to total assets ratio – again by<br>
considering past performance of the same company and the performance of other<br>
similar companies in the same industry, one can better judge whether the ratio of<br>
<b>0.28 is good or not. If in the past, for example, Pendeza Textile Company was able to</b><br>
efficiently produce total assets turnover ratio of 1.2 times, which means, the company<br>
was able to generate TZS 120 of sales for every TZS 100 invested in assets – we<br>
could say for the current year (2021), Pendeza Textile Company performed poorly.<br>
Lastly, it is worth noting that, a more comprehensive evaluation could be achieved<br>
if industry standards/benchmarks were available.<br>

<b>Market prospects and investor performance ratios</b><br>
Investor performance or market prospects ratios have been designed to support<br>
different investment decisions. By using these ratios, shareholders and potential<br>
investors can evaluate the performance of business in terms of its shares value<br>
and expected returns. These ratios incorporate the notion of markets because their<br>
computation requires the use of some financial data from stock exchange market<br>
i.e., an organized marketplace where the sale and purchase of securities such as<br>
shares, stocks, and bonds take place. In Tanzania, this refers to the Dar es Salaam<br>
Stock Exchange (DSE), which was incorporated on 19th September 1996. Some<br>
of the commonly used investor/market-based ratios include the following:<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>

<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
247<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Earnings per share ratio,</b><br>
(ii)	 Price – earnings ratio,<br>
(iii)	 Dividend per share ratio,<br>
(iv)	 Dividend yield ratio, and<br>
(v)<br>
Dividend cover ratio.<br>
Further discussion of different types of investor or market prospects ratios is<br>
considered in the subsections that follow.<br>
<b>Earnings per share (EPS)</b><br>
Earnings per share (EPS) ratio indicates the amount which an entity has earned per<br>
share for the given period. This ratio attempts to show the value of shares by relating<br>
it with the amount of profits made during a particular period. The ratio considers all<br>
the profits available to each share whether distributed as dividend or retained for<br>
reinvestment in the business. It can be determined using the following formula<br>
<b>Earnings per share = Net income after interest and taxes</b><br>
<b>Number of ordinary shares</b><br>
For example, if a company with total ordinary shares of 20 million has earned net<br>
profit after interest and tax of TZS 600 million, what would be its EPS?<br>
<b>EPS = TZS 30 per share</b><br>
The obtained result shows that, for every ordinary share the company is able to pay a<br>
dividend of TZS 30, if all the profits after interest and tax are distributed as dividends<br>
to shareholders. However, in practice, companies usually do not distribute all the<br>
profits to its shareholders. Some of the profit after interest and tax is kept or retained<br>
in the company to be reinvested for new projects or expansion of business activities.<br>
It is shown in the statement of financial position as retained profit/earnings.<br>
<b>Price earnings ratio</b><br>
Price earnings ratio (P/E ratio) can also be termed as price to earnings ratio. This is<br>
a common financial ratio used for valuing a company by measuring its current share<br>
price relative to its earnings per share (EPS), which has already been explained. This<br>
ratio helps in assessing the relative risk of an investment and is determined by using<br>

20,000,000<br>
=<br>

the following formula:<br>
For example, consider the current market price of a company’s share as TZS 1,500/=<br>
while earnings per share is TZS 300 – in this case the P/E ratio would be as follows:<br>
<b>P/E ratio = 5</b><br>
P/E ratio of 5 indicates that it would take an investor an average of 5 years to recover<br>
the cost of the share. It also implies that; the purchaser of the share is investing<br>
TZS 5 per any shilling for annual earning. This interpretation assumes that, all<br>
things would remain the same i.e., no changes in future company’s performance in<br>
comparison to the current year. For a rational investor however, before making any<br>
investment decision he/she would need to compare this figure with other companies’<br>
P/E ratios. Generally, the higher the P/E ratio, the better the expectations of the<br>
company’s future profitability.<br>
<b>Dividends per share (DPS)</b><br>
Dividends per share (DPS) attempts to measure the size of the dividends that the<br>
company actually pays to its shareholders, calculated as follows:<br>
For example, a company with after interest and tax profit of TZS 6,000,000,000<br>
decides to issue 25% of this profit as dividends. Let us assume that, this company<br>
has issued a total of 40 million ordinary shares. For this company the DPS would be<br>
determined as follows:<br>
<b>DPS = TZS 37.5/=</b><br>
This means that every ordinary share would pay a dividend of TZS 37.5 while the<br>
remaining TZS 4.5 billion (TZS 4,500,000,000/=) of the profit after interest and tax<br>
would be retained for future investment (retained earnings). Clearly, some of the<br>
shareholders would want the dividend per share to be as high as possible to finance<br>
their personal expenditures. However, the dividends to be paid is usually determined<br>
<b>Market price per share</b><br>
<b>P/E ratio =</b><br>
<b>Earnings per share</b><br>
1,500<br>
<b>P/E ratio =</b><br>

=<br>
<b>TZS 6,000,000,000  x  25%</b><br>
<b>DPS</b><br>
40,000,000<br>

by board of directors. Among others, their responsibility includes, balancing different<br>

<b>Dividend yield ratio</b><br>
Dividend yield measures the relationship between dividend per share (DPS) and<br>
the market price of the share. This ratio attempts to evaluate the return on capital<br>
investment as a percentage of market prices, calculated as follows:<br>
For example, reflecting more on our previous example where the company paid a<br>
dividend per share of TZS 37.5. Now, assume that you have additional information<br>
where the share market price of the same company is TZS 750. From this, the<br>
dividend yield would be computed as follows:<br>
37.5<br>
<b>DY</b><br>
x  100%<br>
750<br>
=<br>
<b>DY = 5%</b><br>
Indeed, the obtained result of 5% is not a very high return considering the risk<br>
involved in investing money in shares. However, this figure would need to be<br>
compared to other investments (e.g., other companies, banks, etc.) to see if it is<br>
providing a competitive return.<br>
<b>Dividend cover ratio</b><br>
The dividend cover or coverage ratio measures the number of times that a company<br>
can pay dividends to its shareholders. The ratio is useful especially for investors in<br>
estimating the risk of not receiving dividends. Interpretatively, if a company has a<br>
high proportion of net income to its total annual amount of dividend payments, there<br>
is a low risk that the business will not be able to continue making dividend payments<br>
of the same amount. Conversely, if the ratio is less than one, the business may be<br>
borrowing money in order to make dividend payments, which is not sustainable.<br>
The formula to calculate dividend cover ratio is given as follows:<br>
<b>Dividend per share</b><br>
<b>DY</b><br>
x  100%<br>
<b>Market price per share</b><br>
=<br>
<b>Net profit after interest and tax</b><br>
<b>Dividend cover</b><br>
<b>Annual dividend</b><br>
=<br>
<b>Earnings per share</b><br>
<b>Or</b><br>
<b>Dividend per share</b><br>
=<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>

<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>

For example, if a business is having a profit after interest and tax of TZS 600 million<br>
and paid a total dividend of TZS 150 million, what will be the dividend cover ratio?<br>
<b>The answer to this is presented as follows:</b><br>
<b>Dividend cover = 4 times</b><br>
Dividend cover ratio of 4 times indicates that, the company did not pay the<br>
shareholders a significant proportion of the profit after tax in the form of dividends.<br>
The company has actually paid a quarter of their profit after tax as dividends. This<br>
means, the company retained much of the profit after tax as retained profit probably<br>
for re-investment purposes. The amount paid as dividend usually depends on the<br>
company dividend policies and other investment decisions. This may include,<br>
deciding whether to use more of the internally generated funds for investments or<br>
use externally acquired funds. External providers of funds may include, banks and<br>
other types of financial institutions. Dividend decisions are made by the company<br>
board of directors, which represent the interest of all shareholders.<br>

<b>Limitations of financial ratios</b><br>
Although ratios offer a quick and useful method for analysing the performance and<br>
financial position of a business, there are some limitations and problems that one<br>
should be aware of. This is important because failure to understand the inherent<br>
limitations may not only affect the quality of results but also the interpretation derived<br>
from the analysis. Being aware of the limitations will assist one to find or take the<br>
necessary measure to minimize their effect and interpret the results cautiously. The<br>
common limitations and problems involved are considered below:<br>
600,000,000<br>
<b>Dividend cover</b><br>
150,000,000<br>
=<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
251<br>
<b>Absence of qualitative data and</b><br>
information<br>
<b>Financial statements do not fully</b><br>
incorporate qualitative characteristics<br>
of the business. For example, internally<br>
generated goodwill and brands are not<br>
included in the statement of financial<br>
position because they fail to meet the<br>
strict definition of an asset. Factors like<br>
these may be important in interpretation<br>
of financial statements, absence of<br>
which may not give a full picture of<br>
why a particular company is doing well<br>
compared to the other.<br>
<b>Non–uniformity in reporting periods</b><br>
<b>When the businesses report their</b><br>
financial results using different time<br>
period, comparison of the results may<br>
also be limited.<br>
<b>Non–uniformity in accounting</b><br>
estimates and policies<br>
<b>Companies</b><br>
may<br>
choose<br>
different<br>
accounting policies, which can have<br>
a significant effect on reported profits<br>
and asset values (for example, different<br>
methods of calculating depreciation<br>
or valuing inventories). This can<br>
result into incomparability of results<br>
of the company when doing financial<br>
statements analysis.<br>
<b>Inflation</b><br>
<b>Conventional</b><br>
financial<br>
statements<br>
do not take into account the effect<br>
of inflation when reporting financial<br>
performance and financial positions of<br>
the entity. Inflation is the rising of prices<br>
of goods and services due to varying<br>
reasons including raising cost of goods<br>
and increasing demands of goods and<br>
services. Increasing prices may in turn<br>
distort the real values reflected in figures<br>
on which the ratios are based. Distorted<br>
values due to inflation may affect<br>
interpretation of the analysed figures.<br>
<b>This chapter has covered different</b><br>
issues related to the analysis and<br>
interpretation of financial statements.<br>
<b>In total, five categories of financial</b><br>
ratios have been identified including,<br>
liquidity ratios, profitability ratios,<br>
financial leverage ratios, activity/<br>
efficiency ratios and investor/market-<br>
based ratios. These ratios provide<br>
logical relationships between items<br>
found within the income statements<br>
as well as those found within the<br>
statement of financial position. For<br>
example, liquidity ratios indicate the<br>
ability of the business to meet its<br>
short-term financial obligations while<br>
profitability ratios show the ability<br>
of the business to translate sales into<br>
profits. Other types of profitability<br>
ratios attempt to relate profit for the<br>
period to assets or owners’ funds.<br>
<b>Ratios of this nature aim to show the</b><br>
effectiveness of the business in using<br>
its assets in general and particularly,<br>
the owners’ funds in generating profit.<br>
<b>Other important ratios like activity/</b><br>
efficiency ratios are designed to<br>

<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
evaluate the efficiency with which<br>
a business uses its assets to generate<br>
cash and sales. Within the chapter it<br>
has also been shown that, financial<br>
leverage ratios focus on evaluating the<br>
capital structure of the business and<br>
the extent to which business uses funds<br>
from owners’ and external financiers<br>
(e.g. lenders and credit suppliers)<br>
to support business operations. The<br>
chapter ends by considering investor<br>
performance or market prospects<br>
ratios. Market based ratios intend<br>
to<br>
inform<br>
existing<br>
shareholders<br>
and potential investors in making<br>
financing and investment decisions.<br>

11.	 A company has capital of 100 million ordinary shares of TZS 100 each. It pays<br>
a dividend of 6% out of its profits after tax of TZS 48,000,000 on sales of TZS<br>
400 million. The market price of the shares is TZS 240.<br>
