<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Nine</b><br>
<b>Investment</b><br>
accounts<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter, you will learn about the concepts and principles of investments<br>
undertaken by business entities. You will also learn some techniques used to record<br>
acquisition and disposal of financial investments of a business. The competencies<br>
developed in this chapter will enable you to describe the basic concepts and<br>
principles related with financial investments. You will also be able to prepare the<br>
basic financial records in the books of accounts.<br>
<b>Meaning of investment and other</b><br>
associated terms<br>
<b>An investment is an asset or item</b><br>
acquired with the goal of generating<br>
income or appreciation. Appreciation<br>
refers to an increase in the value of an<br>
asset over time. When an individual<br>
purchases an asset as investment, the<br>
intention is not to consume such asset<br>
rather using it in the future to create<br>
wealth. The amount used to invest can<br>
either be a surplus from current spending<br>
needs or a fund generated deliberately<br>
for investment purposes. Surplus income<br>
occurs when the income level exceeds<br>
the spending needs. Instead of keeping<br>
any surplus income in options that will<br>
not attract more income/return in the<br>
future (e.g., under a mattress or bury it<br>
in the backyard or keep the money with<br>
someone trusted), it is better to invest<br>
because it will generate more money than<br>
the current amount. The extra income to<br>
be generated will compensate for the<br>
time passage, the inflation level that<br>
tends to erode the value of money and<br>
the uncertainty in the future payments.<br>
<b>The person who invests is known as</b><br>
an “investor” and can be an individual,<br>
a government, an institution (pension<br>
funds), or a corporation. Meanwhile,<br>
investments can be done in real assets<br>
such as plants and equipment, real estate,<br>
or in financial assets including shares<br>
and bonds. However, as identified in<br>
the introductory subsection, the chapter<br>
focuses on investments in financial<br>
assets, specifically ordinary shares<br>
(equity securities) and bonds (which are<br>
fixed income securities). The following<br>
are some of the key terms associated<br>
with investments in financial assets:<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
259<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Capital gain</b><br>
<b>Capital gain refers to profit earned from</b><br>
the sale of an asset/security which has<br>
increased in value during the holding<br>
period. It is the profit obtained in the sale<br>
of an asset as result of an increase in the<br>
value of an asset.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Contractual claim</b><br>
<b>Contractual claim is a specified amount</b><br>
that must be paid in accordance with<br>
the legal agreement. For the case of<br>
financial asset, it is the amount that<br>
must be paid to the buyer or owner of a<br>
security periodically in accordance with<br>
the terms of the financial asset.<br>
(iii)<br>
<b>Dividend</b><br>
<b>Dividend refers to the distribution of</b><br>
some of the company’s profit to its<br>
shareholders as approved by the board<br>
of directors of the company.<br>
(iv)<br>
<b>Inflation</b><br>
<b>Inflation is simply the general increase</b><br>
in price of goods and services, or a fall in<br>
the purchasing power of money.<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Investor</b><br>
<b>Investor is a person who puts money into</b><br>
an entity or asset with an aim of getting<br>
financial returns.<br>
(vi)<br>
<b>Liquidity</b><br>
<b>Liquidity refers to how easier an asset</b><br>
or security can be converted into cash<br>
without affecting its market value.<br>
(vii)<br>
<b>Stock exchange</b><br>
<b>Stock exchange (is also known as stock</b><br>
market) is a centralised location (market)<br>
where the shares of publicly traded<br>
companies are bought and sold.<br>
(viii)	 Financial assets<br>
<b>A financial asset is an asset such as cash,</b><br>
bonds and equity whose value comes<br>
from a contractual claim i.e., an amount<br>
that by legal agreement must be paid<br>
periodically to the buyer of a security.<br>
<b>Contractual claim may also specify the</b><br>
time at which the principal must be<br>
repaid.<br>
(ix)<br>
<b>Shares and stock</b><br>
<b>A share is referred to as a unit of</b><br>
ownership which represents an equal<br>
proportion of a company’s capital<br>
while stock is the aggregation of shares<br>
ownership in a company.<br>
<b>Major categories of investments</b><br>
<b>Generally, there are two major categories</b><br>
of investments including, financial<br>
assets and real assets. These can also<br>
be categorised as trading investments<br>
and financial securities investments.<br>
<b>Investment in financial assets, which</b><br>
is the focus of this chapter, is further<br>
subdivided into two types which are<br>
equity-based investments and fixed<br>
income-based investments. Following is<br>
<b>Figure 9.1 showing the major categories</b><br>
of investments and their examples.<br>

<b>Figure 9. 1: Major categories of investments</b><br>
The advantages and disadvantages of each category of investments<br>
Investments in real and financial assets do share one important similarity, which<br>
include providing opportunity or potential for generating cash/revenue. However,<br>
there are several differences based on the advantages and disadvantages of each<br>
type of investment. For example, real assets are typically less liquid than financial<br>
assets since they are usually more cumbersome to exchange and their markets are<br>
not as efficient or populated. The value of real assets also is much more dependent<br>
on factors such as location, function, and operation and exchange costs. On the<br>
other hand, financial assets are typically fungible, thereby making their location<br>
independent. The major advantage of real assets includes its ability to offer more<br>
protection against inflation – the value of such assets and the income they help<br>
generate tend to grow with inflation. Financial assets on the other hand, may or may<br>
not be protected against inflation risk. Real assets investment also offers secure and<br>
steady income e.g., receiving monthly income from tenants in the form of rent<br>

<b>Definition, nature and characteristics</b><br>
of financial assets investment<br>
<b>The following are the different sections</b><br>
providing the definition, nature and<br>
characteristics of investment in financial<br>
assets:<br>
<b>Definition of investment in financial</b><br>
assets<br>
<b>Investment in financial assets are the</b><br>
investments that arise from contractual<br>
agreements on future cash flows or from<br>
owning equity instrument of another<br>
entity. Financial instrument refers to<br>
a contract that generates a financial<br>
asset to one of the parties involved in<br>
the contractual agreement. Therefore,<br>
financial asset is an instrument that is<br>
commonly used to facilitate the transfer<br>
of funds between those who have<br>
surplus funds (known as net savers) and<br>
those who have cash deficit (known as<br>
net borrowers) in the financial market.<br>
<b>Those who provide funds are given</b><br>
certificates, such as share certificates and<br>
bond certificates, in return for supplying<br>
the funds. These certificates are called<br>
financial assets, because they promise<br>
future income or returns to the holders<br>
(investors). On the other hand, those<br>
firms receiving funds (net borrowers<br>
or deficit spenders) will issue written<br>
certificates (known as shares certificate<br>
or bonds certificate) as contractual<br>
promise to honour obligations stipulated<br>
in the certificates.<br>
<b>Based on the foregoing explanations, we</b><br>
define a financial asset as that asset which<br>
derives its value because of a contractual<br>
claim. Unlike land or properties, which<br>
are tangible physical assets, financial<br>
assets do not necessarily have physical<br>
worth. It is a claim against the income<br>
or wealth of (the other) business firm<br>
represented by a certificate, computer<br>
record file, receipt or legal document.<br>
<b>The claim is created by or related to the</b><br>
transfer of money from the investing firm<br>
(net saver) to the cash deficit spender (net<br>
borrower). Examples of financial assets<br>
are the certificates of deposits in bank,<br>
shares bought, bonds, treasury bills,<br>
futures contracts and options. These<br>
types of financial assets are also termed<br>
as securities/financial instruments.<br>
<b>Nature of investment in financial assets</b><br>
<b>The nature of investment in financial</b><br>
assets is determined by the liquidity of<br>
an asset. Some investments are short-<br>
term in nature and some investments<br>
are long-term in nature. Short term<br>
investments are more liquid compared<br>
to long term investments. Also, short<br>
term investments are less risky than long<br>
term investments. Consequently, short<br>
term investments promise less return<br>
compared to long term investments. In<br>
contrast, long term financial investments<br>
are those which are not expected to<br>
be easily converted into cash in a near<br>
period of time. Their acquisition is<br>
usually attached to long-term objectives.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
262<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Liquidity preferences can influence</b><br>
investors’ decisions. An investor who is<br>
expecting to have cash requirement soon<br>
(within a year) is more likely to invest in<br>
short-term financial assets than in long-<br>
term assets. Short-term securities are<br>
easily convertible to cash. For example,<br>
a person who saves to buy or construct a<br>
property is expected to invest in an asset<br>
that has different liquidity compared to<br>
a person who invests his/her savings<br>
to finance school fees of the family.<br>
<b>However, some long-term investments</b><br>
(for example publicly traded shares and<br>
corporate bonds) can be sold in an open<br>
market such as in the stock exchange<br>
whenever it is necessary.<br>
<b>Characteristics of financial assets</b><br>
<b>There</b><br>
are<br>
at<br>
least<br>
three<br>
main<br>
characteristics that describe financial<br>
assets, as explained below:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>One of the basic characteristics</b><br>
of financial assets is that they are<br>
promises for future returns to the<br>
holder. If you buy a share from a<br>
certain company, it implies that<br>
you own part of that company<br>
and you are promised to receive<br>
a certain return in form of share<br>
of profit and capital growth,<br>
respectively known as dividends<br>
and capital gain. Similarly, buying<br>
a bond issued by the government<br>
or by a corporation, you become<br>
a creditor to the government or<br>
to that corporation. Holding that<br>
bond promises you to get returns<br>
in form of fixed interest income<br>
over a certain period.<br>
(ii)	 Financial assets serve as store of<br>
value (purchasing power). Their<br>
value rests on faith that the issuer<br>
will honour their contractual<br>
promise to pay. Financial assets<br>
do not physically depreciate in<br>
the same way as physical goods<br>
wear out. This is because their<br>
physical condition is not relevant<br>
in determining their market value<br>
(price). The value of financial<br>
assets can be affected by demand<br>
and supply, company related<br>
factors, interest rates, politics,<br>
current events, natural calamities,<br>
exchange rates, etc. For example,<br>
a share certificate not more or less<br>
valuable because of the quality of<br>
paper used to print it.<br>
(iii)	 Financial assets are fungible – that<br>
is, they can easily be changed into<br>
another form and substituted for<br>
other assets. Thus, a bond or share<br>
can quickly be converted into any<br>
other asset the holder desires.<br>
<b>Types and importance of financial</b><br>
assets investment<br>
<b>Financial assets, as identified earlier</b><br>
in the chapter, are subdivided into two<br>
main groups, which are equity-based<br>
investments and fixed income-based<br>
investments. The two categories are<br>
discussed in the following subsections,<br>
followed by elaboration of importance<br>
of financial assets investment.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>

263<br>
<b>Equity-based investment</b><br>
<b>The most popular equity-based financial</b><br>
asset is the common stock, which is<br>
also known as “ordinary share”. It is<br>
an investment that gives an investor<br>
(shareholder) a right to own part of the<br>
firm’s share capital. Such ownership<br>
gives a right to an investor to fully<br>
participate in deliberations that affect<br>
the success of the investment firm. The<br>
following are the benefits accrued to an<br>
investor of ordinary shares:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Participating in deliberations of</b><br>
the firm’s operations through<br>
voting right. The number of shares<br>
an investor owns determines the<br>
power of the voting right when it<br>
comes to deliberations of a firm’s<br>
operations.<br>
(ii)	 Investment in ordinary shares<br>
attracts a return in the form of<br>
either dividends or capital gain<br>
or both. Dividend refers to the<br>
distribution of the profit generated<br>
to shareholders while capital gain<br>
refers to an increase in the value<br>
of ordinary shares.<br>
(iii)	  Also, an investor of ordinary<br>
shares has a chance to benefit<br>
from the bonus shares and rights<br>
issue.<br>
<b>Fixed income-based investment</b><br>
<b>A fixed income-based investment refers</b><br>
to financial assets which attract a fixed<br>
amount of return after every period. The<br>
return is the interest amount attached<br>
to the asset. Fixed income securities<br>
include certificate of deposits (CDs),<br>
treasury<br>
bills,<br>
government<br>
bonds,<br>
corporate bonds, and preference shares.<br>
<b>The details of these financial instruments</b><br>
are as follows:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Certificate of deposits is the</b><br>
financial asset that is issued by<br>
financial institutions including<br>
banks<br>
requiring<br>
a<br>
minimum<br>
amount to be deposited for a<br>
specified fixed duration. In return,<br>
they offer higher interest rates<br>
than savings accounts.<br>
(ii)	 Treasury bills are the short-term<br>
financial assets that are issued by<br>
the government at a discount and<br>
their maturity time is less than a<br>
year. The yield of the treasury bill<br>
is the difference between the price<br>
paid and face value. In Tanzania,<br>
for instance, the government<br>
through the Bank of Tanzania<br>
(BoT) has been issuing treasury<br>
bills with maturity days of thirty-<br>
five (35), ninety-one (91), one<br>
hundred eighty-two (182) and<br>
three hundred sixty-four (364).<br>
(iii)	 Government<br>
bonds<br>
are<br>
the<br>
financial assets issued by the<br>
government which has maturity<br>
duration of more than one year<br>
carrying a promise to pay interest<br>
periodically. In Tanzania, for<br>
instance, the BoT issues treasury<br>
bonds on behalf of the government<br>
with maturities of two (2) years,<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
264<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
five (5) years, seven (7) years, ten<br>
(10) years, fifteen (15) years, and<br>
twenty (20) years. The interest in<br>
investment is paid on semi-annual<br>
basis.<br>
(iv)	 Corporate bonds are the long-<br>
term financial assets issued by<br>
companies. They carry a promise<br>
to<br>
pay<br>
interest<br>
periodically.<br>
<b>Companies can issue bonds as a</b><br>
way of borrowing funds to finance<br>
their operations.<br>
(v)	 Preference shares are the financial<br>
assets issued by companies as<br>
means of raising capital. They<br>
carry a promise to make an annual<br>
payment to an investor, either as a<br>
coupon percentage or a specified<br>
amount of money, but they do not<br>
have a maturity period.<br>
<b>Generally, investment in fixed income</b><br>
financial assets is a loan provided by an<br>
investor with an expectation of receiving<br>
mandated payment schedules of the<br>
interest income and principal amount,<br>
except for preference shares. Preference<br>
shares do not have a maturity period;<br>
consequently, the investor does not<br>
expect to get back the principal amount,<br>
unless the company is liquidated.<br>
<b>Importance</b><br>
of<br>
financial<br>
assets<br>
investment in business undertaking<br>
<b>Investment in financial assets is very</b><br>
important in the business operations of<br>
both an investor and the issuer. Such<br>
importance is summarised as follows:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>To an investor:</b><br>
<b>Financial</b><br>
assets<br>
provide<br>
an<br>
opportunity to allocate financial<br>
resources<br>
wisely.<br>
<b>Instead</b><br>
of<br>
holding cash, which does not<br>
generate a return, it is better to<br>
invest.<br>
<b>Investments are the source of</b><br>
regular income.<br>
(ii)	 To the government and its<br>
institutions:<br>
<b>When issuing financial assets,</b><br>
it is the sources of funds/capital<br>
to finance their operations and<br>
development projects.<br>
<b>Financial assets can be used as a</b><br>
macroeconomic stabilising tool<br>
through monetary policy. The<br>
government may decide to issue<br>
a bond specifically aiming at<br>
reducing excessive money supply<br>
in the circulation to curb inflation.<br>
(iii)	 To the issuing firms, financial<br>
assets are the source of capital,<br>
which contribute to financing their<br>
operations, project development<br>
and the economic development of<br>
a country in general.<br>
<b>The procedures for purchasing and</b><br>
selling financial assets in Tanzania<br>
<b>In Tanzania, financial assets are publicly</b><br>
traded through Dar es salaam Stock<br>
<b>Exchange (DSE) except government</b><br>
bonds which are initially traded through<br>
auction by the Bank of Tanzania (BoT).<br>

<b>Thereafter, the government bonds are</b><br>
registered in the DSE for secondary<br>
market trading. For an investor to<br>
participate in the DSE, he/she is<br>
required to have an account with the<br>
<b>DSE through the Central Depository</b><br>
<b>System (CDS). The account is used</b><br>
to record the ownership of the stock<br>
holdings and transaction history for<br>
buying or selling shares. The process<br>
involved in opening the CDS accounts<br>
slightly<br>
differs<br>
between<br>
individual<br>
investors and institutional investors.<br>
<b>Individual investors are required to</b><br>
lodge an application form accompanied<br>
by an individual’s information sheet<br>
and a copy of an identity card. DSE has<br>
also introduced mobile trading whereby<br>
investors enter their registration details,<br>
buy shares, sell shares, and check the<br>
balance of the shares that they own among<br>
other services by using the Unstructured<br>
<b>Supplementary Service Data (USSD)</b><br>
code in selected mobile operators or<br>
using the mobile application. Meanwhile,<br>
corporate investors are required to fill<br>
and submit application forms, copies of<br>
incorporation certificates, memorandum<br>
of association, articles of association,<br>
and directors’ identity cards. The<br>
opening of the account is free of charge<br>
to all investors.<br>
<b>Once a CDS account is opened, an</b><br>
investor can buy security by filling<br>
purchase order form, an individual’s<br>
information form, and then depositing<br>
money. Corporate investors are required<br>
to submit a certificate of incorporation<br>
instead of an individual’s information<br>
sheet. Other procedures remain the<br>
same as it is for the individual investor.<br>
<b>Similarly, when an individual investor</b><br>
wants to sell the shares, the original<br>
certificate of deposit is submitted to<br>
the broker together with filling out the<br>
sale order form and submitting payment<br>
details. For a corporate investor, a signed<br>
letter from the company authorising<br>
the sale is also required in addition to<br>
submitting the original certificate of<br>
deposit and filling out the sale order<br>
form. The process of buying and selling<br>
financial assets can be facilitated through<br>
a broker.<br>

<b>Mipango will be required to</b><br>
accomplish to invest in a financial<br>
asset that you have advised him<br>
in (a) above.<br>
<b>Investment in shares and their</b><br>
accounting treatments<br>
<b>Investment in ordinary shares, just like</b><br>
other financial instruments, is considered<br>
an asset to an investor. In the books<br>
of account, an investor is required to<br>
maintain separate investment accounts<br>
for each type of investment. For the case<br>
of ordinary shares, the amount given out<br>
during the acquisition of an asset is the<br>
capital and the amount received from the<br>
asset is an income received in the form<br>
of dividend. By definition, dividend<br>
refers to a reward, cash or otherwise,<br>
that a company gives to its shareholders.<br>
<b>Dividends can be issued in various</b><br>
forms, such as cash payment, stocks or<br>
any other form.<br>
<b>As for accounting treatment, the total</b><br>
cost of investment including acquisition<br>
expenses is usually debited to the<br>
investment account. It is common for<br>
an investor to incur additional expenses<br>
(for example, brokerage fees) besides<br>
the price paid in the acquisition of<br>
common stock. Thus, the full cost of an<br>
investment is a summation of the price<br>
of the stock plus additional expenses.<br>
<b>Investment account has three columns on</b><br>
both sides: the debit side and the credit<br>
side. The following are the details, which<br>
are recorded in each of the columns:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>The first column is for recording</b><br>
the nominal value of an asset,<br>
either when an asset is acquired<br>
(on the debit side) or when the<br>
asset is disposed of (on the credit<br>
side). This column is not part of<br>
the double-entry bookkeeping<br>
system.<br>
(ii)	 The<br>
second<br>
column<br>
is<br>
for<br>
recording income: On the debit<br>
side, it is used to record any<br>
income aspect associated with the<br>
asset acquisition (not transferable<br>
to statement of profit or loss and<br>
other comprehensive income, as<br>
investment income) while on the<br>
credit side it is used to record<br>
income derived from the asset.<br>
<b>The column is part of the double-</b><br>
entry bookkeeping.<br>
(iii)	 The third column is for recording<br>
capital; the debit side is used to<br>
record the cost of investment<br>
during acquisition and the credit<br>
side to record the value of an asset<br>
during disposal or the balance of<br>
the cost of investment brought<br>
down. The column is part of the<br>
bookkeeping system.<br>

<b>Buying and selling of shares/bonds</b><br>
When an investor decides to sell the common stock, it is important to establish<br>
whether there is a profit or loss on the sale. This involves calculations, whereas an<br>
average price of an asset on the selling date is used. In the capital market, two prices<br>
are quoted on a share; the lower is the selling/ask price and the higher is the buying/<br>
bid price. Therefore, it is the average of these two prices which is used to calculate<br>
profit or loss on disposal. Similarly, the average price is used for reporting in the<br>
statement of financial position (balance sheet). The International Financial Reporting<br>
Standards (IFRS 9) guide the appropriate treatment of transactions associated with<br>
financial instruments.<br>
<b>Bonus issue</b><br>
A company can decide to issue bonus shares to the existing shareholders as a way of<br>
capitalising its reserves. Bonus issue does not require existing investors to pay for<br>
them. In this case, the wealth of a shareholder in the common stock account does not<br>
change though the number of shares changes. Therefore, in the investment account,<br>
only the face value of the bonus shares is recorded in the nominal column on the<br>
debit side.<br>
<b>Example 9.1</b><br>
On 1st April, 2020, Zitendwa Ltd purchased 2,000 ordinary shares of TZS 2,000<br>
each from Mkakati Plc at TZS 4,800,000. On 1st August, 2020, Mkakati Plc issued<br>
bonus shares of 1 share for every 5 shares held. Recording of these transactions in<br>
the investment account of Zitendwa Ltd will be as follows:<br>

<b>Rights issue</b><br>
Rights issue of shares occurs when the company issue new shares, but the privilege<br>
is given to the existing shareholders, as their right. This gives existing shareholders<br>
an option of either buying them (exercising his/her right) or passing it over to another<br>
investor for consideration. In case an investor exercises the right, the nominal<br>
value and the amount paid are recorded in the nominal column and capital column,<br>
respectively, on the debit side of an investment account.<br>

<b>Recording transactions in investment account</b><br>
On 1st March, 2021, Msasa Ltd purchased 3,000 ordinary shares of Chata Plc<br>
of TZS 1,000 each at TZS 4,500,000. On 1st July, 2021 Chata Plc gave existing<br>
shareholders a right to buy 1 new share for every 2 shares held at TZS 900. Msasa<br>
Ltd exercised the right to buy new shares. On 31st October, Chata Plc decided to<br>
capitalise part of its reserve, hence issued 1 bonus share for every 10 shares held<br>
by existing shareholders. On 15th December Msasa sold 1,000 shares at a market<br>
price of TZS 1,800 each.<br>
<b>Required</b><br>
Record the transactions in the investment account of Msasa Ltd.<br>
Acquisition and disposal of ordinary shares under different quotations<br>
An investor can decide to buy ordinary shares at any time provided that he/she has<br>
capital. Meanwhile, the value of an ordinary share differs with time, from when<br>
the dividend has just been paid to another dividend payment date because its return<br>
accrues as time goes on. Therefore, the time at which an investor decides to buy<br>
common stock can have an impact on the transaction price.<br>
3,000<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
271<br>
When an investor buys ordinary shares, he/she will be entitled to receive the next<br>
dividend unless it is agreed at the time of sale as to who should receive the next<br>
instalment. Usually, the dividend is paid to the owner, whose name appears on the<br>
share certificate of the company when the payment is done regardless of how long<br>
he/she has owned the ordinary shares. This highlights the importance of such an<br>
agreement between the seller and the purchaser. The agreement can give out two<br>
possible transaction prices: The cumulative dividend (Cum-Div.) and excluding<br>
dividend (Ex-Div.) prices. The same concept applies to fixed income security’s price<br>
quotations discussed in earlier whose examples are also relevant to this section.<br>
<b>Cum-div price quotation</b><br>
The cum-div quotation refers to the price which considers the portion of the<br>
dividend accrued to the transaction date. It is higher than the normal price because it<br>
incorporates accrued dividends. The purchase price the buyer pays does include the<br>
dividend accrued to the time of the transaction. Thus, the capital cost is obtained after<br>
deducting interest accrued from the purchase price (see example 9.8). Therefore, an<br>
investor who buys a stock at such dividend inclusive price, he/she also pays for the<br>
right to receive the accrued dividend. In this case, an investor is required to split<br>
the amount paid into the normal price (capital) and dividend (income) aspect in<br>
the books of accounts i.e., the investment account. The same consideration is made<br>
when an investor disposes-off the common stock at cum-div price.<br>
For illustration purpose, journal entries for recording purchases of ordinary shares at<br>
cum – div price are shown as follows:<br>
<b>Dr. Investment Account (normal price in capital column)</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Dr. Investment Account (accrued dividend in income column</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Cr. Bank Account (Total amount paid)</b><br>
xxx<br>
Note that, in practice, dividend/interest is usually paid to a person who holds the<br>
share/debenture certificate. However, there is a date at which investors are required<br>
to register with the issuer of shares/debenture for the distribution of the dividend/<br>
interest. Thus, if the share/debenture is sold prior to the registration date, it will be<br>
quoted at cum-div price. On the other hand, if it is sold after registration date, it will<br>
be quoted at ex-div price. Thus, once someone has registered for dividend/interest<br>
distribution, the dividend/interest will be paid to him/her regardless of whether he/<br>
she still holds the share/debenture certificate.<br>

<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>

<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>

<b>Ex-div price quotation</b><br>
The ex-div quotation is the transaction price of ordinary shares which does not<br>
include the dividend aspect accrued to the time of sale. The purchase price the<br>
buyer pays does not include the dividend accrued to the time of the transaction.<br>
Unlike the cum-div quotation transaction for ex-div, the accrued dividends are not<br>
deducted from the purchase price to arrive at the capital price. However, when an<br>
investor has quoted the investment at the ex-div price, he/she is required to pay the<br>
seller the accrued dividend to the transaction date, in addition to the quoted price.<br>
In accounting records, the investor will record the quoted price together with the<br>
transaction costs (for example brokerage fees and taxes) in the capital column and<br>
the accrued aspect of the dividend in the income column.<br>
Following is an illustration of journal entries to record ordinary shares purchases at<br>
ex-div price:<br>
<b>Dr. Investment Account (normal price in capital column)</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Dr. Investment Account (accrued dividend in income column</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Cr. Bank Account (total amount paid)</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Purchase and sale of fixed interest securities</b><br>
The purchase and sale of fixed income financial assets are usually done through<br>
brokers, as it is for ordinary shares. The price of any financial asset represents the<br>
present value of the cash flow expected from an asset. To calculate the present value<br>
of an asset it is necessary to establish the cash flows and the discounting rate which<br>
is the required return of an asset. The cash flows of a bond, for instance, are the<br>
coupon interest payments and the principal amount that will be repaid at maturity.<br>

<b>Cash flows of fixed income financial asset</b><br>
For a fixed income financial asset, the cash flows are determined by the terms<br>
stipulated in the asset (coupon rate and par-value at maturity). Meanwhile, the<br>
interest rate or discounted rate is normally established by comparing the yield<br>
offered by other similar financial assets in the market.<br>
<b>Example 9.4</b><br>
The cash flows of a 10-year bond with a 12% (6% per 6 months) coupon rate valued<br>
at par or maturity value of TZS 20,000,000 are established as follows:<br>
Coupon interest to be received semi-annually = 6% x 20,000,000 = TZS<br>
1,200,000<br>
Maturity value i.e., par value = TZS 20,000,000.<br>
It implies that there will be 20 instalments of TZS 1,200,000 interest payment over<br>
the 10 years of the bond period and at the end, the holder of the bond will receive a<br>
face value amount of TZS 20,000,000.<br>
<b>Yield of a fixed income financial asset</b><br>
The return from financial assets including bonds is also known as a yield. The yield<br>
of a bond depends on the coupon rate and the price at which it is sold. If an asset<br>
is sold at the face value, its yield will be the same as the coupon rate. However, an<br>
asset can be sold at a price different from the face value.<br>
In a scenario where an asset is sold at a price lower than the face value, it is said to<br>
be sold at discount. It implies that the required rate of return by investors is higher<br>
than the stated coupon rate. In contrast, an asset that is sold at a higher price than<br>
the stated face value is said to be sold at a premium. In this case, the required return<br>
(market interest rate) is lower than the coupon rate.<br>
<b>Example 9.5: bond sold at par</b><br>
The price of a 10-year government bond with a 12% (6% per 6 months) coupon<br>
rate, a par value of TZS 20,000,000 and the required return of 12% (the same as the<br>
coupon rate) is established as follows:<br>

<b>Recording fixed income security transactions</b><br>
The cost of acquiring a fixed-income asset is the actual price paid plus other costs<br>
such as brokerage fees, stamp duty, and transfer fees associated with the acquisition.<br>
An investor of fixed income security expects to receive a fixed amount (interest)<br>
after every period. Transactions of the fixed income investments are also recorded<br>
in the investment account, in the same way as it is for equity-based investments.<br>
When the purchase or sale of a fixed income asset is done just at the interest payment<br>
date, the quoted price is the cost of an investment. Such a price will not include any<br>
amount of interest. Therefore, in the books of accounts, an investment account will<br>
be debited with the cost of an asset together with the transaction costs in the capital<br>
column and its corresponding double entry will be on the bank account. The journal<br>
entries on acquisition of fixed income financial asset at cost can be presented as<br>
follows:<br>
<b>Dr. Investment account (capital column)</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Cr. Bank account</b><br>
xxx<br>
If the transaction is done before the interest payment date, the quoted price can be<br>
either inclusive or exclusive of the interest accrued to the transaction date. When<br>
the purchase or sale is done at a quoted price inclusive of the interest (cum-interest),<br>
the cost of investment should exclude the interest amount accrued. For illustration,<br>
relevant journal entries on acquisition of fixed income security quoted at cum-<br>
interest price are presented as follows:<br>
<b>Exercise 9.2:</b><br>
<b>Determining income flow and price of a bond</b><br>
Mr. Mafanikio has decided to purchase five years 12% Treasury bonds of TZS<br>
10,000,000 with interest payable 30th June and 31st December.<br>
<b>Required</b><br>
(a)	 Determine the amount of income that Mr. Mafanikio will be receiving at<br>
every interest payment date.<br>

In case the purchase or sale is done at a quoted price exclusive of interest (ex-interest)<br>
the cost of an investment is just the quoted price plus the associated acquisition cost.<br>
However, an investor who buys a stock at an ex-interest price, he/she will be required<br>
to pay the quoted price plus the accrued amount of interest. On the next interest<br>
payment date, an investor will receive the full amount of the interest instalment, but<br>
in his/her books of accounts, only a portion of the interest for the period from when<br>
the asset was purchased to when interest is paid will be recognised. The journal<br>
entries for the acquisition of fixed income asset at ex-interest price are illustrated as<br>

<b>Exercise 9.3: Purchase and sale of bonds</b><br>
Sasa Kazi Ltd bought 50, 12% treasury bonds with a par-value of TZS 1,000,000<br>
each on 1st January 2021 at TZS 960,000. On 1st August, 2021, the company<br>
decided to sell 10 T-bonds of 12%, each at TZS 970,000. Interest is paid semi-<br>
annually; on 30th June and 31st December of every year. The market price of the<br>
bond at the time of sale was TZS 980,000.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
283<br>
<b>Required</b><br>
(a)	 Make necessary computations<br>
and then record the transactions<br>
in the bonds account of Sasa<br>
Kazi Ltd.<br>
(b)	 Make necessary computations<br>
and then record the transactions<br>
in the bonds account of Sasa<br>
<b>Kazi Ltd if the selling price was</b><br>
quoted at ex-interest.<br>
<b>The treatment of year-end entries</b><br>
<b>At the end of the financial year, when a</b><br>
company closes its books of accounts to<br>
establish the outcome of its operations,<br>
the company needs to establish whether it<br>
has made a profit or loss from investment<br>
activities as well as to establish the<br>
value of its investment for reporting in<br>
the statement of financial position. For<br>
investment activities, a company can<br>
determine profit or loss every time an<br>
individual transaction takes place, or it<br>
can wait till the end of an accounting<br>
period where all transactions are<br>
considered together through balancing<br>
the investment account. In balancing the<br>
investment account, the difference in a<br>
nominal column is just showing the face<br>
value of the assets held. Meanwhile, the<br>
balance between the principal columns<br>
reveals the cost of financial assets to be<br>
reported in the statement of financial<br>
position and the balance between the<br>
interest columns is the income derived<br>
from the investment.<br>
<b>While preparing final reports, it is</b><br>
important to remember that transactions<br>
associated with investment activities<br>
can result in accruals. Acquisition and<br>
disposal of financial assets can take<br>
place at any time and the payment of<br>
interest and dividends may not always<br>
match the date at which the books of<br>
accounts are closed. Therefore, when an<br>
investor is closing the books of accounts,<br>
at the end of the accounting period, it is<br>
important to establish accrued income<br>
(interest/dividend) for their recognition<br>
in the financial statements. For instance,<br>
a company that purchased a bond just<br>
two months before the closing date of<br>
its books of accounts will be required<br>
to calculate accrued interest for income<br>
recognition even if the payment date is<br>
not yet.<br>

<b>Activity 9.4</b><br>
<b>When teaching the investment topic,</b><br>
your<br>
teacher<br>
discussed<br>
various<br>
investment terminologies, some of<br>
which are as follows:<br>
1.	 Financial investment, financial<br>
instrument, financial security and<br>
financial asset,<br>
2.	 Nominal value, face value, stated<br>
value and coupon rate,<br>
3.	 Equity-based<br>
investment<br>
and<br>
fixed income investment,<br>
4.	 Investment income, dividends<br>
and interest,<br>
5.	 Returns, yield and capital gain.<br>
<b>Required</b><br>
<b>Discuss with your classmates, and</b><br>
then describe the listed terminologies<br>
concisely indicating their similarities<br>
and differences. You can refer to other<br>
sources of information including<br>
internet and library.<br>
<b>In this chapter, we have described</b><br>
the meaning of various terms and<br>
concepts along with calculations<br>
related to investment in financial<br>
assets, particularly ordinary shares,<br>
and bonds. Investment in financial<br>
assets involves the use of money to<br>
buy marketable security with the<br>
expectation to generate income in<br>
the future. The money used to invest<br>
can be either a surplus to the existing<br>
expenditure requirement or set aside<br>
specifically for investment purposes<br>
instead of current spending.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
two<br>
main<br>
financial<br>
assets<br>
described in this chapter are ordinary<br>
shares<br>
which<br>
are<br>
equity-based<br>
financial instruments and bonds which<br>
are fixed income-based instruments.<br>
<b>The equity-based instruments offer</b><br>
the owner a right to participate in the<br>
deliberations of the matter affecting<br>
the performance of the company.<br>
<b>Chapter summary</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
285<br>
<b>However, these securities have a</b><br>
contingent and varied stream of<br>
income because their return is the<br>
dividend whose distribution depends<br>
on the decisions made from time<br>
to time. In contrast, fixed income<br>
financial asset guarantees the owner<br>
a predictable stream of income based<br>
on the agreed interest rates. However,<br>
for pricing purposes, both equity-<br>
based instruments and fixed income-<br>
based instruments are valued based<br>
on discounting their expected cash<br>
flows using the required rate of return.<br>
<b>The required return is also known as</b><br>
the yield of an asset, the discount rate<br>
or opportunity cost of capital. For<br>
ordinary shares, the expected cash<br>
flows are in the form of divided and<br>
capital gain which is the increment of<br>
ordinary shares prices. Meanwhile,<br>
for fixed income security, the cash<br>
flows are the interest payments and the<br>
repayment of the principal amount.<br>
<b>In the books of accounts, transactions</b><br>
associated with an investment in<br>
financial assets are recorded in<br>
investment<br>
accounts. A<br>
separate<br>
investment account is opened for every<br>
category of asset. All transactions<br>
associated with the purchase and sale<br>
of financial assets are recorded in such<br>
an account throughout the financial<br>
year. At the end of an accounting<br>
period, the investment accounts are<br>
closed to establish income derived<br>
from<br>
investment<br>
activities.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
income is established for reporting in<br>
the income statement while investment<br>
cost is established for reporting in the<br>
statement of financial position.<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Ten</b><br>
<b>Accounting</b><br>
for branches<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
In this chapter you will learn about different concepts related to branch accounting.<br>
You will also learn the basic methods used in recording business transactions<br>
between the head office and its branches. The competencies developed in this<br>
chapter will enable you to identify and describe all important concepts associated<br>
with branch accounting. You will also be able to apply the basic principles and<br>
methods to prepare different types of branch accounts.<br>
<b>Nature and meaning of branch</b><br>
accounts<br>
<b>A branch is a business operating unit</b><br>
that operates away from the head office<br>
as a representative and it is meant to<br>
break geographical barriers. As a result,<br>
the accounting systems of an entity are<br>
designed to facilitate operations of the<br>
entity at the head office as well as in<br>
the branches. Therefore, accounting for<br>
branches originates from the expansion<br>
of the business operations to the extent<br>
of having establishments in different<br>
geographical locations. Substantially,<br>
these branches undertake the same<br>
activities as those undertaken by the head<br>
office. For example, when a company<br>
starts operations at a small scale, with<br>
only a few customers, it may not need<br>
to have branches. It may have customers<br>
located in different regions, but the<br>
company can still manage to serve them<br>
from one location. However, the growth<br>
of the business may necessitate the<br>
establishment of branches to increase<br>
business visibility and accessibility of<br>
its products to customers.<br>
<b>When a company has branches, obviously</b><br>
there will be transactions undertaken by<br>
the head office and there will be others<br>
undertaken by the branches. In addition,<br>
there will be inter-company transactions<br>
i.e., between the head office and branches.<br>
<b>Accounting</b><br>
for<br>
branches<br>
provides<br>
methods and techniques that enable<br>
capturing transactions of the branches<br>
separate from those undertaken by the<br>
head office. The importance of capturing<br>
business operations of every operating<br>
unit separately is to facilitate profit<br>
determination, performance evaluation<br>
and controlling branch operations. This<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
288<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
is important for the management to<br>
take appropriate actions if the situation<br>
requires so. If the branch is making<br>
losses, for instance, it will be worthless<br>
to keep it, except for strategic reasons.<br>
<b>Attention will be required to address the</b><br>
reasons for any branch that is making<br>
a loss. Therefore, the basic purpose of<br>
branch accounting is to ascertain the<br>
branch income, branch expenses, branch<br>
assets and branch liabilities.<br>
<b>Branches have three key features. Firstly,</b><br>
branches do not have separate legal<br>
statuses. It means their legal existence<br>
is depending on the legal existence of<br>
the main office and that is why they are<br>
considered as an extension of the main<br>
office. Secondly, branches do not have<br>
independent capital. Their activities are<br>
financed through the capital of the head<br>
office. Even if they are given the power to<br>
generate capital, it will be just on behalf<br>
of the head office. Thirdly, the assets<br>
and liabilities of the branches are part<br>
of the head office. This feature is related<br>
to the first and the second, because if<br>
branches have neither independent legal<br>
status nor independent capital, then they<br>
cannot have independent assets. The<br>
features of branches are summarised as<br>
follows:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Branches are not separate legal</b><br>
entities; they are just extensions<br>
of the head office,<br>
(ii)	 Branches do not have independent<br>
capital,<br>
(iii)	 Assets and liabilities of branches<br>
are part of the head office’s<br>
properties and liabilities.<br>
<b>Classification of branches</b><br>
<b>From</b><br>
an<br>
accounting<br>
perspective,<br>
branches can be classified based on either<br>
location or control. When branches are<br>
classified based on location, we get two<br>
classes which are inland/local branches<br>
and foreign branches. Inland (sometimes<br>
known as domestic or home) branches<br>
are those which operate in the same<br>
country as its head office while foreign<br>
branches are those which operate in a<br>
different country from the head office<br>
with a different currency. On the other<br>
hand, when branches are classified<br>
based on control, we get two classes,<br>
which are dependent (non-autonomous)<br>
branches and independent (autonomous)<br>
branches. Dependent branches are those<br>
which are wholly controlled by the head<br>
office and their accounts are kept by the<br>
head office. In contrast, independent<br>
branches are those which have the<br>
autonomy to prepare and maintain their<br>
books of accounts.<br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
branch accounting<br>
<b>Branch accounting benefits the company</b><br>
in the making, analysing and tracking<br>
of business decisions according to a<br>
particular branch’s requirements over<br>
time and in controlling each branch’s<br>
overall operations. Its disadvantages<br>
include, chances of mismanagement<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
289<br>
and decision-making delays may occur due to various reasons. It also increases a<br>
company’s expense due to separate set-ups at different locations.<br>
Activity 10.1.<br>
Use different sources of information including the library and the internet to<br>
respond to the following questions:<br>
(a)	 From an accounting perspective, discuss the relevance of a branch and<br>
branch accounting in general.<br>
(b)	 What are the advantages and limitations of branch accounting.<br>
(c)	 Identify at least three companies that operate in Tanzania which have foreign<br>
branches.<br>
(d) 	 In the current era of advanced information technology, is there still a need<br>
for a company to establish branches while the internet could facilitate<br>
e-commerce?<br>
<b>Non-autonomous or integrated branches</b><br>
As identified above, non-autonomous branches are those which do not have the<br>
power to prepare their own books of accounts. They are wholly controlled by the<br>
head office. Examples of companies that have non-autonomous branches in Tanzania<br>
include those operating in the passenger transportation industry. For example, bus<br>
services operators have agencies in different regions/bus stations for ticket booking,<br>
and fuel retailing companies which have petrol stations across the country.<br>
The nature of the operations of non-autonomous branches can be in one of the<br>
following forms:<br>
(i)<br>
A branch is established as an agent of the head office for receiving customers’<br>
orders for the products and/or services offered by the company. Then it<br>
remits the orders to the head office for execution.<br>
(ii)	 A branch is established as a retail selling unit for the products from head<br>
office.<br>
(iii)	 A branch is established as a wholesale delivery unit for the products supplied<br>
by the head office.<br>
As a result, the head office prepares and maintains the books of accounts of the<br>
branch to monitor their operations closely. The accounting records of the branch are<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
290<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
useful because they enable the head office to observe the changes in assets, liability,<br>
and capital. Furthermore, the separate records of each branch help to determine<br>
the profitability of each branch and exercise necessary controls to avoid misuse of<br>
resources held by the branches.<br>
<b>Activity 10.2</b><br>
XYZ Ltd., is a big company specialised in processing agricultural food products<br>
in Tanzania. The company headquarter is in Dar es salaam where it has two<br>
processing plants; one is for cooking oil and the other is for wheat flour. In addition,<br>
the company has three branches processing cooking oil in Dodoma, Singida and<br>
Kigoma and two branches processing wheat flour in Arusha and Mwanza.<br>
<b>Required</b><br>
(a)	 Identify the basic features for the branches of XYZ Ltd. to be classified as<br>
non-autonomous branches.<br>
(b)	 Explain operational arrangements/procedures to be established by XYZ Ltd,<br>
that will be used as guidelines for effective operation of its non-autonomous<br>
branches.<br>
Transactions and accounts when non-autonomous branches are used<br>
The contents of the accounts for recording branch transactions in relation to the<br>
head office depend on the pricing approach the head office adopts (discussed in<br>
next section) when sending goods to the branch. Generally, the company can adopt<br>
any of the three approaches to transfer goods to a branch. These are: (i) goods are<br>
transferred at cost, (ii) goods are transferred at selling price, and (iii) goods are sent<br>
at wholesale price. Despite the pricing approach adopted, there are common books<br>
of accounts maintained by the company operating with branches. Such accounts<br>
include: (i) branch stock account, (ii) branch debtors account, (iii) branch expenses<br>
account, (iv) goods sent to branch accounts, and (v) adjustment accounts. The<br>
descriptions of these accounts are as follows:<br>
<b>Branch stock account</b><br>
The branch stock account is used to keep records of stock movement. On the debit<br>
side, the account is used to record stock received from the head office at invoice<br>
price and on the credit side, the stock sold will be recorded at its cost, and the<br>
value of stock on hand. Invoice price is the price in which goods are sent to the<br>
branch at relatively higher value than the cost price. Under this system. the head<br>
office will invoice goods by adding a certain percentage above the cost prices (e.g.,<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
291<br>
10% or 20%) which account for profit.<br>
<b>Therefore, when goods are transferred to</b><br>
a branch at a selling price, the balancing<br>
figure between the debit side and credit<br>
side is the value of the remaining stock<br>
at the invoiced (selling) price. However,<br>
if the goods are transferred to the branch<br>
at cost, the difference between the debit<br>
side (stock received at cost) and the<br>
credit side (at selling price and the value<br>
of closing stock at cost) is the gross<br>
profit generated by the branch.<br>
<b>Branch debtors account</b><br>
<b>The branch debtors account is used to</b><br>
keep a record of the debtors whenever<br>
branches have the permission to sell<br>
goods on credit. It is maintained by the<br>
branch just for recording credit sales and<br>
payments to establish debtors’ balances.<br>
<b>Branch expenses account</b><br>
<b>The branch expenses account is used</b><br>
to record all expenses incurred by<br>
the branch, such as sundry expenses<br>
and casual labours supporting branch<br>
operations. It includes expenses paid<br>
by the branch itself as well as expenses<br>
incurred by the head office on behalf of<br>
the branch. These expenses are included<br>
in the ascertainment of the profit made<br>
by the branch.<br>
<b>Goods sent to branch account</b><br>
<b>This account is used to record the</b><br>
movement of goods from the head office<br>
to the branch. The head office maintains<br>
this account. Once goods are sent to the<br>
branch this account is debited. If the<br>
branch is permitted to make credit sales,<br>
then the account is also used to record<br>
(on the credit side) cash paid by branch<br>
debtors directly to the head office. In<br>
addition, it records any returns of stock<br>
from the branch on the credit side.<br>
<b>Adjustment account</b><br>
<b>The account is used to reconcile the</b><br>
differences between the branch stock<br>
account and goods sent to the branch<br>
account whenever the head office sends<br>
goods at a cost-plus (profit margin)<br>
price. Simply, the adjustment account<br>
is used to establish unrealised profit and<br>
the realised profit on the goods sent to<br>
the branch.<br>
<b>For example, the head office of XYZ</b><br>
<b>Company sends 100 units of stock to</b><br>
its branch at a cost-plus price of TZS<br>
10,000. If this price includes a profit<br>
margin of TZS 1,500 per unit, then the<br>
head office will recognise TZS 150,000<br>
(i.e., 100 units x TZS 1,500 profit<br>
margin) as profit from branch sales, but<br>
this is correct only when the branch has<br>
sold all stock. However, if the branch has<br>
sold only 80 units, then the head office<br>
will recognise only TZS 120,000 (i.e.,<br>
80 x 1,500) as profit earned from branch<br>
sales and the remaining TZS 30,000 as<br>
unrealised profit on the goods sent to<br>
the branch. Thus, all these adjustments<br>
related to goods sent to the branch for<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
292<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
realised and unrealised profit are made through the adjustment account.<br>
<b>Invoicing goods to branch from head office</b><br>
Goods can be transferred to a branch at either the selling price or at cost or wholesale<br>
(cost plus) price. The details of each of these pricing approaches together with<br>
their accounting treatments are provided in the following sections and related sub-<br>
sections:<br>
<b>Goods sent to branch at selling price</b><br>
Under this approach, the head office uses the price at which the goods are sold<br>
in the market to charge the goods which are sent to the branch. Therefore, when<br>
the goods are transferred to the branch, the head office treats them as if they are<br>
sold. However, if at the end of the year some remains as branch closing stock, it<br>
is not acceptable for the head office to recognise part of the profit associated with<br>
the unsold stock as profit because the sale is just within the company. Thus, it is<br>
necessary to adjust unrealised profit on unsold stock at the end of the year through<br>
the branch adjustment account.<br>
When the company decides to use selling price to charge goods sent to the branch,<br>
there are three methods it can adopt. The first is the debtor’s method, the second is<br>
the stock and debtors method, and the third is the final accounts method.<br>
(a)	Debtors method<br>
This is an approach which uses selling price to charge goods sent to the branch. The<br>
approach considers branch as a selling outlet or as a debtor to the head office. This<br>
method is usually adopted when branch is small and does not make credit sales, and<br>
if it does, the debtors are paying in the same branch at which the sale took place. The<br>
main accounts for recording branch transactions at the head office are the branch<br>
stock account, goods sent to branch account, and stock reserve account. In this case,<br>
the head office maintains a separate account for each branch. For instance, a branch<br>
that just receives goods and sells them, its account will be debited with the goods<br>
sent at the selling price and credited with the value of goods sold by the branch. The<br>
difference, which is the balance, will be showing the value of the stock on hand at<br>
the selling price.<br>
(i)<br>
<b>When goods are sent to branch at the selling price</b><br>
<b>Dr. Branch stock account</b><br>
xxx<br>
<b>Cr. Goods sent to branch account</b><br>
xxx<br>

When a company uses the debtors’ method to control the operations of the branches,<br>
it is important to make physical verification of the amount of stock at regular intervals.<br>
In operations of the branch, some stock losses can occur due to wastage (broken,<br>
expired) and thefts by customers or employees. Therefore, based on experience, the<br>
head office usually provides a certain per cent of the closing stock as an allowance<br>
for such deficiencies. Through physical verification of stocks, the head office will<br>
be able to establish whether the deficiency in the branch is within the tolerance rate/<br>
provision.<br>
The company can also use columnar format, without adjustment account, to prepare<br>
the branch account to establish the amount of profit derived by the branch. With<br>
the use of a computerised accounting system, keeping branch accounts in columns<br>
would not be a problem. In such a format, one column on the debit side keeps the<br>
value of goods sent from head office and opening stock at the selling price (which<br>
is the invoice price) and the other column keeps their corresponding values at cost.<br>
The column on the debit side which keeps records of the selling price is just for<br>
control purposes and it is not part of the double-entry system. Meanwhile, on the<br>
credit side, both columns are recorded in the selling price (invoice price).<br>
<b>Example 10.2</b><br>

The following are the basic steps used to establishing whether there is deficiency<br>
within the tolerance rate:<br>
1.	 Determine the allowed deficiency = 4% x 37,000,000 = 1,480,000<br>
2.	 Identify the amount of the actual deficiency i.e., TZS 2,000,000<br>
3.	 Compare the two i.e., (1) and (2) above, you will see that, the actual deficiency<br>
is higher than the allowable deficiency by TZS 520,000. Thus, the company will<br>
initiate an investigation to establish the reasons.<br>
(b)	Stock and debtors method<br>
This method is suitable when branches are permitted to make credit sales. There are<br>
two ways the company can keep its records under stock and debtors method. The<br>
first is the integrated approach and the second is the columnar approach. Under the<br>
integrated approach, the head office is required to keep the following six accounts<br>
about branch operations:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Branch stock account,</b><br>
(ii)	 Branch debtors account,<br>
(iii)	 Branch expenses account,<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
296<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
(iv)	 Branch income statement (statement of profit or loss and other comprehensive<br>
income),<br>
(v)	 Goods sent to branch account and<br>
(vi)	 Branch adjustment account.<br>
A branch will be required to maintain branch assets account and branch cash<br>
account in addition to those kept at the head office, especially when the branch has<br>
permission to buy stock and incur expenses. However, when the columnar approach<br>
is adopted, there is no need of preparing a branch adjustment account.<br>

office. At the end of the year, the value of the closing stock (at cost), outstanding<br>

(c)	 Income statement (final accounts) method<br>
In this method, separate income statements are prepared for each branch to reflect<br>
their trading activities and other operations. In preparation of the branch income<br>
statements, the cost of the goods transferred to the branch at selling price is<br>
converted into cost price. The income statement does not make part of the double-<br>
entry system, it simply shows the financial performance of a branch.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
300<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Goods sent to branch at cost price</b><br>
Under this approach, the goods sent to the branch are charged at the cost. The<br>
approach can be used either under the debtors’ method, stock and debtor’s method,<br>
or income statement (final accounts) method.<br>
(a)	Debtors method<br>
Under debtor’s method only one account namely branch account is used to record<br>
business transactions between the head office and the branch. When goods are<br>
transferred at cost, the debit side of the branch account is recorded with the opening<br>
balance of the stock, debtors’ opening balance, and the petty cash. It is also debited<br>
with the cost of goods sent to the branch, expenses of the branch including those<br>
paid by the head office. On the other side, it is credited with the amount remitted by<br>
the branch and the cost of goods returned to the head office. At the end of the year,<br>
the value of the closing stock, outstanding debtors and any petty cash balance are<br>
credited to this account to ascertain the profit or loss made by the branch.<br>

(b)	Stock and debtors method<br>
When the company adopts this method, the head office is required to keep five<br>
accounts concerning branch operations. These are branch stock account, branch<br>
debtors account, branch expenses account, branch income statement (statement of<br>
profit or loss) and other comprehensive income), and goods sent to branch account.<br>
Moreover, a branch that has permission to buy stock and incur expenses, will be<br>
required to maintain branch assets account and branch cash account in addition to<br>
those kept at the head office.<br>
(c)	Income statement (final accounts) method<br>
In this method, separate income statements are prepared for each branch to reflect<br>
their trading activities and other operations. The statement does not make part of the<br>
double-entry system; it simply shows the financial performance of a branch.<br>

<b>Goods sent to branch at cost plus</b><br>
This approach is also known as wholesale pricing, whereby the head office transfers<br>
goods to branches at cost plus wholesale profit. As a result, branches are supposed<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
303<br>
to realise profit equal to the difference between retail price and the wholesale price.<br>
Meanwhile, the goods sent account will realise the amount of profit on goods<br>
sent to branches equal to the profit margin added to arrive at the whole sale price.<br>
Meanwhile, the branch stock account or income statement (in the trading aspect)<br>
is debited by the value of opening stock and the value of goods sent to the branch.<br>
Correspondingly, the account is credited with branch sales at retailing price and<br>
closing stock at the wholesale price.<br>
<b>Branch Profit Margin = Sales – cost of goods sold</b><br>
= Sales – (opening stock + purchases – closing stock)<br>
= Sales – opening stock – purchases + closing stock<br>
= (Sales + closing stock) – (opening stock + purchases at wholesale price)<br>
<b>Note:</b><br>
Both opening stock and closing stock are at a wholesale price while purchases<br>
reflect goods sent from the head office to the branch.<br>
<b>The returns of goods by branches</b><br>
During business operations, especially under non-autonomous branches, there are<br>
two possible pathways for returning goods. The first is when the branch returns some<br>
of the goods to the head office and the second is when goods sold to customers are<br>
returned either to the branch or straight to the head office. The accounting treatment<br>
of the goods returned by the branch to the head office is to debit goods sent account<br>
and to credit branch stock account.<br>
Meanwhile, for the return of the goods by customers through a branch, the<br>
accounting treatment will involve debiting the branch stock accounts and crediting<br>
branch debtors. If customers return goods straight to the head office, its accounting<br>
treatment is the same as if the goods are returned by the branch, but through branch<br>
debtors. Therefore, the goods sent account is debited and the branch debtors account<br>
is credited. However, branches will also need to update their records regarding<br>
branch debtors.<br>

<b>Autonomous branches/independent branches</b><br>
Autonomous branches are those branches that are big and the nature of their<br>
operations is complex to the extent of requiring them to manage their operations.<br>
Obviously, when branch becomes big it becomes difficult to manage them from the<br>
head office. In this case, they should prepare and maintain their books of accounts.<br>
However, for branches to prepare their financial reports, they must have sufficient<br>
resources to allow them to employ their own accounting staff. An example of a<br>
company with independent branches is the Tanzania Breweries Co. Ltd whose head<br>
office is in Dar Es Salaam and its branches are in Arusha, Mwanza, and Mbeya.<br>
<b>Recording of business transactions in autonomous branches</b><br>
The transactions of each branch are recorded in their books of accounts and at<br>
the end of the accounting period, each branch prepare its trial balance. The head<br>
office maintains only one account known as the current account for recording<br>
all transactions associated with the branches. Similarly, each branch maintains<br>
a current account for recording transactions associated with the head office. The<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
305<br>
current account of the head office considers the branches as debtors while the<br>
current account of the branches considers the head office as a creditor. It means<br>
every transaction between the head office and the branch is recorded in both current<br>
accounts; in the head office current account and branch current account. However,<br>
for transactions that are just concerned with the branches only, they will appear in<br>
branch books of accounts and are not recorded in the head office.<br>

<b>End of the year financial reports under autonomous branches</b><br>
At the end of the financial year, branches do close books of accounts and send<br>
their copies to the head office where reconciliation between the branch’s current<br>
account and the head office’s current account is conducted. Thereafter, necessary<br>
journal entries are passed to incorporate the trial balance of the branch in the head<br>
office books of accounts. Once branches have prepared their income statement, the<br>
net profits are transferred to the head office by crediting the head office’s current<br>
account. Then, the head office will recognise the branch’s net profit in their income<br>
statement by debiting the branch’s current account and corresponding entry to the<br>
income statement.<br>
When preparing final accounts, the head office values should be at cost price and<br>
the branch final accounts should be at mark-up prices. However, the whole business<br>
final accounts should be on cost price basis. After the preparation of the income<br>
statement, the head office prepares the combined statement of financial position<br>
to establish the total assets and liabilities of the whole company. The head office<br>
incorporates in its trial balance the assets and liabilities of the branches as shown<br>
in the branches’ trial balances. In the preparation of the combined statement of<br>
financial position, the balances of the current accounts are ignored because they<br>
are like contra-entries; the current account of the head office shows a debit balance<br>
of the same figure shown on the credit side of the current account of the branch,<br>
resulting to entries cancelling each other.<br>
Referring to example 10.5, the final accounts of the head office and the branch<br>
assuming the income statement of the branch for the first month of its operations<br>
generated a profit of TZS 6,000,000 would be as follows:<br>

<b>Activity 10.3</b><br>
XYZ Ltd is a food processing company, which initially specialised in the agricultural<br>
sector before expanding its operations into the livestock and fishery sectors. In<br>
agricultural food processing, the company has three cooking oil processing plants<br>
located in Dar es salaam, Singida and Kigoma and three branches processing<br>
wheat flour located in Arusha, Dar es salaam, and Mwanza. In the livestock sector,<br>
the company has a big plant in Shinyanga processing meats for local market and<br>
export, and in the fishery sector, the company has two branches, one in Mtwara<br>
and the other in Mwanza.<br>
Initially, the company had only one accounting clerk in each of the branches just<br>
for capturing bookkeeping records which are then submitted to the headquarters<br>
for financial report preparations. However, following its expansion the company<br>
has been struggling in recent years with record keeping challenges because of the<br>
volume of transactions undertaken by branches and the need for the branches to<br>
make strategic decisions to suit their sectors and local operating environment.<br>
<b>Required</b><br>
(a)<br>
Identify whether the existing structure of the company reflects non-<br>
autonomous or autonomous branches.<br>
(b)<br>
Identify and explain the best structure (between autonomous and non-<br>
autonomous) by which the company should re-arrange its branches to<br>
resolve the problem it experiences in recent years. Your explanations should<br>
consider the sectors it operates.<br>
<b>Items in transit</b><br>
Items in transit reflect those transactions which have been recorded in books of<br>
accounts of one part (either at the branch or head office) but not in the other part<br>
because the items or transaction is not complete. It also refers to transactions that<br>
are supposed to be recorded in the current accounts of both, the branch, and the head<br>
office, but due to a mismatch in the time of recording, they are seen in the records<br>
of one part only. For example, when the head office sends goods to the branch, it<br>
records them straight away in a current account, but the branch will not record them<br>
until they are received. Similarly, a branch may return goods to the head office or<br>
remit cash to the head office, but their arrival may delay. Hence, they will not be<br>
captured in the books of accounts of the head office promptly. Therefore, when<br>
these kinds of transactions are made a few days before the closure of the financial<br>
year, it is very possible to find some records in the current account of one part<br>
missing in the other part.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
310<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
In preparing the final accounts, items in transit require adjustments. Otherwise,<br>
the books of accounts will not balance because of the differences in the current<br>
account’s balances. All adjustments are made in the books of accounts of the head<br>
office so that the balance of its current account matches that of the branch. Then, the<br>
adjusted balance will be used in the preparation of the final accounts as well as in<br>
carrying forward to the next accounting period.<br>
The items in transit are normally assets in nature. For instance, the goods in transit<br>
to the branch, the goods returned by a branch to the head office, and cash remittance<br>
in transit are all assets to the head office. Thus, their adjustment will involve adding<br>
(debiting) them to the balances of the head office. Remember, asset balances always<br>
increase through debiting.<br>
