In this chapter, we have discussed various accounting concepts, methods, and<br>
techniques associated with the recognition of branch transactions in relations to<br>
the head office. A branch is simply a sub-unit of the company operating in different<br>
geographical locations as an agency of the head office. Generally, branches can be<br>
categorised geographically which gives inland and foreign branches, and according<br>
to control which gives out dependent/non-autonomous branches and independent/<br>
autonomous branches. The chapter focused on the later categorisation. For non-<br>
autonomous branches, the head office wholly controls operations of the branches<br>
while autonomous have autonomy in their operations. Consequently, non-<br>
autonomous branches do not prepare their books of accounts while autonomous<br>
branches have the autonomy to prepare their books of accounts.<br>
In keeping transaction records of the branches with the head office, various<br>
accounts are prepared and maintained. These accounts are meant to help the head<br>
office to control the operations of the branch and to evaluate their performance.<br>
Some of the most common accounts for non-autonomous include branch stock<br>
account, branch debtors account, branch expenses account, goods sent to branch<br>
account, and an adjustment account. However, we have seen that the nature of<br>
information recorded in these accounts depends on the pricing approach used by<br>
the head office sending goods to the branch. The head office can opt to transfer<br>
<b>Chapter summary</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
315<br>
goods to the branch either at cost or at the selling price or the wholesale price.<br>
Meanwhile, for the autonomous branch which has the autonomy to keep full records<br>
of accounting, the main account which shows interrelated transactions between the<br>
head office and the branch is the current account. When branches are autonomous,<br>
their treatment in the current account of the head office is like debtors. In contrast,<br>
the head office is considered a creditor in the current account of the branch.<br>
In establishing the performance of the company, the head office incorporates the<br>
performance of the branches in the financial statements of the head office. In<br>
the case of autonomous branches, which prepare their books of accounts, their<br>
trial balances are first incorporated in the trial balance of the head office then the<br>
final reports are prepared. However, the head office records should be adjusted to<br>
incorporate items in transit because these cause differences in the balance of the<br>
current account of a branch and that of the head office. Items in transit include<br>
goods in transit to the branch, goods returned by the branch to the head office,<br>
and remittances from branches to the head office, but they have not reached the<br>
designated destination by the time the books of accounts are prepared.<br>

the stock and debtors method.<br>

accounting, and how they are treated in books of accounts.<br>

<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Accountancy is a profession composed</b><br>
of a systematic field of knowledge about<br>
accounting, including the rules and<br>
principles that govern actual accounting<br>
procedures.<br>
<b>Accountant</b><br>
is<br>
a<br>
person<br>
who<br>
professionally deals with accounting<br>
or accountancy. Their job span beyond<br>
those performed by bookkeepers (i.e.<br>
recording, classifying, and summarizing<br>
financial records) to include, preparation,<br>
analysis, and interpretation of financial<br>
statements. Is a highly trained personnel<br>
and capable of designing accounting<br>
systems for different organizations.<br>
<b>Accounting cycle also known as the</b><br>
accounting process reflects a series<br>
of steps that are regularly repeated in<br>
the same order from identifying and<br>
recording business transactions to the<br>
time of preparing financial statements<br>
<b>Accounting is a subset of accountancy</b><br>
involving the practical application of<br>
accountancy principles to execute the<br>
profession’s core duties. It explains the<br>
nature of the work of the accountants.<br>
<b>Accounting is composed of different</b><br>
activities, including identifying and<br>
classifying<br>
business<br>
transactions,<br>
recording<br>
in<br>
books<br>
of<br>
accounts,<br>
preparing financial statements, and<br>
analyzing and interpreting the obtained<br>
results.<br>
<b>Activity ratios are a group of ratios</b><br>
that attempt to measure how well the<br>
business utilizes its assets to generate<br>
sales. Some activity ratios are useful<br>
in managing liabilities and others show<br>
the number of times particular assets<br>
convert into cash or sales. These ratios<br>
are also known as efficiency ratios.<br>
<b>Examples of activity ratios; are stock</b><br>
turnover, debtors turnover, and creditors<br>
turnover ratios.<br>
<b>Aging schedule of doubtful debt refers</b><br>
to a periodic report that categorizes<br>
business accounts receivable according<br>
to the length of time an invoice has<br>
been outstanding. It is used as a gauge<br>
to determine the financial health and<br>
reliability of the business customers. It<br>
is used as an indicator of risk regarding<br>
the collectibility of debts from customers<br>
hence useful in determining the amount<br>
of provision for doubtful debts and<br>
eventually, bad debts.<br>
<b>Amortization is a term that resembles</b><br>
depreciation as a technique used in<br>
spreading out the costs associated with<br>
the use of assets but in the category<br>
of intangible assets. Examples of<br>
intangible assets include goodwill,<br>
patents and trademarks, and copyrights,<br>
over the expected period.<br>
<b>Arithmetical errors are accounting</b><br>
errors<br>
arising<br>
due<br>
to<br>
transaction<br>
discrepancies between amounts in<br>
<b>Glossary</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
321<br>
words and amounts in figures. Also,<br>
they can arise due to wrong addition to<br>
the content of an invoice.<br>
<b>Auditor is a professional who has been</b><br>
authorized to conduct audit work. This<br>
involves reviewing and examining<br>
financial<br>
statements<br>
to<br>
determine<br>
whether they reflect a true and fair<br>
view of the business performance and<br>
its financial position (i.e. operational<br>
results and financial position).<br>
<b>Autonomous branches are also known</b><br>
as independent branches to reflect<br>
their design and functional behaviour.<br>
<b>Compared</b><br>
to<br>
non–autonomous<br>
branches, they are more independent at<br>
least within the broad policies framed by<br>
the head office. For example, they may<br>
be allowed to purchase goods from the<br>
local markets and maintain their own set<br>
of books. An autonomous branch can<br>
either be a home or a foreign branch.<br>
<b>Bad</b><br>
debts<br>
refer<br>
to<br>
debts/loans/<br>
outstanding balances owed that are no<br>
longer deemed recoverable and must<br>
be written off. Bad debts are treated as<br>
part and parcel of operating expenses or<br>
losses for the business.<br>
<b>Bookkeeping is part of the accounting</b><br>
process concerned with the classification<br>
and systematically recording of business<br>
transactions into different books of<br>
accounts.<br>
<b>Books of prime entry are used like</b><br>
diaries of business activities where the<br>
transactions made by a business are<br>
recorded for the first time before posting<br>
into separate ledger accounts. Other<br>
names for books of prime entry include<br>
books of original entries, journals, and<br>
day books.<br>
<b>Branch accounting is the system of</b><br>
bookkeeping under which the company<br>
maintains separate accounts for each of<br>
its branches to increase transparency as<br>
well as knowledge of the company’s<br>
cash flow position and financial position.<br>
<b>Business transaction refers to an</b><br>
economic event, which involves the<br>
exchange of goods or services from one<br>
person to another with money or money<br>
equivalent.<br>
<b>Cash discount refers to an incentive</b><br>
that a seller offers to a buyer in return<br>
for paying a bill before the scheduled<br>
due date. The seller will usually reduce<br>
the amount that the buyer owes by<br>
either a small percentage or an agreed<br>
monetary figure.<br>
<b>Clerical errors in accounting arise</b><br>
from wrong transaction posting due to<br>
copying or writing mistakes. Clerical<br>
errors are those errors that are generally<br>
committed by the clerical staff in<br>
recording transactions either in the<br>
books of prime entry or in the ledger<br>
accounts (books of final entry).<br>
<b>Cost accounting is the branch of</b><br>
accounting concerned with the recording<br>
and analysis of costs as well as the<br>
determination of the cost of products.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
322<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Cumulative Dividend (Cum div) is</b><br>
an investment term when a buyer of a<br>
security is entitled to receive the next<br>
dividend scheduled for distribution.<br>
<b>Therefore, the price of the security will</b><br>
be set relatively higher since the value<br>
of the pending dividend is included in<br>
the sale price of a security.<br>
<b>Debentures a medium to long-term</b><br>
security yielding a fixed rate of interest,<br>
issued by a company to raise capital<br>
from the public.<br>
<b>Depletion is an accrual accounting</b><br>
technique used to allocate the cost<br>
of extracting natural resources such<br>
as timber, minerals, and oil from<br>
the earth. Like depreciation and<br>
amortization, depletion is a non-cash<br>
expense that lowers the cost value of an<br>
asset incrementally through scheduled<br>
charges to income.<br>
<b>Depreciation refers to an accounting</b><br>
method that is used to allocate the cost<br>
of a tangible or physical asset (non–<br>
current assets) over its useful life. Thus,<br>
depreciation expenses will be posted to<br>
the income statement similar to other<br>
operating expenses while accumulated<br>
depreciation will be deducted from the<br>
cost of an asset to show its book value<br>
in the statement of financial position.<br>
<b>Disposal of assets is the elimination</b><br>
of an asset from a company’s records,<br>
typically by selling or scrapping<br>
it. These are often none – current<br>
assets that contributed to generating<br>
profits e.g., machinery, technology, or<br>
company vehicles.<br>
<b>Dividends are the sum of money paid</b><br>
regularly, typically annually, by a<br>
company to its shareholders out of its<br>
profits or reserves.<br>
<b>Double-entry</b><br>
bookkeeping<br>
is<br>
a<br>
fundamental<br>
principle<br>
underlying<br>
present-day<br>
bookkeeping<br>
and<br>
accounting, which states that every<br>
financial transaction has equal and<br>
opposite effects in at least two different<br>
accounts. It requires that, for every debit<br>
entry, there must be a corresponding<br>
credit entry and for every credit entry,<br>
there must be a corresponding debit<br>
entry. It is the foundation of accounting<br>
that satisfies the accounting equation,<br>
assets = liabilities + equity.<br>
<b>Doubtful debt is a debt that is unlikely</b><br>
to be paid i.e., a debt, with which there<br>
is uncertainty, as to the degree to which<br>
the amount will be recovered from the<br>
debtor. Thus, provision for doubtful<br>
debts is calculated based on debtors’<br>
balances after the elimination of bad<br>
debts.<br>
<b>Equity is the net amount of funds</b><br>
invested in a business by its owners,<br>
plus any retained earnings. It is also<br>
calculated as the difference between<br>
the total of all recorded assets and<br>
liabilities on an entity’s balance sheet.<br>
<b>Excluding dividend (Ex div) is an</b><br>
investment term that describes a stock<br>
that is trading without the value of the<br>
next dividend payment.<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
323<br>
<b>Financial accounting is a specific</b><br>
branch of accounting involving a<br>
process of recording financial records,<br>
summarizing,<br>
and<br>
preparation<br>
of<br>
financial statements i.e., statements<br>
of income, statements of financial<br>
position, and statement of cash flows<br>
over a specified period.<br>
<b>Financial leverage ratios are ratios</b><br>
designed to assess the ability of a<br>
company to pay its long-term liabilities<br>
i.e., debt and the interest on that debt.<br>
<b>The focus is on the capital structure of</b><br>
the business measured by using ratios<br>
such as debt ratio, equity ratio, and<br>
interest cover ratios.<br>
<b>Financing activities refer to any cash</b><br>
flows that result in changes in the size<br>
and composition of the contributed<br>
equity capital or borrowings of the<br>
entity (i.e., share capital, dividends)<br>
<b>Folio number is a way to reference</b><br>
a bookkeeping entry, most often<br>
numbered<br>
in<br>
chronological<br>
or<br>
sequential order. For example, entries<br>
in the sales day book will have<br>
reference numbers to indicate in which<br>
account or page number in the sales<br>
ledger a record has been posted.<br>
<b>Forensic accounting is a branch</b><br>
of accounting that deals with the<br>
application of accounting, auditing,<br>
and investigative skills in analysing<br>
financial<br>
information,<br>
which<br>
can<br>
appropriately be used as evidence in<br>
the Court of law.<br>
<b>General journal is the company’s</b><br>
journal in which initial record keeping<br>
of all the transactions is done which<br>
are not recorded in any of the specialty<br>
journals maintained by the company<br>
like purchase journal, sales journal,<br>
and cash journal.<br>
<b>General ledger is the main ledger</b><br>
book of the business, which contains<br>
all the accounts of the business<br>
recorded using the principle of double-<br>
entry. General ledger contains different<br>
accounts such as assets, liabilities,<br>
capital/equity, revenue, and expenses.<br>
<b>In short, the general ledger provides all</b><br>
the accounts used in the preparation of<br>
financial statements.<br>
<b>International Accounting Standards</b><br>
(IAS) refer to internationally-agreed<br>
principles and procedures guiding the<br>
preparation of financial statements by<br>
the company. How does this concept<br>
relate<br>
to<br>
international<br>
financial<br>
reporting standards (IFRS)? Since<br>
2001, those standards have been<br>
released under the new name IFRS. In<br>
this process, several IAS were replaced<br>
with IFRS but some were maintained<br>
until to date they are relevantly used in<br>
tandem with IFRS.<br>
<b>International</b><br>
financial<br>
reporting<br>
standards (IFRS) refer to a set<br>
of accounting rules governing the<br>
preparation and reporting of financial<br>
statements<br>
of<br>
public<br>
companies<br>
with the objectives of making them<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
324<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
consistent, transparent, and easily<br>
comparable around the world.<br>
<b>Investment is an asset or item acquired</b><br>
to generate income or appreciation. The<br>
latter refers to an increase in the value<br>
of an asset over time. The asset that has<br>
been acquired as an investment will be<br>
maintained to use in the future to create<br>
wealth rather than use or consume it.<br>
<b>Liquidity ratios are ratios assessing</b><br>
how easily a particular category of<br>
assets can be converted into cash. The<br>
focus is on current assets e.g., debtors<br>
and stocks/inventory – these can be<br>
converted into cash within a relatively<br>
shorter period – not exceeding one year.<br>
<b>Thus seen as appropriate in settling the</b><br>
current liabilities like creditors, which<br>
need to be paid within one accounting<br>
period. They are computed by dividing<br>
current assets by current liabilities.<br>
<b>Management accounting is a branch</b><br>
of accounting designed to assist<br>
managers in carrying out their basic<br>
functions of planning, control, and<br>
decision-making. It utilizes different<br>
methods and techniques from cost<br>
accounting and other fields of business,<br>
economics, and statistics to process<br>
information to support management<br>
functions.<br>
<b>Market prospects ratios also known</b><br>
as investors ratios are designed to<br>
assist<br>
shareholders<br>
and<br>
potential<br>
investors to evaluate the performance<br>
of the business in terms of the value<br>
of its shares and expected returns. The<br>
computations of these ratios require<br>
the use of some financial data from the<br>
stock exchange market.<br>
<b>Operating activities are the principal</b><br>
revenue-generating activities of a<br>
business and other activities resulting<br>
in a change of current assets and<br>
current liabilities provided they do not<br>
fall under the categories of financing<br>
and investing activities<br>
<b>Profitability ratios are financial ratios</b><br>
designed to measure the ability of a<br>
business to generate profit i.e., revenue<br>
over expenses. A good example of<br>
profitability ratios includes profit<br>
margin which relates profit to the<br>
amount of revenue.<br>
<b>Provisions refer to any funds set aside</b><br>
from a company’s profit for probable<br>
future expenses or a reduction in the<br>
asset value, although the exact amount<br>
is unknown at present e.g., provision<br>
for doubtful debts and allowances for<br>
discounts.<br>
<b>Purchases day book is a book of</b><br>
prime entries that are used to record<br>
credit purchases of goods.<br>
<b>Purchases ledger is a book that</b><br>
contains all the accounts of individual<br>
credit suppliers (the personal accounts)<br>
used to record credit transactions<br>
between business entities and their<br>
suppliers of goods and services. The<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
325<br>
purchases ledger thus provides the<br>
detailed transactions of each credit<br>
supplier of goods.<br>
<b>Reporting entity may also be referred</b><br>
to as an accounting entity, meaning a<br>
business entity in respect of which the<br>
financial statements are prepared. The<br>
<b>IFRS states that reporting entity may</b><br>
have a legal obligation or may choose<br>
to prepare external financial reports for<br>
the benefit of parties with an interest in<br>
its operations. These include suppliers,<br>
lenders, and investors.<br>
<b>Reserves are part of profits or gains</b><br>
that have been set aside to achieve<br>
specific objectives. These may include<br>
paying for bonuses, expected legal<br>
settlement, repairs and maintenance or<br>
long-term debts, buying non-current<br>
assets, or developing and expanding a<br>
business.<br>
<b>Retained earnings are the amount of</b><br>
profit remaining after the company<br>
has paid all of the costs of goods sold,<br>
operating expenses, income taxes,<br>
and dividends to shareholders. This<br>
represents the portion of the company’s<br>
equity that can be used, for instance, to<br>
invest in new equipment, research and<br>
development, and marketing.<br>
<b>Revenue recognition principle asserts</b><br>
that revenue must be recognized as it<br>
is earned. This means after a certain<br>
critical event has occurred such as<br>
the product being delivered to the<br>
customer.<br>
<b>Sales day book is a book of prime</b><br>
entries that is used to record sales of<br>
goods made on credit.<br>
<b>Sales ledger is a book that is used to</b><br>
record all the accounts of individual<br>
credit customers, which are personal<br>
accounts. In other words, the sales<br>
ledger is used to record all transactions<br>
made between credit customers for<br>
goods or services and the business<br>
entity.<br>
<b>Share capital refers to the amount of</b><br>
money the owners of a company have<br>
invested in the business as represented<br>
by common and/or preferred shares.<br>
<b>Social responsibility accounting is</b><br>
the process of communicating the<br>
social and environmental effects of<br>
an organization’s economic actions to<br>
particular interest groups within the<br>
society and the government at large.<br>
<b>Source document is an original</b><br>
document evidencing the occurrence<br>
of a transaction e.g., sales invoice or<br>
receipt, title deed, purchase order,<br>
bank statement, and canceled cheque.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
source<br>
document<br>
contains<br>
important details that substantiate the<br>
financial transactions that are entered<br>
into the internal accounting system of<br>
a business.<br>
<b>Statement of cash flows is one of the</b><br>
key financial statements that a business,<br>
especially a company, is required to<br>
produce to show an overview of all<br>
cash flows. These include, operating<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE  USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five</b><br>
326<br>
<b>Accountancy for Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
activities, financing activities, and<br>
investing activities. Through this, one<br>
can be able to know the actual cash<br>
position of the business.<br>
<b>Statement of changes in owners’</b><br>
equity is a financial statement that<br>
reports changes in equity from net<br>
income,<br>
owner<br>
investment,<br>
and<br>
withdrawals over a period. For a<br>
company, the changes will be observed<br>
in<br>
the<br>
company’s<br>
share<br>
capital,<br>
accumulated reserves, and retained<br>
earnings over the reporting period.<br>
<b>Suspense account is a general ledger</b><br>
account used for recording business<br>
transactions temporarily for different<br>
reasons<br>
including,<br>
correction<br>
of<br>
bookkeeping errors or simply being<br>
unsure of the type of account most<br>
appropriate to record those transactions.<br>
<b>Tax accounting is the branch of</b><br>
accounting<br>
concerned<br>
with<br>
tax-<br>
related issues such as determining<br>
the tax liability of the business,<br>
and preparation of tax returns for<br>
individuals, companies, and other<br>
types of organizations.<br>
<b>Trade discount is the reduction in</b><br>
price<br>
a<br>
manufacturer/wholesaler<br>
gives a wholesaler/retailer when they<br>
buy a product or group of products.<br>
<b>A trade discount is offered as a</b><br>
certain percentage that the offerer<br>
(manufacturer or wholesaler) is willing<br>
to reduce from its list price to benefit<br>
the wholesalers or retailers.<br>
